text
### Gastroesophageal Reflux (GER) and Disease (GERD)

1. What is the definition of <b style="color:red">Gastroesophageal Reflux (GER)</b>? | Physiologic retrograde movement. <br>Movement of gastric contents across the LES into the esophagus.
2. What is the definition of <b style="color:red">Gastroesophageal Reflux Disease (GERD)</b>? | Pathologic reflux. <br>Associated with troublesome symptoms or complications.
3. Contrast the complications of <b style="color:red">GER vs GERD</b>. | GER: None; physiologic. <br>GERD: Esophagitis, stricture, Barrett's, failure to thrive.
4. Contrast the effort of passage in <b style="color:red">GER vs GERD</b>. | GER: Effortless (spitting up). <br>GERD: Forceful passage or distress.
5. What is the most common esophageal disorder in children? | <b style="color:red">GERD</b>.
6. Which dietary factors are linked to the increasing incidence of <b style="color:red">GERD</b>? | Western diets. <br>High fatty food intake.
7. What is the major mechanism of pathologic reflux in <b style="color:red">GERD</b>? | <b style="color:red">Transient LES relaxation (TLESR)</b>.
8. Is <b style="color:red">Transient lower esophageal sphincter relaxation (TLESR)</b> related to swallowing? | No.
9. What factor increases the frequency of <b style="color:red">Transient lower esophageal sphincter relaxation (TLESR)</b>? | Gastric distention.
10. Name the components of the <b style="color:red">defective anti-reflux barrier</b> in GERD. (3) | 1) LES <br>2) Crural diaphragm <br>3) Hiatal hernia.
11. How does <b style="color:red">diminished esophageal clearance</b> contribute to GERD? | Prolongs acid contact. <br>Affected by poor peristalsis or supine position.
12. How does <b style="color:red">xerostomia</b> affect GERD? | Lack of saliva <br>Decreases esophageal clearance.
13. Name three external triggers that exacerbate <b style="color:red">GERD</b>. | 1) High-fat diets <br>2) Smoking <br>3) Medications.
14. How does <b style="color:red">nicotine</b> from smoking affect the esophagus? | Relaxes the LES.
15. Which specific medication classes can exacerbate <b style="color:red">GERD</b>? (2) | ACE inhibitors; <br>Calcium Channel Blockers (CCBs).
16. At what age does <b style="color:red">infantile reflux</b> peak? | <b style="color:red">4 months of age</b>.
17. By what age does <b style="color:red">GER</b> resolve in 88% of cases? | <b style="color:red">12 months</b>.
18. What is the term for infants who are active and feeding well despite <b style="color:red">GER</b>? | <b style="color:red">"Happy spitters"</b>.
19. What are the clinical signs of <b style="color:red">Sandifer Syndrome</b>? (2) | <b style="color:red">Neck contortions and arching</b>.
20. What does the arching in <b style="color:red">Sandifer Syndrome</b> resemble? | Opisthotonus.
21. What behavioral symptom is often associated with <b style="color:red">Sandifer Syndrome</b>? | Food refusal.
22. Name the manifestations of <b style="color:red">Infantile GERD</b>. (5) | Irritability, arching, choking, <br>feeding aversion, failure to thrive.
23. What are the typical symptoms of <b style="color:red">GERD in older children</b>? (3) | <b style="color:red">Heartburn, chest pain</b>, abdominal pain.
24. Which respiratory conditions may be associated with <b style="color:red">GERD in older children</b>? | Asthma or chronic cough.
25. How is <b style="color:red">GERD</b> primarily diagnosed? | <b style="color:red">Clinical diagnosis</b>.
26. In which patients can an <b style="color:red">empirical trial of PPI therapy</b> confirm GERD? | <b style="color:red">Older children and adults only</b>.
27. What is the duration of an empirical PPI trial for <b style="color:red">GERD</b>? | 2-4 weeks.
28. What is the role of a <b style="color:red">barium swallow</b> in vomiting? | Identify structural abnormalities.
29. What is the classic barium swallow finding for <b style="color:red">achalasia</b>? | <b style="color:red">"Bird’s beak"</b> appearance.
30. Is a <b style="color:red">barium swallow</b> used to diagnose GERD? | No.
31. List nonpharmacologic strategies for <b style="color:red">GERD</b> management. (4) | Avoid overfeeding, thicken feeds, <br>hydrolyzed formula, upright position.
32. Which acid suppressants are recommended for long-term <b style="color:red">GERD</b> treatment? | <b style="color:red">Proton Pump Inhibitors (PPIs)</b>.
33. Are <b style="color:red">H2RAs</b> recommended for chronic GERD use? | No.
34. What is the status of <b style="color:red">prokinetics</b> (e.g., domperidone) in GERD management? | Generally discouraged.

### Gastrointestinal Infections and Acute Gastroenteritis

35. Describe the mechanism of <b style="color:red">Noninflammatory diarrhea</b>. | Enterotoxin production. <br>Or villus destruction.
36. Are there WBCs or RBCs in the stool of <b style="color:red">Noninflammatory diarrhea</b>? | No.
37. Name three pathogens that cause <b style="color:red">Noninflammatory diarrhea</b>. | Rotavirus, Vibrio cholerae, ETEC.
38. Describe the mechanism of <b style="color:red">Inflammatory diarrhea</b>. | Direct mucosal invasion. <br>Or cytotoxin production.
39. What is the clinical stool appearance in <b style="color:red">Inflammatory diarrhea</b>? | Bloody stools.
40. Name three pathogens that cause <b style="color:red">Inflammatory diarrhea</b>. | Shigella, Salmonella, E. histolytica.
41. Describe the mechanism of <b style="color:red">Penetrating diarrhea</b>. | Invasion beyond epithelium. <br>Invasion beyond the intestinal epithelium.
42. Name three pathogens that cause <b style="color:red">Penetrating diarrhea</b>. | Nontyphoidal Salmonella, Yersinia, Campylobacter.
43. What is the global impact of <b style="color:red">diarrhea</b> in children under 5? | <b style="color:red">Top three cause of mortality</b>.
44. What etiologic group is the most common cause of <b style="color:red">Gastroenteritis</b>? | <b style="color:red">Viruses</b>.
45. What is the most common cause of <b style="color:red">severe diarrhea</b> in children? | <b style="color:red">Rotavirus</b>.
46. What is the primary route of transmission for <b style="color:red">GI infections</b>? | <b style="color:red">Fecal-oral transmission</b>.
47. What is the most common manifestation of <b style="color:red">acute gastroenteritis</b>? | <b style="color:red">Dehydration</b>.
48. What is the priority for clinical assessment in <b style="color:red">Gastroenteritis</b>? | Dehydration.
49. What extraintestinal complication involves knee pain 1-3 weeks after <b style="color:red">Salmonella or Shigella</b>? | <b style="color:red">Reactive Arthritis</b>.
50. What complication occurs weeks after a <b style="color:red">Campylobacter</b> infection? | <b style="color:red">Guillain-Barre Syndrome</b>.
51. What does <b style="color:red">Hemolytic Uremic Syndrome (HUS)</b> present with? | Sudden onset renal failure.
52. Which pathogens are associated with <b style="color:red">Hemolytic Uremic Syndrome (HUS)</b>? (2) | <b style="color:red">Shigella dysenteriae 1; E. coli O157:H7</b>.
53. What is the mainstay of treatment for <b style="color:red">acute gastroenteritis</b>? | Rehydration therapy.
54. What is used for rehydration in <b style="color:red">mild</b> cases of gastroenteritis? | <b style="color:red">Oral Rehydration Salts (ORS)</b>.
55. What is the treatment for <b style="color:red">severe shock</b> in gastroenteritis? | IV fluids.
56. Why is <b style="color:red">Zinc</b> recommended in diarrhea management? | Reduce duration/severity.
57. List three strategies for <b style="color:red">prevention</b> of GI infections. | 1) Rotavirus vaccination <br>2) Breastfeeding (up to 2 yrs) <br>3) Handwashing.

### Functional Constipation and Encopresis

58. How many Rome IV criteria must be met for <b style="color:red">Functional Constipation</b>? | 2 or more.
59. What is the frequency criterion for <b style="color:red">Functional Constipation</b>? | ≤2 defecations per week.
60. What is the <b style="color:red">incontinence</b> criterion for children ≥4 years? | At least 1 episode/week.
61. What is the <b style="color:red">posturing</b> criterion for functional constipation? | Retentive posturing. <br>History of volitional retention.
62. What is the <b style="color:red">mass</b> criterion for functional constipation? | Large fecal mass in rectum.
63. What is the <b style="color:red">diameter</b> criterion for functional constipation? | History of large diameter stools.
64. What is the definition of <b style="color:red">Encopresis</b>? | <b style="color:red">Involuntary loss</b> of stools.
65. What is the developmental age required for <b style="color:red">Encopresis</b> diagnosis? | <b style="color:red">4 years</b>.
66. At what age does <b style="color:red">functional constipation</b> often start? | <b style="color:red">6 months of age</b>.
67. What dietary change often triggers <b style="color:red">functional constipation</b>? | Introduction of complementary feeding.
68. What is suggested if constipation onset is <b style="color:red">&lt;6 months of age</b>? | Organic causes. <br>Ex: <b style="color:red">Hirschsprung Disease</b>.
69. Name four alarm signs for <b style="color:red">Hirschsprung Disease</b>. | 1) Onset month 1 <br>2) Meconium >48 hrs <br>3) Family history <br>4) Ribbon stools.
70. What is the meconium passage time alarm sign for <b style="color:red">Hirschsprung Disease</b>? | <b style="color:red">>48 hours</b>.
71. What do <b style="color:red">ribbon stools</b> suggest? | Hirschsprung Disease.
72. Name three neurologic alarm signs for <b style="color:red">constipation</b>. | 1) Tuft of hair on spine <br>2) Sacral dimple <br>3) Gluteal cleft deviation.
73. Where is a hard fecal mass usually palpated in <b style="color:red">functional constipation</b>? | <b style="color:red">Left lower quadrant</b> (LLQ).
74. What is the first step of <b style="color:red">Pharmacologic Management</b> for constipation? | Disimpaction.
75. Which disimpaction method is used for <b style="color:red">infants</b>? | <b style="color:red">Glycerin suppositories</b>.
76. Which disimpaction method is used for <b style="color:red">older children</b>? | <b style="color:red">Phosphate enemas</b>.
77. What oral agent can be used for <b style="color:red">"slow" disimpaction</b>? | Oral Polyethylene glycol (PEG).
78. What is the purpose of <b style="color:red">maintenance laxatives</b>? | Prevent recurrence.
79. Name two laxatives used for <b style="color:red">maintenance</b>. | <b style="color:red">Lactulose; Polyethylene glycol</b>.
80. Describe the proper <b style="color:red">toilet training position</b>. | Knees higher than hips. <br>Leaning forward; feet supported.

### Peptic Ulcer Disease (PUD)

81. What is the most common location for <b style="color:red">Primary PUD</b>? | <b style="color:red">Duodenum</b> (Duodenal bulb).
82. What is the primary cause of <b style="color:red">Primary PUD</b>? | <b style="color:red">Helicobacter pylori</b>.
83. What is the common location for <b style="color:red">Secondary PUD</b>? | Stomach (Gastric).
84. Name causes of <b style="color:red">Secondary PUD</b>. (4) | NSAIDs, Stress (sepsis), <br>burns, intracranial lesions.
85. What is a <b style="color:red">Cushing Ulcer</b> associated with? | Intracranial lesions.
86. What is a <b style="color:red">Curling Ulcer</b> associated with? | Severe burn injuries.
87. What causes <b style="color:red">PUD Pathogenesis</b>? | Imbalance of factors. <br>Acid/pepsin vs mucus/bicarbonate/prostaglandins.
88. What is the classic symptom of <b style="color:red">Peptic Ulcer Disease (PUD)</b>? | <b style="color:red">Epigastric pain</b>.
89. How is <b style="color:red">PUD pain</b> typically described? | Dull or aching.
90. What factor often alleviates <b style="color:red">PUD pain</b>? | <b style="color:red">Ingestion of food</b>.
91. Name symptoms of <b style="color:red">PUD in infants</b>. | Irritability, vomiting, <br>feeding difficulty, hematemesis.
92. What is the gold standard diagnosis for <b style="color:red">PUD</b>? | <b style="color:red">Upper Endoscopy (EGD)</b>.
93. What can be performed during an <b style="color:red">Upper Endoscopy (EGD)</b>? | Biopsy and H. pylori screening.
94. What is the PPI duration in <b style="color:red">H. pylori Triple Therapy</b>? | <b style="color:red">One month</b>.
95. What antibiotics are used in <b style="color:red">H. pylori Triple Therapy</b>? (2) | Amoxicillin; <br>Clarithromycin or Metronidazole.
96. How long is the course for antibiotics in <b style="color:red">Triple Therapy</b>? | 14 days.
97. List three surgical indications for <b style="color:red">PUD</b>. | 1) Uncontrollable bleeding <br>2) Perforation <br>3) Obstruction.

### Critical Comparisons and Differential Diagnoses

98. Compare <b style="color:red">GER vs GERD</b>. | GER: Normal ("happy spitter"). <br>GERD: Pathologic symptoms/complications.
99. Compare <b style="color:red">Vomiting vs Reflux</b>. | Vomiting: <b style="color:red">Forceful</b> passage. <br>Reflux: <b style="color:red">Effortless</b> passage.
100. Compare <b style="color:red">PUD vs GERD pain</b> location. | PUD: <b style="color:red">Epigastric</b>. <br>GERD: <b style="color:red">Retrosternal/heartburn</b>.
101. How is <b style="color:red">PUD vs GERD pain</b> affected by eating? | PUD: Relieved by food. <br>GERD: Worsens after eating.
102. Compare <b style="color:red">Cushing vs Curling Ulcer</b> triggers. | Cushing: <b style="color:red">CNS injury</b>. <br>Curling: <b style="color:red">Severe burns</b>.
103. Compare <b style="color:red">Non-inflammatory vs Inflammatory Diarrhea</b>. | Non-inflammatory: Watery/toxin-mediated. <br>Inflammatory: <b style="color:red">Bloody (dysentery)</b>.
104. Compare <b style="color:red">Functional Constipation vs Hirschsprung</b> onset. | Functional: <b style="color:red">6 months</b>. <br>Hirschsprung: <b style="color:red">At birth</b>.
105. Compare <b style="color:red">Functional Constipation vs Hirschsprung</b> rectal exam. | Functional: Stools in rectal vault. <br>Hirschsprung: Empty rectum.
106. Compare <b style="color:red">Encopresis vs Diarrhea</b>. | Encopresis: Leakage around <b style="color:red">fecal impaction</b>. <br>Diarrhea: Increased frequency/liquidity.
107. Compare <b style="color:red">PPI vs H2RA</b> in GERD. | PPI: <b style="color:red">Long-term management</b>. <br>H2RA: Not for chronic use.
108. What causes <b style="color:red">Projectile vomiting</b>? | <b style="color:red">Increased ICP</b> or <b style="color:red">Pyloric Stenosis</b>.
109. What causes <b style="color:red">Non-projectile vomiting</b>? | GERD or Gastroenteritis.
110. What does <b style="color:red">Bilious vomiting</b> (green) indicate? | Obstruction <b style="color:red">distal to ampulla of Vater</b>.
111. What does <b style="color:red">Bloody vomiting</b> (hematemesis) indicate? | Gastritis, ulcers, or esophagitis.
112. Compare <b style="color:red">Achalasia vs Pyloric Stenosis</b> imaging. | Achalasia: Barium swallow ("bird's beak"). <br>Pyloric Stenosis: <b style="color:red">Ultrasound</b> (thickened pylorus).
113. Compare <b style="color:red">Reactive Arthritis vs Sepsis</b> joint timing. | Reactive: <b style="color:red">Post-infectious</b> (1-3 weeks later). <br>Sepsis: Acute systemic infection.
114. Compare <b style="color:red">Lactulose vs Polyethylene Glycol (PEG)</b> use. | Both: Maintenance. <br>PEG: Maintenance AND rapid disimpaction.
115. Compare <b style="color:red">Sandifer Syndrome vs Seizures</b>. | Sandifer: related to <b style="color:red">feeding</b>. <br>Seizures: Not related to meals.


# LE 2 - 6 
## Summary
text
### Gastroesophageal Reflux (GER) and Disease (GERD)

#### Overview and Comparison
| Feature | Gastroesophageal Reflux (GER) | Gastroesophageal Reflux Disease (GERD) |
| :--- | :--- | :--- |
| **Definition** | Physiologic retrograde movement of gastric contents across the LES into the esophagus. | Pathologic reflux associated with troublesome symptoms or complications. |
| **Complications** | None; usually considered "physiologic." | Esophagitis, stricture, Barrett's esophagus, failure to thrive. |
| **Effort** | Effortless passage of contents (spitting up). | May involve forceful passage or associated distress. |

* **Epidemiology of GERD**: Gastroesophageal reflux disease is the <b style="color:red">most common esophageal disorder</b> in children, with an increasing incidence linked to Western diets and high fatty food intake.
* **GERD Pathophysiology**: <b style="color:red">Transient lower esophageal sphincter relaxation (TLESR)</b> is the major mechanism of pathologic reflux; it is not related to swallowing and increases with gastric distention.
* **GERD Barriers**: Reflux can be caused by a <b style="color:red">defective anti-reflux barrier</b>, which includes the lower esophageal sphincter (LES), the crural diaphragm, or the presence of a hiatal hernia.
* **GERD Clearance Factors**: Diminished esophageal clearance, affected by poor peristalsis, supine body position, or lack of saliva (xerostomia), contributes to the development of GERD.
* **GERD External Triggers**: High-fat diets, smoking (nicotine relaxes the LES), and medications like <b style="color:red">ACE inhibitors or Calcium Channel Blockers (CCBs)</b> can exacerbate reflux.
* **Natural History of GER**: Infantile reflux peaks at <b style="color:red">4 months of age</b> and resolves in 88% of cases by 12 months; infants are often called "happy spitters" if they are active and feeding well despite reflux.
* **Sandifer Syndrome**: A unique clinical manifestation of GERD in infants characterized by <b style="color:red">neck contortions and arching</b> (resembling opisthotonus) often associated with food refusal.
* **Infantile GERD Symptoms**: Manifestations include irritability, arching, choking, gagging, feeding aversion, and <b style="color:red">failure to thrive</b>.
* **Older Children GERD Symptoms**: Presentations typically include <b style="color:red">heartburn, chest pain</b>, abdominal pain, and sometimes difficult-to-treat asthma or chronic cough.
* **GERD Diagnosis**: GERD is primarily a <b style="color:red">clinical diagnosis</b> based on history and physical examination; an empirical trial of PPI therapy for 2-4 weeks can confirm the diagnosis in <b style="color:red">older children and adults only</b>.
* **Diagnostic Imaging in Vomiting**: Barium swallow can identify structural abnormalities such as achalasia (showing a <b style="color:red">"bird’s beak"</b> appearance) but is not used to diagnose GERD itself.
* **Nonpharmacologic Management of GERD**: Strategies include avoiding overfeeding, thickening feeds, using hydrolyzed protein formula if allergy is suspected, and maintaining an upright position after feeds.
* **Pharmacologic Management of GERD**: <b style="color:red">Proton Pump Inhibitors (PPIs)</b> like omeprazole are the only acid suppressants recommended for long-term treatment; H2RAs are not for chronic use, and prokinetics (e.g., domperidone) are generally discouraged.

### Gastrointestinal Infections and Acute Gastroenteritis

#### Pathogenesis of Diarrhea
| Type | Mechanism | Common Pathogens |
| :--- | :--- | :--- |
| **Noninflammatory** | Enterotoxin production or villus destruction; no WBC/RBC in stool. | Rotavirus, Vibrio cholerae, ETEC. |
| **Inflammatory** | Direct mucosal invasion or cytotoxin production; results in bloody stools. | Shigella, Salmonella, E. histolytica, EIEC. |
| **Penetrating** | Invasion beyond the intestinal epithelium. | Nontyphoidal Salmonella, Yersinia, Campylobacter. |

* **Global Impact**: Diarrhea is the <b style="color:red">top three cause of mortality</b> in children under 5 years worldwide, particularly in low- to middle-income countries.
* **Etiology of Gastroenteritis**: <b style="color:red">Viruses</b> are the most common etiologic group, with <b style="color:red">Rotavirus</b> being the most common cause of severe diarrhea in children.
* **Transmission**: The primary route for GI infections is <b style="color:red">fecal-oral transmission</b> via contaminated water, food, or person-to-person contact.
* **Clinical Manifestation**: <b style="color:red">Dehydration</b> is the most common manifestation of acute gastroenteritis and is the priority for clinical assessment.
* **Extraintestinal Complication - Reactive Arthritis**: Patients may develop knee pain and joint inflammation 1-3 weeks after an infection with <b style="color:red">Salmonella or Shigella</b>.
* **Extraintestinal Complication - Guillain-Barre Syndrome**: A neurological complication occurring a few weeks after a <b style="color:red">Campylobacter</b> infection.
* **Extraintestinal Complication - HUS**: <b style="color:red">Hemolytic Uremic Syndrome</b> presents with sudden onset renal failure following infection with <b style="color:red">Shigella dysenteriae 1 or E. coli O157:H7</b>.
* **Management - Rehydration**: Rehydration therapy is the mainstay of treatment, using <b style="color:red">Oral Rehydration Salts (ORS)</b> for mild cases and IV fluids for severe shock.
* **Management - Zinc Supplementation**: Zinc is recommended as a mainstay treatment to reduce the duration and severity of diarrhea episodes.
* **Prevention**: Strategies include <b style="color:red">Rotavirus vaccination</b>, breastfeeding for up to 2 years, handwashing, and improved sanitation.

### Functional Constipation and Encopresis

#### Rome IV Diagnostic Criteria (Must meet 2 or more for at least 1 month)
| Criteria | Child & Adolescent (≥4 years) | Neonate & Toddler (<4 years) |
| :--- | :--- | :--- |
| **Frequency** | ≤2 defecations per week. | ≤2 defecations per week. |
| **Incontinence** | At least 1 episode of fecal incontinence/week. | At least 1 episode/week after toilet training. |
| **Posturing** | History of retentive posturing/volitional retention. | History of excessive stool retention. |
| **Pain** | History of painful or hard bowel movements. | History of painful or hard bowel movements. |
| **Mass** | Presence of a large fecal mass in the rectum. | Presence of a large fecal mass in the rectum. |
| **Diameter** | History of large diameter stools. | History of large diameter stools. |

* **Definition of Encopresis**: The <b style="color:red">involuntary loss</b> of stools into underwear after a child has reached the developmental age of <b style="color:red">4 years</b>.
* **Timing of Constipation**: Constitutional or functional constipation often starts around <b style="color:red">6 months of age</b> with the introduction of complementary feeding; onset <6 months suggests organic causes like <b style="color:red">Hirschsprung Disease</b>.
* **Alarm Signs of Hirschsprung Disease**: 1) Onset in first month of life, 2) Meconium passage <b style="color:red">>48 hours</b>, 3) Family history of HD, and 4) <b style="color:red">Ribbon stools</b>.
* **Neurologic Alarm Signs**: The presence of a <b style="color:red">tuft of hair on the spine</b>, sacral dimple, or gluteal cleft deviation may indicate a neurologic cause for constipation.
* **Physical Exam Findings**: Palpation often reveals a hard fecal mass in the <b style="color:red">left lower quadrant</b> of the abdomen.
* **Pharmacologic Management - Disimpaction**: The first step of treatment is removing the stool ball via <b style="color:red">Glycerin suppositories</b> (infants) or <b style="color:red">Phosphate enemas</b> (older children); oral PEG can also be used for "slow" disimpaction.
* **Pharmacologic Management - Maintenance**: Once disimpacted, patients require weeks to months of maintenance laxatives (e.g., <b style="color:red">Lactulose or Polyethylene glycol</b>) to prevent recurrence.
* **Toilet Training Position**: Proper positioning involves knees higher than hips, leaning forward with elbows on knees, and feet well-supported (not "frog" position).

### Peptic Ulcer Disease (PUD)

#### Classification of Ulcers
| Type | Common Location | Primary Cause |
| :--- | :--- | :--- |
| **Primary PUD** | Duodenum (Duodenal bulb). | <b style="color:red">Helicobacter pylori</b> infection. |
| **Secondary PUD** | Stomach (Gastric, lesser curvature). | NSAIDs, Stress (sepsis/shock), burns, or intracranial lesions. |

* **Secondary Ulcer Eponyms**: <b style="color:red">Cushing Ulcer</b> is associated with intracranial lesions; <b style="color:red">Curling Ulcer</b> is associated with severe burn injuries.
* **PUD Pathogenesis**: Disease results from an imbalance where <b style="color:red">damaging factors</b> (acid, pepsin, NSAIDs, H. pylori) outweigh <b style="color:red">defensive mechanisms</b> (mucus, bicarbonate, prostaglandins).
* **Clinical Presentation of PUD**: The classic symptom is <b style="color:red">epigastric pain</b> that is dull or aching and often <b style="color:red">alleviated by ingestion of food</b>.
* **PUD in Infants**: Infants may present with non-specific symptoms such as irritability, vomiting, feeding difficulty, or <b style="color:red">hematemesis</b>.
* **Gold Standard Diagnosis**: <b style="color:red">Upper Endoscopy (EGD)</b> is the preferred diagnostic tool to visualize the ulcer, perform a biopsy, and screen for H. pylori.
* **H. pylori Triple Therapy**: Treatment consists of <b style="color:red">one month of a PPI</b> combined with <b style="color:red">two antibiotics</b> (e.g., Amoxicillin and Clarithromycin or Metronidazole) for 14 days.
* **Surgical Indications**: Surgery is reserved for complications such as <b style="color:red">uncontrollable bleeding, perforation, or obstruction</b>.

### Critical Comparisons and Differential Diagnoses

* **GER vs. GERD**: GER is a normal physiologic process ("happy spitter"), while GERD involves pathologic symptoms, complications, or failure to thrive.
* **Vomiting vs. Reflux**: Vomiting is a <b style="color:red">forceful</b> passage of gastric contents; Reflux is an <b style="color:red">effortless</b> passage.
* **Classic PUD vs. GERD Pain**: PUD pain is typically <b style="color:red">epigastric</b> and relieved by food; GERD pain is often described as <b style="color:red">retrosternal/heartburn</b> and may worsen after eating.
* **Primary vs. Secondary PUD**: Primary PUD is usually <b style="color:red">duodenal</b> and H. pylori-related; secondary PUD is usually <b style="color:red">gastric</b> and related to acute stress or drugs.
* **Cushing vs. Curling Ulcer**: Cushing ulcers are triggered by <b style="color:red">CNS/intracranial injury</b>; Curling ulcers are triggered by <b style="color:red">severe burns</b>.
* **Non-inflammatory vs. Inflammatory Diarrhea**: Non-inflammatory diarrhea is watery (toxin-mediated, no blood); inflammatory diarrhea is often <b style="color:red">bloody (dysentery)</b> due to mucosal invasion.
* **Functional Constipation vs. Hirschsprung Disease**: Functional constipation usually starts at <b style="color:red">6 months</b> (dietary change) and has stools in the rectal vault; Hirschsprung presents <b style="color:red">at birth</b> with delayed meconium and an empty rectum on exam.
* **Encopresis vs. Diarrhea**: Encopresis is the leakage of stool around a <b style="color:red">fecal impaction</b> in a constipated child; diarrhea is an increase in stool frequency and liquidity without impaction.
* **PPIs vs. H2RAs in GERD**: PPIs (e.g., Omeprazole) are used for <b style="color:red">long-term management</b>; H2RAs (e.g., Ranitidine) are not recommended for chronic GERD treatment.
* **Projectile vs. Non-projectile Vomiting**: Projectile vomiting suggests <b style="color:red">Increased ICP or Pyloric Stenosis</b>; non-projectile vomiting is more common in GERD or Gastroenteritis.
* **Bilious vs. Bloody Vomitus**: Bilious vomiting (green) indicates obstruction <b style="color:red">distal to the ampulla of Vater</b>; bloody vomiting (hematemesis) indicates gastritis, ulcers, or esophagitis.
* **Achalasia vs. Pyloric Stenosis Imaging**: Achalasia shows a <b style="color:red">"bird's beak"</b> on barium swallow; Pyloric stenosis is typically diagnosed via <b style="color:red">ultrasound</b> (thickened pylorus).
* **Reactive Arthritis vs. Sepsis**: Reactive arthritis is a <b style="color:red">post-infectious</b> (1-3 weeks later) joint pain; sepsis is an acute, systemic inflammatory response during the active infection.
* **Lactulose vs. Polyethylene Glycol (PEG)**: Both are osmotic laxatives used for <b style="color:red">maintenance</b> in constipation; PEG is often used for both rapid disimpaction and maintenance.
* **Sandifer Syndrome vs. Seizures**: Sandifer syndrome involves arching and neck posturing <b style="color:red">temporally related to feeding</b> (reflux-induced); seizures are generally not related to meals.


## QA
text
### Gastroesophageal Reflux (GER) and Disease (GERD)

1. What is the definition of <b style="color:red">Gastroesophageal Reflux (GER)</b>? | Physiologic retrograde movement. <br>Movement of gastric contents across the LES into the esophagus.
2. What is the definition of <b style="color:red">Gastroesophageal Reflux Disease (GERD)</b>? | Pathologic reflux. <br>Associated with troublesome symptoms or complications.
3. Contrast the complications of <b style="color:red">GER vs GERD</b>. | GER: None; physiologic. <br>GERD: Esophagitis, stricture, Barrett's, failure to thrive.
4. Contrast the effort of passage in <b style="color:red">GER vs GERD</b>. | GER: Effortless (spitting up). <br>GERD: Forceful passage or distress.
5. What is the most common esophageal disorder in children? | <b style="color:red">GERD</b>.
6. Which dietary factors are linked to the increasing incidence of <b style="color:red">GERD</b>? | Western diets. <br>High fatty food intake.
7. What is the major mechanism of pathologic reflux in <b style="color:red">GERD</b>? | <b style="color:red">Transient LES relaxation (TLESR)</b>.
8. Is <b style="color:red">Transient lower esophageal sphincter relaxation (TLESR)</b> related to swallowing? | No.
9. What factor increases the frequency of <b style="color:red">Transient lower esophageal sphincter relaxation (TLESR)</b>? | Gastric distention.
10. Name the components of the <b style="color:red">defective anti-reflux barrier</b> in GERD. (3) | 1) LES <br>2) Crural diaphragm <br>3) Hiatal hernia.
11. How does <b style="color:red">diminished esophageal clearance</b> contribute to GERD? | Prolongs acid contact. <br>Affected by poor peristalsis or supine position.
12. How does <b style="color:red">xerostomia</b> affect GERD? | Lack of saliva <br>Decreases esophageal clearance.
13. Name three external triggers that exacerbate <b style="color:red">GERD</b>. | 1) High-fat diets <br>2) Smoking <br>3) Medications.
14. How does <b style="color:red">nicotine</b> from smoking affect the esophagus? | Relaxes the LES.
15. Which specific medication classes can exacerbate <b style="color:red">GERD</b>? (2) | ACE inhibitors; <br>Calcium Channel Blockers (CCBs).
16. At what age does <b style="color:red">infantile reflux</b> peak? | <b style="color:red">4 months of age</b>.
17. By what age does <b style="color:red">GER</b> resolve in 88% of cases? | <b style="color:red">12 months</b>.
18. What is the term for infants who are active and feeding well despite <b style="color:red">GER</b>? | <b style="color:red">"Happy spitters"</b>.
19. What are the clinical signs of <b style="color:red">Sandifer Syndrome</b>? (2) | <b style="color:red">Neck contortions and arching</b>.
20. What does the arching in <b style="color:red">Sandifer Syndrome</b> resemble? | Opisthotonus.
21. What behavioral symptom is often associated with <b style="color:red">Sandifer Syndrome</b>? | Food refusal.
22. Name the manifestations of <b style="color:red">Infantile GERD</b>. (5) | Irritability, arching, choking, <br>feeding aversion, failure to thrive.
23. What are the typical symptoms of <b style="color:red">GERD in older children</b>? (3) | <b style="color:red">Heartburn, chest pain</b>, abdominal pain.
24. Which respiratory conditions may be associated with <b style="color:red">GERD in older children</b>? | Asthma or chronic cough.
25. How is <b style="color:red">GERD</b> primarily diagnosed? | <b style="color:red">Clinical diagnosis</b>.
26. In which patients can an <b style="color:red">empirical trial of PPI therapy</b> confirm GERD? | <b style="color:red">Older children and adults only</b>.
27. What is the duration of an empirical PPI trial for <b style="color:red">GERD</b>? | 2-4 weeks.
28. What is the role of a <b style="color:red">barium swallow</b> in vomiting? | Identify structural abnormalities.
29. What is the classic barium swallow finding for <b style="color:red">achalasia</b>? | <b style="color:red">"Bird’s beak"</b> appearance.
30. Is a <b style="color:red">barium swallow</b> used to diagnose GERD? | No.
31. List nonpharmacologic strategies for <b style="color:red">GERD</b> management. (4) | Avoid overfeeding, thicken feeds, <br>hydrolyzed formula, upright position.
32. Which acid suppressants are recommended for long-term <b style="color:red">GERD</b> treatment? | <b style="color:red">Proton Pump Inhibitors (PPIs)</b>.
33. Are <b style="color:red">H2RAs</b> recommended for chronic GERD use? | No.
34. What is the status of <b style="color:red">prokinetics</b> (e.g., domperidone) in GERD management? | Generally discouraged.

### Gastrointestinal Infections and Acute Gastroenteritis

35. Describe the mechanism of <b style="color:red">Noninflammatory diarrhea</b>. | Enterotoxin production. <br>Or villus destruction.
36. Are there WBCs or RBCs in the stool of <b style="color:red">Noninflammatory diarrhea</b>? | No.
37. Name three pathogens that cause <b style="color:red">Noninflammatory diarrhea</b>. | Rotavirus, Vibrio cholerae, ETEC.
38. Describe the mechanism of <b style="color:red">Inflammatory diarrhea</b>. | Direct mucosal invasion. <br>Or cytotoxin production.
39. What is the clinical stool appearance in <b style="color:red">Inflammatory diarrhea</b>? | Bloody stools.
40. Name three pathogens that cause <b style="color:red">Inflammatory diarrhea</b>. | Shigella, Salmonella, E. histolytica.
41. Describe the mechanism of <b style="color:red">Penetrating diarrhea</b>. | Invasion beyond epithelium. <br>Invasion beyond the intestinal epithelium.
42. Name three pathogens that cause <b style="color:red">Penetrating diarrhea</b>. | Nontyphoidal Salmonella, Yersinia, Campylobacter.
43. What is the global impact of <b style="color:red">diarrhea</b> in children under 5? | <b style="color:red">Top three cause of mortality</b>.
44. What etiologic group is the most common cause of <b style="color:red">Gastroenteritis</b>? | <b style="color:red">Viruses</b>.
45. What is the most common cause of <b style="color:red">severe diarrhea</b> in children? | <b style="color:red">Rotavirus</b>.
46. What is the primary route of transmission for <b style="color:red">GI infections</b>? | <b style="color:red">Fecal-oral transmission</b>.
47. What is the most common manifestation of <b style="color:red">acute gastroenteritis</b>? | <b style="color:red">Dehydration</b>.
48. What is the priority for clinical assessment in <b style="color:red">Gastroenteritis</b>? | Dehydration.
49. What extraintestinal complication involves knee pain 1-3 weeks after <b style="color:red">Salmonella or Shigella</b>? | <b style="color:red">Reactive Arthritis</b>.
50. What complication occurs weeks after a <b style="color:red">Campylobacter</b> infection? | <b style="color:red">Guillain-Barre Syndrome</b>.
51. What does <b style="color:red">Hemolytic Uremic Syndrome (HUS)</b> present with? | Sudden onset renal failure.
52. Which pathogens are associated with <b style="color:red">Hemolytic Uremic Syndrome (HUS)</b>? (2) | <b style="color:red">Shigella dysenteriae 1; E. coli O157:H7</b>.
53. What is the mainstay of treatment for <b style="color:red">acute gastroenteritis</b>? | Rehydration therapy.
54. What is used for rehydration in <b style="color:red">mild</b> cases of gastroenteritis? | <b style="color:red">Oral Rehydration Salts (ORS)</b>.
55. What is the treatment for <b style="color:red">severe shock</b> in gastroenteritis? | IV fluids.
56. Why is <b style="color:red">Zinc</b> recommended in diarrhea management? | Reduce duration/severity.
57. List three strategies for <b style="color:red">prevention</b> of GI infections. | 1) Rotavirus vaccination <br>2) Breastfeeding (up to 2 yrs) <br>3) Handwashing.

### Functional Constipation and Encopresis

58. How many Rome IV criteria must be met for <b style="color:red">Functional Constipation</b>? | 2 or more.
59. What is the frequency criterion for <b style="color:red">Functional Constipation</b>? | ≤2 defecations per week.
60. What is the <b style="color:red">incontinence</b> criterion for children ≥4 years? | At least 1 episode/week.
61. What is the <b style="color:red">posturing</b> criterion for functional constipation? | Retentive posturing. <br>History of volitional retention.
62. What is the <b style="color:red">mass</b> criterion for functional constipation? | Large fecal mass in rectum.
63. What is the <b style="color:red">diameter</b> criterion for functional constipation? | History of large diameter stools.
64. What is the definition of <b style="color:red">Encopresis</b>? | <b style="color:red">Involuntary loss</b> of stools.
65. What is the developmental age required for <b style="color:red">Encopresis</b> diagnosis? | <b style="color:red">4 years</b>.
66. At what age does <b style="color:red">functional constipation</b> often start? | <b style="color:red">6 months of age</b>.
67. What dietary change often triggers <b style="color:red">functional constipation</b>? | Introduction of complementary feeding.
68. What is suggested if constipation onset is <b style="color:red">&lt6 months of age</b>? | Organic causes. <br>Ex: <b style="color:red">Hirschsprung Disease</b>.
69. Name four alarm signs for <b style="color:red">Hirschsprung Disease</b>. | 1) Onset month 1 <br>2) Meconium >48 hrs <br>3) Family history <br>4) Ribbon stools.
70. What is the meconium passage time alarm sign for <b style="color:red">Hirschsprung Disease</b>? | <b style="color:red">>48 hours</b>.
71. What do <b style="color:red">ribbon stools</b> suggest? | Hirschsprung Disease.
72. Name three neurologic alarm signs for <b style="color:red">constipation</b>. | 1) Tuft of hair on spine <br>2) Sacral dimple <br>3) Gluteal cleft deviation.
73. Where is a hard fecal mass usually palpated in <b style="color:red">functional constipation</b>? | <b style="color:red">Left lower quadrant</b> (LLQ).
74. What is the first step of <b style="color:red">Pharmacologic Management</b> for constipation? | Disimpaction.
75. Which disimpaction method is used for <b style="color:red">infants</b>? | <b style="color:red">Glycerin suppositories</b>.
76. Which disimpaction method is used for <b style="color:red">older children</b>? | <b style="color:red">Phosphate enemas</b>.
77. What oral agent can be used for <b style="color:red">"slow" disimpaction</b>? | Oral Polyethylene glycol (PEG).
78. What is the purpose of <b style="color:red">maintenance laxatives</b>? | Prevent recurrence.
79. Name two laxatives used for <b style="color:red">maintenance</b>. | <b style="color:red">Lactulose; Polyethylene glycol</b>.
80. Describe the proper <b style="color:red">toilet training position</b>. | Knees higher than hips. <br>Leaning forward; feet supported.

### Peptic Ulcer Disease (PUD)

81. What is the most common location for <b style="color:red">Primary PUD</b>? | <b style="color:red">Duodenum</b> (Duodenal bulb).
82. What is the primary cause of <b style="color:red">Primary PUD</b>? | <b style="color:red">Helicobacter pylori</b>.
83. What is the common location for <b style="color:red">Secondary PUD</b>? | Stomach (Gastric).
84. Name causes of <b style="color:red">Secondary PUD</b>. (4) | NSAIDs, Stress (sepsis), <br>burns, intracranial lesions.
85. What is a <b style="color:red">Cushing Ulcer</b> associated with? | Intracranial lesions.
86. What is a <b style="color:red">Curling Ulcer</b> associated with? | Severe burn injuries.
87. What causes <b style="color:red">PUD Pathogenesis</b>? | Imbalance of factors. <br>Acid/pepsin vs mucus/bicarbonate/prostaglandins.
88. What is the classic symptom of <b style="color:red">Peptic Ulcer Disease (PUD)</b>? | <b style="color:red">Epigastric pain</b>.
89. How is <b style="color:red">PUD pain</b> typically described? | Dull or aching.
90. What factor often alleviates <b style="color:red">PUD pain</b>? | <b style="color:red">Ingestion of food</b>.
91. Name symptoms of <b style="color:red">PUD in infants</b>. | Irritability, vomiting, <br>feeding difficulty, hematemesis.
92. What is the gold standard diagnosis for <b style="color:red">PUD</b>? | <b style="color:red">Upper Endoscopy (EGD)</b>.
93. What can be performed during an <b style="color:red">Upper Endoscopy (EGD)</b>? | Biopsy and H. pylori screening.
94. What is the PPI duration in <b style="color:red">H. pylori Triple Therapy</b>? | <b style="color:red">One month</b>.
95. What antibiotics are used in <b style="color:red">H. pylori Triple Therapy</b>? (2) | Amoxicillin; <br>Clarithromycin or Metronidazole.
96. How long is the course for antibiotics in <b style="color:red">Triple Therapy</b>? | 14 days.
97. List three surgical indications for <b style="color:red">PUD</b>. | 1) Uncontrollable bleeding <br>2) Perforation <br>3) Obstruction.

### Critical Comparisons and Differential Diagnoses

98. Compare <b style="color:red">GER vs GERD</b>. | GER: Normal ("happy spitter"). <br>GERD: Pathologic symptoms/complications.
99. Compare <b style="color:red">Vomiting vs Reflux</b>. | Vomiting: <b style="color:red">Forceful</b> passage. <br>Reflux: <b style="color:red">Effortless</b> passage.
100. Compare <b style="color:red">PUD vs GERD pain</b> location. | PUD: <b style="color:red">Epigastric</b>. <br>GERD: <b style="color:red">Retrosternal/heartburn</b>.
101. How is <b style="color:red">PUD vs GERD pain</b> affected by eating? | PUD: Relieved by food. <br>GERD: Worsens after eating.
102. Compare <b style="color:red">Cushing vs Curling Ulcer</b> triggers. | Cushing: <b style="color:red">CNS injury</b>. <br>Curling: <b style="color:red">Severe burns</b>.
103. Compare <b style="color:red">Non-inflammatory vs Inflammatory Diarrhea</b>. | Non-inflammatory: Watery/toxin-mediated. <br>Inflammatory: <b style="color:red">Bloody (dysentery)</b>.
104. Compare <b style="color:red">Functional Constipation vs Hirschsprung</b> onset. | Functional: <b style="color:red">6 months</b>. <br>Hirschsprung: <b style="color:red">At birth</b>.
105. Compare <b style="color:red">Functional Constipation vs Hirschsprung</b> rectal exam. | Functional: Stools in rectal vault. <br>Hirschsprung: Empty rectum.
106. Compare <b style="color:red">Encopresis vs Diarrhea</b>. | Encopresis: Leakage around <b style="color:red">fecal impaction</b>. <br>Diarrhea: Increased frequency/liquidity.
107. Compare <b style="color:red">PPI vs H2RA</b> in GERD. | PPI: <b style="color:red">Long-term management</b>. <br>H2RA: Not for chronic use.
108. What causes <b style="color:red">Projectile vomiting</b>? | <b style="color:red">Increased ICP</b> or <b style="color:red">Pyloric Stenosis</b>.
109. What causes <b style="color:red">Non-projectile vomiting</b>? | GERD or Gastroenteritis.
110. What does <b style="color:red">Bilious vomiting</b> (green) indicate? | Obstruction <b style="color:red">distal to ampulla of Vater</b>.
111. What does <b style="color:red">Bloody vomiting</b> (hematemesis) indicate? | Gastritis, ulcers, or esophagitis.
112. Compare <b style="color:red">Achalasia vs Pyloric Stenosis</b> imaging. | Achalasia: Barium swallow ("bird's beak"). <br>Pyloric Stenosis: <b style="color:red">Ultrasound</b> (thickened pylorus).
113. Compare <b style="color:red">Reactive Arthritis vs Sepsis</b> joint timing. | Reactive: <b style="color:red">Post-infectious</b> (1-3 weeks later). <br>Sepsis: Acute systemic infection.
114. Compare <b style="color:red">Lactulose vs Polyethylene Glycol (PEG)</b> use. | Both: Maintenance. <br>PEG: Maintenance AND rapid disimpaction.
115. Compare <b style="color:red">Sandifer Syndrome vs Seizures</b>. | Sandifer: related to <b style="color:red">feeding</b>. <br>Seizures: Not related to meals.







# 7 - Intestinal Obstruction

## Summary
text
### Intestinal Obstruction: General Principles

| Feature                           | Details                                                                                                                                                     |
| :-------------------------------- | :---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| **Classification by Mechanism**   | <b style="color:red">Functional</b> (myopathic, neuropathic) or <b style="color:red">Mechanical</b> (physical intraluminal or extraintestinal).             |
| **Classification by Extent**      | <b style="color:red">Partial</b> (allows passage of liquid/gas) or <b style="color:red">Complete</b> (nothing passes).                                      |
| **Anatomic Localization**         | <b style="color:red">Upper GI</b> (bilious vomiting, minimal distention) vs <b style="color:red">Lower GI</b> (abdominal distention, feculent vomiting).    |
| **Obstruction Type**              | <b style="color:red">Simple</b> (partial/complete without ischemia) vs <b style="color:red">Strangulating</b> (compromised blood flow).                     |
| **Neonatal Presentation**         | Maternal polyhydramnios, abdominal distention, failure to pass meconium (within 24-48 hours), "currant jelly" stools.                                       |
| **Child/Adolescent Presentation** | Colicky abdominal pain, distention, nausea, vomiting, obstipation.                                                                                          |
| **Physical Exam Findings**        | Hyperactive bowel sounds and tenderness (Mechanical); <b style="color:red">Absent bowel sounds and painful mass</b> (Strangulating).                        |
| **Radiographic Signs**            | Plain X-ray: Distended loops, <b style="color:red">air-fluid levels</b>, paucity of colonic air.                                                            |
| **CT Scan Findings**              | Target sign (bowel wall thickening), pneumatosis intestinalis (air in wall), portal venous gas, <b style="color:red">beak sign</b> (distal narrowing).      |
| **Management Priorities**         | 1) Bowel decompression (NGT/OGT) 2) Fluid resuscitation 3) Monitoring electrolytes 4) Conservative trial (Partial) 5) Early surgery (Complete/Malrotation). |

### Functional Obstruction: Ileus
*   <b style="color:red">Ileus</b> is defined as the failure of intestinal peristalsis (paralytic/adynamic) without evidence of mechanical obstruction.
*   Common triggers for <b style="color:red">Ileus</b> include abdominal surgery (usually resolves within 72 hours), infections (AGE, pneumonia, peritonitis), and metabolic abnormalities like <b style="color:red">hypokalemia</b>, hypercalcemia, and acidosis.
*   Medications that cause <b style="color:red">Ileus</b> include opiates, vincristine, and antimotility agents like <b style="color:red">loperamide</b>.
*   The clinical presentation of <b style="color:red">Ileus</b> involves abdominal distention, nausea, vomiting, a tympanitic abdomen, and <b style="color:red">minimal or absent bowel sounds</b>.
*   Radiographic diagnosis of <b style="color:red">Ileus</b> shows air-fluid levels without progressive bowel distention; barium enema reveals delayed movement through the lumen.
*   Management of <b style="color:red">Ileus</b> focuses on correcting underlying abnormalities, nasogastric decompression, fluid replacement, and mitigating iatrogenic causes (e.g., stopping offending drugs).

### Mechanical Obstruction: Intussusception
*   <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b> is the invagination of one segment of intestine (intussusceptum) into an adjacent segment (intussuscipiens).
*   <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b> is the most common cause of intestinal obstruction in children between <b style="color:red">5 months and 3 years of age</b>.
*   <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b> is the most common abdominal emergency in children under 2 years old, with a 3:1 male-to-female ratio.
*   The most common anatomic type of <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b> is <b style="color:red">ileocolic</b>, followed by cecocolic and ileoileal.
*   While 90% of cases are <b style="color:red">idiopathic</b> (often linked to hypertrophied Peyer's patches), lead points like <b style="color:red">Meckel diverticulum</b>, polyps, or lymphoma are common in children over 2 years old.
*   The typical presentation of <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b> is a previously well child with sudden, <b style="color:red">paroxysmal colicky pain</b> who is comfortable between episodes.
*   The classic triad of <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b> (occurring in <30% of cases) includes abdominal pain, a sausage-shaped mass, and <b style="color:red">"currant jelly" stools</b> (blood + mucus).
*   Physical examination of <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b> may reveal a slightly tender, <b style="color:red">sausage-shaped mass</b> in the right upper abdomen.
*   On Ultrasound, <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b> is diagnosed by the <b style="color:red">"Target sign"</b> or <b style="color:red">"Pseudo-kidney sign"</b>; Barium enema shows the <b style="color:red">"Coiled spring sign."</b>
*   Conservative treatment for <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b> involves radiologic <b style="color:red">hydrostatic reduction</b> (fluoroscopic or ultrasound-guided).
*   Surgical intervention for <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b> is indicated for shock, suspected bowel necrosis/perf, peritonitis, or multiple recurrences.

### Inflammatory Bowel Disease (IBD)

| Feature | <b style="color:red">Crohn's Disease (CD)</b> | <b style="color:red">Ulcerative Colitis (UC)</b> |
| :--- | :--- | :--- |
| **Distribution** | Any part of GI tract (mouth to anus); <b style="color:red">Skip lesions</b>. | Confined to colon and rectum; <b style="color:red">Continuous involvement</b>. |
| **Inflammation Depth** | <b style="color:red">Transmural</b> (all layers). | <b style="color:red">Mucosal</b> (surface layer). |
| **GI Symptoms** | Chronic pain, RLQ mass, <b style="color:red">perianal lesions</b> (fistula/abscess). | <b style="color:red">Rectal bleeding</b>, diarrhea with mucus. |
| **Growth Failure** | <b style="color:red">Common</b> in pediatric cases. | Not common. |
| **Histology** | <b style="color:red">Noncaseating granulomas</b>; crypt distortion. | Crypt distortion and abscesses; no granulomas. |
| **Extraintestinal** | Arthritis, uveitis, skin lesions. | <b style="color:red">Pyoderma gangrenosum</b>, Sclerosing cholangitis. |

*   <b style="color:red">IBD pathogenesis</b> involves genetic predisposition (e.g., <b style="color:red">NOD2 gene</b> in CD), dysbiosis (microbiota imbalance), and environmental triggers like the Western diet.
*   Diagnosis of <b style="color:red">IBD</b> utilizes <b style="color:red">Fecal calprotectin</b> as a marker of inflammation and colonoscopy with biopsy for confirmation.
*   Pharmacological treatment of <b style="color:red">IBD</b> includes <b style="color:red">Aminosalicylates</b> (first-line for mild UC), Corticosteroids (remission induction), and <b style="color:red">Biologics</b> (Anti-TNF like Infliximab).
*   <b style="color:red">Exclusive Enteral Nutrition (EEN)</b> is a primary therapy for pediatric Crohn's Disease.
*   Long-term monitoring for <b style="color:red">IBD</b> involves growth tracking, bone density (osteopenia risk), and <b style="color:red">cancer screening</b> for chronic UC.

### Disorders of Malabsorption

*   <b style="color:red">Celiac Disease</b> is an immune-mediated disorder elicited by <b style="color:red">gluten</b> (found in wheat, rye, and barley) in genetically predisposed individuals (HLA-DQ2.5/DQ8).
*   <b style="color:red">Repeated rotavirus infections</b> are associated with an increased risk of developing Celiac Disease.
*   <b style="color:red">Iron deficiency anemia</b> is the most common extraintestinal manifestation of Celiac Disease.
*   Diagnostic serology for <b style="color:red">Celiac Disease</b> includes <b style="color:red">anti-TG2 IgA</b> (first line) and anti-endomysial antibodies.
*   Small bowel biopsy in <b style="color:red">Celiac Disease</b> reveals <b style="color:red">villous atrophy</b>, crypt hyperplasia, and increased intraepithelial lymphocytes.
*   Treatment for <b style="color:red">Celiac Disease</b> requires a lifelong, strict <b style="color:red">gluten-free diet</b> and correction of micronutrient deficiencies (Iron, Zinc, Vit D).
*   <b style="color:red">Postinfectious Diarrhea</b> in toddlers is often caused by <b style="color:red">secondary lactase deficiency</b> or food protein allergy following an acute GI infection.
*   <b style="color:red">Bacterial Overgrowth</b> (SIBO) involves colonization of the small intestine, leading to <b style="color:red">steatorrhea</b> (from bile salt deconjugation) and Vitamin B12 deficiency; treated with Metronidazole.
*   <b style="color:red">Short Bowel Syndrome</b> results from malabsorption secondary to the loss of <b style="color:red">>50% of the small bowel</b>.
*   <b style="color:red">Lactase Deficiency</b> types include primary (physiologic decline common in 40% of Asians) and <b style="color:red">secondary lactose intolerance</b> (transient damage post-infection/malnutrition).
*   Diagnosis of <b style="color:red">Lactase Deficiency</b> is usually made via a <b style="color:red">dietary elimination trial</b> or an H2 breath test.

### Critical Comparisons and High-Yield Distinctions

1.  <b style="color:red">Functional (Ileus) vs Mechanical Obstruction</b>: Ileus presents with <b style="color:red">absent/minimal bowel sounds</b> and is non-mechanical; Mechanical presents with hyperactive sounds initially and a physical blockage.
2.  <b style="color:red">Upper vs Lower GI Obstruction</b>: Upper GI presents with <b style="color:red">bilious vomiting</b> and low distention; Lower GI presents with <b style="color:red">feculent vomiting</b> and significant distention.
3.  <b style="color:red">Crohn's vs Ulcerative Colitis Distribution</b>: Crohn's is <b style="color:red">anywhere</b> (skip lesions); UC is <b style="color:red">rectum/colon only</b> (continuous).
4.  <b style="color:red">Crohn's vs Ulcerative Colitis Depth</b>: Crohn's is <b style="color:red">transmural</b> (all layers); UC is <b style="color:red">mucosal</b> only.
5.  <b style="color:red">Growth Failure</b>: Highly common in pediatric <b style="color:red">Crohn's</b>; rare in Ulcerative Colitis.
6.  <b style="color:red">Bleeding Profile</b>: <b style="color:red">Gross rectal bleeding</b> is the hallmark of UC; bleeding in Crohn's is less consistent but perianal disease (fistulas) is prominent.
7.  <b style="color:red">Intussusception Triad</b>: Sausage mass, paroxysmal pain, and <b style="color:red">currant jelly stools</b>.
8.  <b style="color:red">Imaging Signs</b>: <b style="color:red">Target sign</b> (Intussusception U/S or CT obstruction); <b style="color:red">Coiled spring</b> (Intussusception Barium); <b style="color:red">Bird's beak</b> (CT obstruction).
9.  <b style="color:red">Meconium Passage</b>: Failure to pass meconium within <b style="color:red">24-48 hours</b> is an alarm sign for Hirschsprung or neonatal obstruction.
10. <b style="color:red">Celiac Pathology</b>: Characterized by <b style="color:red">villous atrophy</b>; whereas Lactase deficiency is an enzymatic/transport defect without necessarily having atrophy (except in secondary cases).
11. <b style="color:red">Nutrient Deficiencies</b>: <b style="color:red">Iron deficiency</b> is the top sign in Celiac; <b style="color:red">Vitamin B12 deficiency</b> is a hallmark of Bacterial Overgrowth.
12. <b style="color:red">Age of Onset</b>: Intussusception peaks at <b style="color:red">5 months to 3 years</b>; IBD peaks in late adolescence.
13. <b style="color:red">Strangulating vs Simple Obstruction</b>: Strangulating presents with <b style="color:red">fever, acidosis, and leukocytosis</b>; simple obstruction presents mainly with mechanical symptoms.
14. <b style="color:red">Celiac Serology</b>: Anti-TG2 IgA is the first-line test; total IgA must be checked to ensure no IgA deficiency is masking results.
15. <b style="color:red">Lactose Intolerance Diagnosis</b>: A clinical <b style="color:red">dietary elimination trial</b> is often preferred over the more complex H2 breath test.


## QA
text
### Intestinal Obstruction: General Principles

1. What are the 2 classifications of <b style="color:red">Intestinal Obstruction</b> by mechanism? | <b style="color:red">Functional</b> and <b style="color:red">Mechanical</b>

2. Define <b style="color:red">Functional Obstruction</b> mechanisms. | Myopathic or neuropathic

3. Define <b style="color:red">Mechanical Obstruction</b> mechanisms. | Physical intraluminal or extraintestinal

4. What are the 2 classifications of <b style="color:red">Intestinal Obstruction</b> by extent? | <b style="color:red">Partial</b> and <b style="color:red">Complete</b>

5. Which <b style="color:red">Obstruction</b> extent allows the passage of liquid or gas? | <b style="color:red">Partial</b>

6. Which <b style="color:red">Obstruction</b> extent allows nothing to pass? | <b style="color:red">Complete</b>

7. Compare <b style="color:red">Upper GI</b> vs <b style="color:red">Lower GI</b> Localization: Vomiting type. | Upper: Bilious <br>Lower: Feculent

8. Compare <b style="color:red">Upper GI</b> vs <b style="color:red">Lower GI</b> Localization: Distention. | Upper: Minimal <br>Lower: Abdominal distention

9. What are the 2 <b style="color:red">Obstruction</b> types based on blood flow? | <b style="color:red">Simple</b> and <b style="color:red">Strangulating</b>

10. Define <b style="color:red">Simple Obstruction</b>. | Partial/complete without ischemia

11. Define <b style="color:red">Strangulating Obstruction</b>. | Compromised blood flow

12. Neonatal Presentation: What maternal history suggests <b style="color:red">Obstruction</b>? | Maternal polyhydramnios

13. Neonatal Presentation: Failure to pass meconium occurs within what timeframe? | 24-48 hours

14. Neonatal Presentation: What color/type of stool is concerning? | "Currant jelly" stools

15. <b style="color:red">Intestinal Obstruction</b>: Child/Adolescent Presentation (5). | 1) Colicky abdominal pain <br>2) Distention <br>3) Nausea <br>4) Vomiting <br>5) Obstipation

16. Physical Exam: What bowel sounds are found in <b style="color:red">Mechanical Obstruction</b>? | Hyperactive bowel sounds

17. Physical Exam: What findings suggest <b style="color:red">Strangulating Obstruction</b>? | <b style="color:red">Absent bowel sounds</b> and <b style="color:red">painful mass</b>

18. Radiographic Signs: What is seen on Plain X-ray for <b style="color:red">Obstruction</b>? (3) | 1) Distended loops <br>2) <b style="color:red">Air-fluid levels</b> <br>3) Paucity of colonic air

19. CT Scan Findings: What does the "Target sign" indicate? | Bowel wall thickening

20. CT Scan Findings: What is the term for "air in the bowel wall"? | Pneumatosis intestinalis

21. CT Scan Findings: What finding indicates gas in the veins? | Portal venous gas

22. CT Scan Findings: What does the <b style="color:red">beak sign</b> indicate? | Distal narrowing

23. <b style="color:red">Intestinal Obstruction</b> Management Priorities (5). | 1) Bowel decompression <br>2) Fluid resuscitation <br>3) Monitoring electrolytes <br>4) Conservative trial <br>5) Early surgery

24. Management: What is the first priority for bowel decompression? | NGT or OGT

25. Management: When is a conservative trial appropriate? | Partial obstruction

26. Management: When is early surgery indicated? (2) | 1) Complete obstruction <br>2) Malrotation

### Functional Obstruction: Ileus

27. Define <b style="color:red">Ileus</b>. | Failure of intestinal peristalsis

28. Is <b style="color:red">Ileus</b> a mechanical or non-mechanical obstruction? | Non-mechanical (paralytic/adynamic)

29. <b style="color:red">Ileus</b> Triggers: Abdominal surgery usually resolves within how many hours? | 72 hours

30. <b style="color:red">Ileus</b> Triggers: List 3 infection types. | 1) AGE <br>2) Pneumonia <br>3) Peritonitis

31. <b style="color:red">Ileus</b> Triggers: What metabolic abnormalities are causes? (3) | 1) <b style="color:red">Hypokalemia</b> <br>2) Hypercalcemia <br>3) Acidosis

32. What medication types cause <b style="color:red">Ileus</b>? (3) | 1) Opiates <br>2) Vincristine <br>3) Antimotility agents

33. Which specific antimotility agent is a known cause of <b style="color:red">Ileus</b>? | <b style="color:red">Loperamide</b>

34. Clinical Presentation of <b style="color:red">Ileus</b> (5). | 1) Abdominal distention <br>2) Nausea <br>3) Vomiting <br>4) Tympanitic abdomen <br>5) <b style="color:red">Minimal/absent bowel sounds</b>

35. Radiographic diagnosis of <b style="color:red">Ileus</b>: Plain X-ray finding. | Air-fluid levels without distention

36. Radiographic diagnosis of <b style="color:red">Ileus</b>: Barium enema finding. | Delayed movement through lumen

37. Management of <b style="color:red">Ileus</b> focuses on what primary step? | Correcting underlying abnormalities

38. Management of <b style="color:red">Ileus</b>: How is bowel pressure relieved? | Nasogastric decompression

39. Management of <b style="color:red">Ileus</b>: What prevents iatrogenic progression? | Stopping offending drugs

### Mechanical Obstruction: Intussusception

40. Define <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b>. | Invagination of one intestine segment

41. In <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b>, what is the name of the segment that invaginates? | Intussusceptum

42. In <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b>, what is the name of the receiving segment? | Intussuscipiens

43. What is the peak age range for <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b>? | <b style="color:red">5 months to 3 years</b>

44. <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b> is the most common abdominal emergency in children under what age? | 2 years old

45. What is the male-to-female ratio in <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b>? | 3:1 ratio

46. What is the most common anatomic type of <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b>? | <b style="color:red">Ileocolic</b>

47. Name 2 less common anatomic types of <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b>. | Cecocolic and ileoileal

48. What percentage of <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b> cases are <b style="color:red">idiopathic</b>? | 90%

49. What physiologic finding is often linked to idiopathic <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b>? | Hypertrophied Peyer's patches

50. Lead points in <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b> are common in children over what age? | 2 years old

51. Name 3 potential "lead points" for <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b>. | 1) <b style="color:red">Meckel diverticulum</b> <br>2) Polyps <br>3) Lymphoma

52. Describe the typical pain in <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b>. | Sudden, <b style="color:red">paroxysmal colicky pain</b>

53. How does a child with <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b> act between pain episodes? | Comfortable/well

54. The classic triad of <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b> includes (3). | 1) Abdominal pain <br>2) Sausage-shaped mass <br>3) <b style="color:red">"Currant jelly" stools</b>

55. What is the consistency of <b style="color:red">"currant jelly" stools</b>? | Blood + mucus

56. Physical examination of <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b>: Mass description and location. | <b style="color:red">Sausage-shaped mass</b> in RUQ

57. Ultrasound signs for <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b> (2). | <b style="color:red">"Target sign"</b> or <b style="color:red">"Pseudo-kidney sign"</b>

58. Barium enema sign for <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b>. | <b style="color:red">"Coiled spring sign"</b>

59. What is the primary conservative treatment for <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b>? | Radiologic <b style="color:red">hydrostatic reduction</b>

60. Name 2 methods of hydrostatic reduction for <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b>. | Fluoroscopic or ultrasound-guided

61. List 4 indications for surgical intervention in <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b>. | 1) Shock <br>2) Bowel necrosis/perf <br>3) Peritonitis <br>4) Multiple recurrences

### Inflammatory Bowel Disease (IBD)

62. IBD Distribution: Compare <b style="color:red">Crohn's Disease</b> vs <b style="color:red">Ulcerative Colitis</b>. | Crohn's: Mouth to anus (Skip lesions) <br>UC: Colon and rectum (Continuous)

63. IBD Inflammation Depth: Compare <b style="color:red">Crohn's Disease</b> vs <b style="color:red">Ulcerative Colitis</b>. | Crohn's: <b style="color:red">Transmural</b> <br>UC: <b style="color:red">Mucosal</b>

64. GI Symptoms: Which IBD type presents with RLQ mass and perianal lesions? | <b style="color:red">Crohn's Disease</b>

65. GI Symptoms: What is the hallmark symptom of <b style="color:red">Ulcerative Colitis</b>? | <b style="color:red">Rectal bleeding</b> (with mucus)

66. Growth Failure: Is it more common in <b style="color:red">Crohn's Disease</b> or <b style="color:red">Ulcerative Colitis</b>? | <b style="color:red">Crohn's Disease</b>

67. IBD Histology: Which disease features <b style="color:red">Noncaseating granulomas</b>? | <b style="color:red">Crohn's Disease</b>

68. IBD Histology: What is found in <b style="color:red">Ulcerative Colitis</b>? | Crypt distortion and abscesses

69. Extraintestinal: What skin condition is specific to <b style="color:red">Ulcerative Colitis</b> in this text? | <b style="color:red">Pyoderma gangrenosum</b>

70. Extraintestinal: Which IBD is linked to Sclerosing cholangitis? | <b style="color:red">Ulcerative Colitis</b>

71. Pathogenesis of <b style="color:red">IBD</b>: What gene is associated with <b style="color:red">Crohn's Disease</b>? | <b style="color:red">NOD2 gene</b>

72. Pathogenesis of <b style="color:red">IBD</b>: Define "dysbiosis". | Microbiota imbalance

73. Diagnosis of <b style="color:red">IBD</b>: What stool marker measures inflammation? | <b style="color:red">Fecal calprotectin</b>

74. Diagnosis of <b style="color:red">IBD</b>: What is required for confirmation? | Colonoscopy with biopsy

75. Pharmacological treatment: What is the first-line for mild <b style="color:red">Ulcerative Colitis</b>? | <b style="color:red">Aminosalicylates</b>

76. Pharmacological treatment: What is used for remission induction in <b style="color:red">IBD</b>? | Corticosteroids

77. Name a common Anti-TNF <b style="color:red">Biologic</b> used in <b style="color:red">IBD</b>. | Infliximab

78. What is the primary dietary therapy for pediatric <b style="color:red">Crohn's Disease</b>? | <b style="color:red">Exclusive Enteral Nutrition (EEN)</b>

79. Long-term monitoring for <b style="color:red">IBD</b> (3). | 1) Growth <br>2) Bone density <br>3) <b style="color:red">Cancer screening</b>

80. Why is bone density monitored in <b style="color:red">IBD</b>? | Osteopenia risk

81. Which IBD chronic condition requires <b style="color:red">cancer screening</b>? | Chronic UC

### Disorders of Malabsorption

82. Define <b style="color:red">Celiac Disease</b>. | Immune-mediated disorder elicited by gluten

83. What grains contain <b style="color:red">gluten</b>? (3) | Wheat, rye, and barley

89. Small bowel biopsy findings in <b style="color:red">Celiac Disease</b> (3). | 1) <b style="color:red">Villous atrophy</b> <br>2) Crypt hyperplasia <br>3) Increased lymphocytes

91. Which micronutrients often require correction in <b style="color:red">Celiac</b>? (3) | Iron, Zinc, Vitamin D

92. What causes <b style="color:red">Postinfectious Diarrhea</b> in toddlers? | <b style="color:red">Secondary lactase deficiency</b> or allergy

93. Define <b style="color:red">Bacterial Overgrowth (SIBO)</b> mechanism. | Colonization of small intestine

94. <b style="color:red">Bacterial Overgrowth</b> leads to what stool finding? | <b style="color:red">Steatorrhea</b>

95. Why does <b style="color:red">SIBO</b> cause steatorrhea? | Bile salt deconjugation

96. Which vitamin is deficient in <b style="color:red">Bacterial Overgrowth</b>? | Vitamin B12

97. What is the treatment for <b style="color:red">Bacterial Overgrowth</b>? | Metronidazole

98. Define <b style="color:red">Short Bowel Syndrome</b>. | Loss of <b style="color:red">>50% of small bowel</b>

99. <b style="color:red">Lactase Deficiency</b>: Describe "Primary" deficiency. | Physiologic decline (common in Asians)

100. <b style="color:red">Lactase Deficiency</b>: Describe "Secondary" deficiency. | Transient damage (post-infection)

101. What is the preferred diagnosis for <b style="color:red">Lactase Deficiency</b>? | <b style="color:red">Dietary elimination trial</b>

102. Besides elimination, what test diagnoses <b style="color:red">Lactase Deficiency</b>? | H2 breath test

### Critical Comparisons and High-Yield Distinctions

103. Distinguish <b style="color:red">Ileus</b> vs <b style="color:red">Mechanical Obstruction</b>: Sounds. | Ileus: <b style="color:red">Absent/minimal</b> <br>Mechanical: Hyperactive (initially)

104. Distinguish <b style="color:red">Upper GI</b> vs <b style="color:red">Lower GI</b>: Vomiting. | Upper: <b style="color:red">Bilious</b> <br>Lower: <b style="color:red">Feculent</b>

105. Distinguish <b style="color:red">Crohn's</b> vs <b style="color:red">Ulcerative Colitis</b>: Skip vs Continuous. | CD: <b style="color:red">Skip lesions</b> <br>UC: <b style="color:red">Continuous</b>

106. Compare <b style="color:red">Crohn's</b> vs <b style="color:red">UC</b> distribution. | CD: <b style="color:red">Anywhere</b> <br>UC: <b style="color:red">Rectum/colon only</b>

107. Distinguish <b style="color:red">Crohn's</b> vs <b style="color:red">UC</b>: Tissue depth. | CD: <b style="color:red">Transmural</b> <br>UC: <b style="color:red">Mucosal</b>

108. Which IBD type rarely presents with growth failure? | Ulcerative Colitis

109. Which IBD type is the hallmark for <b style="color:red">Gross rectal bleeding</b>? | Ulcerative Colitis

110. Which IBD type is characterized by prominent perianal fistulas? | Crohn's Disease

111. What are the 3 signs in the <b style="color:red">Intussusception Triad</b>? | Sausage mass, paroxysmal pain, <b style="color:red">currant jelly stools</b>

112. Imaging: What does the <b style="color:red">Target sign</b> mean? | Intussusception (U/S) or CT obstruction

113. Imaging: What does the <b style="color:red">Coiled spring</b> indicate? | Intussusception (Barium)

114. Imaging: What does the <b style="color:red">Bird's beak</b> indicate? | CT obstruction

115. Meconium Passage: Failure to pass meconium is an alarm for what 2 conditions? | Hirschsprung or neonatal obstruction

116. <b style="color:red">Celiac</b> vs <b style="color:red">Lactase</b>: Pathological difference. | Celiac: <b style="color:red">Villous atrophy</b> <br>Lactose: Enzymatic defect (no atrophy)

117. Nutrient Deficiency: Top sign in <b style="color:red">Celiac Disease</b>? | <b style="color:red">Iron deficiency</b>
