121. Compare <b style="color:red">Strangulating</b> vs <b style="color:red">Simple</b>: Systemic signs. | Strangulating: <b style="color:red">Fever, acidosis, leukocytosis</b> <br>Simple: Mechanical symptoms

122. <b style="color:red">Celiac Serology</b>: What must be checked alongside Anti-TG2 IgA? | Total IgA

123. Why check total IgA in <b style="color:red">Celiac</b> screening? | Ensure IgA deficiency isn't masking results

124. What is the preferred clinical way to diagnose <b style="color:red">Lactose Intolerance</b>? | <b style="color:red">Dietary elimination trial</b>



# 8

## Summary
text
### General Principles of Pediatric Gastrointestinal Obstruction

* Classification by Mechanism: Intestinal obstruction is classified as Functional (myopathic or neuropathic failure of peristalsis) or Mechanical (physical intraluminal or extraintestinal blockage).
* Classification by Extent: Intestinal obstruction is categorized as Partial (allows liquid/gas passage) or Complete (nothing passes).
* Localization by Vomiting Type: Upper GI obstructions typically present with bilious vomiting, while lower GI obstructions present with feculent vomiting.
* Localization by Distention: Upper GI obstructions usually show minimal distention; lower GI obstructions show significant abdominal distention.
* Classification by Blood Flow: Obstructions are either Simple (no ischemia) or Strangulating (compromised blood flow, often with absent bowel sounds and a painful mass).
* Neonatal Presentation: Warning signs of obstruction include maternal polyhydramnios, failure to pass meconium within 24-48 hours, and "currant jelly" stools.
* Radiographic Signs: Plain X-rays of intestinal obstruction show distended loops, air-fluid levels, and a paucity of colonic air.
* CT Scan Findings: The "Target sign" indicates bowel wall thickening, "Pneumatosis intestinalis" refers to air in the bowel wall, and the "beak sign" indicates distal narrowing.
* Management Priorities: Treatment for intestinal obstruction includes bowel decompression (NGT/OGT), fluid resuscitation, monitoring electrolytes, and either a conservative trial (for partial) or early surgery (for complete or malrotation).

### Esophageal Atresia (EA) and Tracheoesophageal Fistula (TEF)

| Category | Features of Esophageal Atresia and Tracheoesophageal Fistula (EA/TEF) |
| :--- | :--- |
| **Pathogenesis** | Failure of the esophagus to develop as a continuous passage; over 90% are associated with a Tracheoesophageal Fistula (TEF). Frequently associated with VACTERL syndrome (Vertebral, Anorectal, Cardiac, Tracheal, Esophageal, Renal, Limb). |
| **Classification** | Type C is the <b style="color:red">most common</b> (86%): upper esophagus is atretic, distal portion connects to the trachea. Type E (H-type) is a fistula without atresia, presenting as chronic respiratory problems. |
| **Clinical Presentation** | Neonates exhibit <b style="color:red">frothing and bubbling</b> at the mouth/nose, episodes of coughing, cyanosis, and respiratory distress. H-type presents later with recurrent pneumonia or respiratory symptoms. |
| **Diagnosis** | Primary bedside test: <b style="color:red">failure to pass an NGT or OGT</b> into the stomach. Prenatal U/S shows polyhydramnios and absence of a stomach bubble. |
| **Imaging** | X-ray shows a <b style="color:red">coiled feeding tube</b> in the esophageal pouch. An airless scaphoid abdomen suggests pure EA; an air-distended stomach indicates a coexisting TEF. |
| **Management** | Priorities include airway maintenance, pouch decompression, and avoiding mechanical ventilation, as positive pressure can cause gastric distension/perforation via fistula. |
| **Treatment** | Definitively treated with Primary Complete Repair (ligation and anastomosis) or Staged Repair (gastrostomy first) for high-risk infants. |

### Hypertrophic Pyloric Stenosis (HPS)

| Category | Features of Hypertrophic Pyloric Stenosis (HPS) |
| :--- | :--- |
| **Epidemiology** | More common in white males; strongly associated with maternal history and <b style="color:red">erythromycin/macrolide</b> use in the first 2 weeks of life. |
| **Pathogenesis** | Hypertrophy of the pyloric muscle leading to gastric outlet obstruction. |
| **Clinical Presentation** | <b style="color:red">Non-bilious, projectile vomiting</b> that is postprandial, typically starting after 3 weeks of age. |
| **Physical Exam** | Hallmark finding is a firm, movable, <b style="color:red">olive-shaped mass</b> (2cm) in the epigastrium, often palpated after vomiting, plus visible gastric peristaltic waves. |
| **Metabolic Profile** | Significant vomiting leads to <b style="color:red">hypochloremic metabolic alkalosis</b> and potentially "Icteropyloric Syndrome" (unconjugated hyperbilirubinemia). |
| **Diagnosis** | Ultrasound (95% sensitivity) shows pyloric thickness 3-4mm and length 15-19mm. |
| **Contrast Imaging** | Contrast studies reveal the <b style="color:red">string sign</b> (narrow channel), <b style="color:red">shoulder sign</b> (muscle bulge), and <b style="color:red">double tract sign</b>. |
| **Treatment** | Initial management is fluid/electrolyte correction. The surgical procedure of choice is <b style="color:red">Pyloromyotomy</b>. |

### Intestinal Atresia and Stenosis

| Category                        | Features of Duodenal Atresia                                                                                                                                                               |
| :------------------------------ | :----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| **Pathogenesis**                | Caused by <b style="color:red">failed recanalization</b> of the intestinal lumen during the 6th-7th week of gestation.                                                                     |
| **Associations**                | 1/3 of patients have <b style="color:red">Trisomy 21</b>; 50% are premature.                                                                                                               |
| **Clinical Presentation**       | The <b style="color:red">hallmark is bilious vomiting WITHOUT abdominal distention</b>, often with a history of maternal polyhydramnios.                                                   |
| **Diagnosis**                   | Plain abdominal X-ray shows the classic <b style="color:red">"double bubble" sign</b> (air in the stomach and proximal duodenum).                                                          |
| **Treatment**                   | Surgical intervention is required, typically a <b style="color:red">Duodenoduodenostomy</b>.                                                                                               |
| **Note on Jejunoileal Atresia** | Unlike duodenal atresia, jejunoileal atresia is usually caused by <b style="color:red">intrauterine vascular accidents</b> and is not typically associated with extraintestinal anomalies. |

### Hirschsprung’s Disease (HD)

| Category | Features of Hirschsprung’s Disease (HD) |
| :--- | :--- |
| **Pathogenesis** | Failure of neuroblasts to migrate, resulting in an <b style="color:red">absence of ganglion cells</b> (Meissner/Auerbach plexuses). This causes a lack of bowel relaxation and functional obstruction. |
| **Epidemiology** | The <b style="color:red">most common cause of lower GI obstruction</b> in neonates; Male:Female ratio is 4:1. Strongly associated with the RET gene and Trisomy 21. |
| **Clinical Presentation** | Suspected in full-term infants with <b style="color:red">delayed passage of meconium</b> (>24-48h). Signs include distention, bilious emesis, and explosive stool passage on DRE. |
| **Diagnosis (Gold Standard)** | <b style="color:red">Rectal Suction Biopsy</b> showing absence of ganglion cells and hypertrophied nerve bundles. |
| **Imaging (Barium Enema)** | Shows a transition zone; a <b style="color:red">Rectal:Sigmoid diameter ratio of ≤1</b> is highly suggestive of HD. |
| **Complications** | The most serious complication is <b style="color:red">Enterocolitis</b> (Hirschsprung’s-associated enterocolitis), presenting with sepsis, fever, and explosive diarrhea. |
| **Treatment** | Definitive treatment is a <b style="color:red">Primary pull-through procedure</b> (e.g., Soave). Staged surgery with an ostomy is used if enterocolitis is present. |

### Functional Obstruction: Ileus

* Functional Obstruction (Ileus) refers to the failure of intestinal peristalsis without a physical blockage, often triggered by abdominal surgery (resolves in 72h), infections (AGE, pneumonia, peritonitis), or metabolic issues.
* Metabolic causes of Ileus include <b style="color:red">hypokalemia</b>, hypercalcemia, and acidosis.
* Medications inducing Ileus include opiates, vincristine, and antimotility agents like <b style="color:red">loperamide</b>.
* Clinical signs of Ileus include abdominal distention, nausea, vomiting, and <b style="color:red">minimal/absent bowel sounds</b> (tympanitic abdomen).
* Diagnosis of Ileus via X-ray shows air-fluid levels without the distinct distended loops seen in mechanical obstruction.
* Management of Ileus focuses on correcting underlying abnormalities, stopping offending drugs, and nasogastric decompression.

### Mechanical Obstruction: Intussusception

* Intussusception is the invagination (telescoping) of one bowel segment (intussusceptum) into another (intussuscipiens), most commonly the <b style="color:red">ileocolic</b> type.
* Epidemiology: The peak age for intussusception is <b style="color:red">5 months to 3 years</b>; it is the most common abdominal emergency in children under 2.
* Lead Points: 90% are idiopathic (linked to hypertrophied Peyer's patches), but in children >2 years, lead points like <b style="color:red">Meckel diverticulum</b>, polyps, or lymphoma are common.
* Clinical Triad: The classic triad of intussusception is paroxysmal colicky pain, <b style="color:red">"currant jelly" stools</b> (blood + mucus), and a <b style="color:red">sausage-shaped mass</b> in the RUQ.
* Ultrasound Signs: Diagnosis is supported by the <b style="color:red">"Target sign"</b> or <b style="color:red">"Pseudo-kidney sign"</b> on ultrasound.
* Radiographic Signs: Barium enema shows the <b style="color:red">"coiled spring sign"</b>.
* Conservative Management: The primary treatment for intussusception is <b style="color:red">radiologic hydrostatic reduction</b> (fluoroscopic or U/S guided).
* Surgical Indications: Surgery for intussusception is indicated if there is shock, peritonitis, bowel necrosis, or multiple recurrences.

### Meckel Diverticulum

| Category | Features of Meckel Diverticulum |
| :--- | :--- |
| **Pathogenesis** | The <b style="color:red">most common congenital GI anomaly</b>; caused by incomplete obliteration of the omphalomesenteric duct. |
| **Rule of 2s** | 2% of population, symptomatic before age 2, 2 inches long, 2 feet from ileocecal valve, 2 types of ectopic tissue (gastric/pancreatic). |
| **Clinical Presentation** | Manifests as <b style="color:red">painless rectal bleeding</b>; stools are classically described as <b style="color:red">brick-colored or maroon</b>. |
| **Diagnosis** | Confirmed by a <b style="color:red">Meckel radionuclide scan</b> (Technetium-99m) which detects ectopic gastric mucosa. |
| **Treatment** | Surgical excision via <b style="color:red">Diverticulectomy</b> is the standard management. |

### Inflammatory Bowel Disease (IBD)

* IBD Distribution: Crohn's Disease can affect the entire tract (mouth to anus) with <b style="color:red">skip lesions</b>; Ulcerative Colitis (UC) is continuous and limited to the colon/rectum.
* Inflammation Depth: Crohn's is <b style="color:red">transmural</b> (entire wall); UC is limited to the <b style="color:red">mucosa</b>.
* Hallmark Symptoms: <b style="color:red">Rectal bleeding</b> is the hallmark of UC; Crohn's presents with abdominal pain, RLQ mass, and perianal lesions.
* Growth Failure: Significantly more common in <b style="color:red">Crohn's Disease</b> than UC.
* Histology: <b style="color:red">Noncaseating granulomas</b> are pathognomonic for Crohn's; UC shows crypt abscesses and distortion.
* Extraintestinal Manifestations: UC is specifically linked to <b style="color:red">pyoderma gangrenosum</b> and primary sclerosing cholangitis.
* Diagnosis: <b style="color:red">Fecal calprotectin</b> is used as a stool marker for inflammation; colonoscopy with biopsy is required for confirmation.
* Treatment: Mild UC is treated with <b style="color:red">aminosalicylates</b>; IBD remission is induced with corticosteroids; biologics like Infliximab are used for severe cases.
* Dietary Therapy: <b style="color:red">Exclusive Enteral Nutrition (EEN)</b> is a primary dietary therapy for pediatric Crohn’s Disease.

### Celiac Disease and Malabsorption

* Celiac Disease is an immune-mediated disorder triggered by <b style="color:red">gluten</b> (wheat, rye, barley) in genetically susceptible individuals (HLA-DQ2.5/DQ8).
* Extraintestinal Celiac Sign: The most common extraintestinal manifestation of Celiac is <b style="color:red">iron deficiency anemia</b>.
* Celiac Serology: First-line test is <b style="color:red">anti-TG2 IgA</b>; total IgA must be checked to ensure deficiency doesn't mask a negative result.
* Celiac Histology: Small bowel biopsy shows <b style="color:red">villous atrophy</b>, crypt hyperplasia, and increased lymphocytes.
* Bacterial Overgrowth (SIBO): Small intestine colonization that causes <b style="color:red">steatorrhea</b> (via bile salt deconjugation) and <b style="color:red">Vitamin B12 deficiency</b>; treated with Metronidazole.
* Lactase Deficiency: Primary (physiologic decline) vs. Secondary (transient post-infection damage); diagnosed primarily via <b style="color:red">dietary elimination trial</b> or H2 breath test.
* Short Bowel Syndrome: Defined as the loss of <b style="color:red">>50% of the small bowel</b>.

### High-Yield Comparisons for Exams

* Vomiting Color: Pyloric Stenosis is <b style="color:red">non-bilious</b>; Duodenal Atresia and Hirschsprung's are <b style="color:red">bilious</b>.
* Abdominal Distention: Pyloric Stenosis and Duodenal Atresia have <b style="color:red">no/minimal distention</b>; Hirschsprung's and Lower GI Obstruction have <b style="color:red">prominent distention</b>.
* Stool Characteristics: Intussusception has <b style="color:red">currant jelly stools</b>; Meckel Diverticulum has <b style="color:red">maroon/brick stools</b>; UC has gross <b style="color:red">bloody mucus stools</b>.
* Key X-ray Signs: Duodenal Atresia shows the <b style="color:red">Double Bubble</b>; Intussusception shows the <b style="color:red">Coiled Spring</b> (on barium); Pyloric Stenosis shows the <b style="color:red">String Sign</b>.
* Key Physical Exam Signs: Pyloric Stenosis has an <b style="color:red">Olive mass</b>; Intussusception has a <b style="color:red">Sausage mass</b>.
* Bowel Sounds: Mechanical obstruction has <b style="color:red">hyperactive</b> sounds initially; Ileus and Strangulating obstruction have <b style="color:red">absent/minimal</b> sounds.
* Celiac vs. Lactose Intolerance: Celiac involves <b style="color:red">villous atrophy</b> and iron deficiency; Lactose intolerance is an enzymatic defect with no mucosal damage on biopsy.
* Crohn's vs. UC Depth: Crohn's is <b style="color:red">transmural</b> (risk of fistulas); UC is <b style="color:red">mucosal</b> only.
* Meconium Passage: Delayed meconium passage in a full-term infant is an alarm for <b style="color:red">Hirschsprung's Disease</b>.
* Growth Failure: Prominent in <b style="color:red">Crohn's Disease</b> and <b style="color:red">Celiac Disease</b>; rare in Ulcerative Colitis.
* Anemia Type: Celiac Disease is classic for <b style="color:red">Iron deficiency</b>; Bacterial Overgrowth (SIBO) is classic for <b style="color:red">B12 deficiency</b>.
* EA/TEF subtypes: Pure Atresia results in a <b style="color:red">scaphoid abdomen</b> (no air); coexisting TEF results in a <b style="color:red">gas-distended stomach</b>.
* Crohn's vs. UC distribution: Crohn's features <b style="color:red">Skip lesions</b>; UC features <b style="color:red">Continuous</b> inflammation starting from the rectum.
* Age of Diagnosis: HPS (3-6 weeks); Intussusception (5mo - 3 years); Meckel (often by 2 years); IBD (late adolescence).


## QA
text
### General Principles of Pediatric Gastrointestinal Obstruction

1. How is <b style="color:red">Intestinal Obstruction</b> classified by mechanism? (2) | Functional and Mechanical
2. Define the mechanism of <b style="color:red">Functional Obstruction</b>. | Myopathic or neuropathic failure of peristalsis.
3. Define the mechanism of <b style="color:red">Mechanical Obstruction</b>. | Physical intraluminal or extraintestinal blockage.
4. How is <b style="color:red">Intestinal Obstruction</b> categorized by extent? (2) | Partial and Complete
5. What does <b style="color:red">Partial Intestinal Obstruction</b> allow to pass? | Liquid and gas
6. What is the characteristic of <b style="color:red">Complete Intestinal Obstruction</b> passage? | Nothing passes
7. What type of vomiting is typical in <b style="color:red">Upper GI Obstructions</b>? | Bilious vomiting
8. What type of vomiting is typical in <b style="color:red">Lower GI Obstructions</b>? | Feculent vomiting
9. Contrast abdominal distention in <b style="color:red">Upper vs. Lower GI Obstruction</b>. | Upper: Minimal distention. <br>Lower: Significant distention.
10. How is <b style="color:red">Intestinal Obstruction</b> classified by blood flow? (2) | Simple and Strangulating
11. Define <b style="color:red">Strangulating Obstruction</b>. | Compromised blood flow. <br>Often with absent bowel sounds and a painful mass.
12. What are the neonatal warning signs of <b style="color:red">Gastrointestinal Obstruction</b>? (3) | 1) Maternal polyhydramnios <br>2) Failure to pass meconium <br>3) Currant jelly stools
13. Within how many hours should <b style="color:red">meconium</b> normally be passed? | 24-48 hours
14. What are the common findings on plain X-rays of <b style="color:red">Intestinal Obstruction</b>? (3) | 1) Distended loops <br>2) Air-fluid levels <br>3) Paucity of colonic air
15. What does the <b style="color:red">Target sign</b> indicate on a CT scan? | Bowel wall thickening
16. Define the CT finding <b style="color:red">Pneumatosis intestinalis</b>. | Air in the bowel wall
17. What does the <b style="color:red">beak sign</b> indicate on a CT scan? | Distal narrowing
18. What are the priority treatments for <b style="color:red">Intestinal Obstruction</b> management? (3) | 1) Bowel decompression (NGT/OGT) <br>2) Fluid resuscitation <br>3) Electrolyte monitoring
19. Compare management for <b style="color:red">Partial vs. Complete Obstruction</b>. | Partial: Conservative trial. <br>Complete: Early surgery.

### Esophageal Atresia (EA) and Tracheoesophageal Fistula (TEF)

20. What is the pathogenesis of <b style="color:red">Esophageal Atresia</b>? | Failure of the esophagus to develop as a continuous passage.
21. What percentage of <b style="color:red">Esophageal Atresia</b> cases are associated with a fistula? | Over 90%
22. Enumerate the components of <b style="color:red">VACTERL syndrome</b>. (7) | Vertebral, Anorectal, Cardiac, Tracheal, Esophageal, Renal, Limb.
23. What is the most common classification of <b style="color:red">EA/TEF</b>? | Type C (86%)
24. Describe the anatomy of <b style="color:red">Type C EA/TEF</b>. | Upper esophagus is atretic; distal portion connects to the trachea.
25. What is <b style="color:red">Type E (H-type) EA/TEF</b>? | Fistula without atresia.
26. How does <b style="color:red">H-type TEF</b> typically present? | Chronic respiratory problems.
27. What are the classic clinical signs of <b style="color:red">EA/TEF</b> in neonates? | Frothing and bubbling at the mouth/nose.
28. List the respiratory symptoms associated with neonatal <b style="color:red">EA/TEF</b>. (3) | 1) Coughing <br>2) Cyanosis <br>3) Respiratory distress
29. What is the primary bedside diagnostic test for <b style="color:red">Esophageal Atresia</b>? | Failure to pass an NGT or OGT into the stomach.
30. What are the prenatal ultrasound findings for <b style="color:red">EA/TEF</b>? (2) | 1) Polyhydramnios <br>2) Absence of a stomach bubble
31. What does an X-ray show in a patient with <b style="color:red">Esophageal Atresia</b>? | Coiled feeding tube in the esophageal pouch.
32. What does an <b style="color:red">airless scaphoid abdomen</b> suggest in EA imaging? | Pure Esophageal Atresia
33. What does an <b style="color:red">air-distended stomach</b> indicate in EA imaging? | Coexisting Tracheoesophageal Fistula (TEF)
34. Why should <b style="color:red">mechanical ventilation</b> be avoided in EA/TEF management? | Positive pressure causes gastric distension or perforation via fistula.
35. What is the definitive treatment for <b style="color:red">EA/TEF</b>? | Primary Complete Repair (ligation and anastomosis).
36. When is <b style="color:red">Staged Repair</b> (gastrostomy first) indicated for EA/TEF? | High-risk infants.

### Hypertrophic Pyloric Stenosis (HPS)

37. Which demographic is most affected by <b style="color:red">Hypertrophic Pyloric Stenosis</b>? | White males.
38. Which medications are strongly associated with <b style="color:red">HPS</b> if used in the first 2 weeks of life? | Erythromycin or macrolides.
39. What is the pathogenesis of <b style="color:red">Hypertrophic Pyloric Stenosis</b>? | Hypertrophy of the pyloric muscle causing gastric outlet obstruction.
40. Describe the vomiting in <b style="color:red">Hypertrophic Pyloric Stenosis</b>. | Non-bilious, projectile, and postprandial.
41. At what age does vomiting typically start in <b style="color:red">HPS</b>? | After 3 weeks of age.
42. What is the hallmark physical exam finding for <b style="color:red">HPS</b>? | Olive-shaped mass (2cm) in the epigastrium.
43. When is the <b style="color:red">pyloric olive mass</b> most easily palpated? | After vomiting.
44. What visible sign may be seen on the abdomen of an <b style="color:red">HPS</b> patient? | Gastric peristaltic waves.
45. Describe the classic metabolic profile of <b style="color:red">Hypertrophic Pyloric Stenosis</b>. | Hypochloremic metabolic alkalosis.
46. What is <b style="color:red">Icteropyloric Syndrome</b>? | Unconjugated hyperbilirubinemia associated with HPS.
47. What is the sensitive diagnostic tool for <b style="color:red">HPS</b>? | Ultrasound (95% sensitivity).
48. What are the ultrasound criteria for <b style="color:red">Pyloric Stenosis</b>? | Thickness: 3-4mm; Length: 15-19mm.
49. List 3 contrast imaging signs of <b style="color:red">Pyloric Stenosis</b>. | 1) String sign <br>2) Shoulder sign <br>3) Double tract sign
50. What is the initial management priority for <b style="color:red">HPS</b>? | Fluid and electrolyte correction.
51. What is the surgical procedure of choice for <b style="color:red">HPS</b>? | Pyloromyotomy.

### Intestinal Atresia and Stenosis

52. What is the pathogenesis of <b style="color:red">Duodenal Atresia</b>? | Failed recanalization of the intestinal lumen.
53. During which weeks of gestation does <b style="color:red">duodenal recanalization</b> fail? | 6th-7th week.
54. <b style="color:red">Duodenal Atresia</b> is associated with which chromosomal abnormality? | Trisomy 21 (Down Syndrome).
55. What is the hallmark clinical presentation of <b style="color:red">Duodenal Atresia</b>? | Bilious vomiting WITHOUT abdominal distention.
56. What prenatal history is often present in <b style="color:red">Duodenal Atresia</b>? | Maternal polyhydramnios.
57. What is the classic X-ray sign for <b style="color:red">Duodenal Atresia</b>? | Double bubble sign.
58. What does the <b style="color:red">Double Bubble</b> represent? | Air in the stomach and proximal duodenum.
59. What is the surgical treatment for <b style="color:red">Duodenal Atresia</b>? | Duodenoduodenostomy.
60. What is the typical cause of <b style="color:red">Jejunoileal Atresia</b>? | Intrauterine vascular accidents.
61. Compare <b style="color:red">Jejunoileal</b> and <b style="color:red">Duodenal Atresia</b> regarding extraintestinal anomalies. | Jejunoileal atresia is not typically associated with extraintestinal anomalies.

### Hirschsprung’s Disease (HD)

62. What is the pathogenesis of <b style="color:red">Hirschsprung’s Disease</b>? | Failure of neuroblasts to migrate, resulting in absence of ganglion cells.
63. Which nerve plexuses are absent in <b style="color:red">Hirschsprung’s Disease</b>? | Meissner and Auerbach plexuses.
64. What is the functional result of the absence of ganglion cells in <b style="color:red">HD</b>? | Lack of bowel relaxation and functional obstruction.
65. What is the most common cause of <b style="color:red">lower GI obstruction</b> in neonates? | Hirschsprung’s Disease.
66. What is the Male-to-Female ratio in <b style="color:red">Hirschsprung’s Disease</b>? | 4:1.
67. Which gene and syndrome are strongly linked to <b style="color:red">Hirschsprung’s Disease</b>? | RET gene and Trisomy 21.
68. When should <b style="color:red">Hirschsprung’s Disease</b> be suspected in a full-term infant? | Delayed passage of meconium (>24-48 hours).
69. What physical exam finding occurs during a DRE in <b style="color:red">Hirschsprung’s Disease</b>? | Explosive stool passage.
70. What is the Gold Standard for diagnosing <b style="color:red">Hirschsprung’s Disease</b>? | Rectal Suction Biopsy.
71. What does histology show in <b style="color:red">Hirschsprung’s Disease</b> biopsy? | Absence of ganglion cells and hypertrophied nerve bundles.
72. What barium enema finding is highly suggestive of <b style="color:red">Hirschsprung’s Disease</b>? | Rectal:Sigmoid diameter ratio of ≤1 (Transition zone).
73. What is the most serious complication of <b style="color:red">Hirschsprung’s Disease</b>? | Enterocolitis (Hirschsprung’s-associated enterocolitis).
74. How does <b style="color:red">Hirschsprung’s Enterocolitis</b> present? (3) | Sepsis, fever, and explosive diarrhea.
75. What is the definitive surgical treatment for <b style="color:red">Hirschsprung’s Disease</b>? | Primary pull-through procedure (ex: Soave).
76. When is <b style="color:red">staged surgery</b> with an ostomy used for HD? | If enterocolitis is present.

### Functional Obstruction: Ileus

77. Define <b style="color:red">Functional Obstruction (Ileus)</b>. | Failure of intestinal peristalsis without a physical blockage.
78. How long does post-abdominal surgery <b style="color:red">Ileus</b> typically take to resolve? | Within 72 hours.
79. List 3 metabolic causes of <b style="color:red">Ileus</b>. | 1) Hypokalemia <br>2) Hypercalcemia <br>3) Acidosis.
80. Which medications can induce <b style="color:red">Ileus</b>? (3) | Opiates, vincristine, and antimotility agents (loperamide).
81. What are the clinical signs of <b style="color:red">Ileus</b>? (4) | 1) Abdominal distention <br>2) Nausea <br>3) Vomiting <br>4) Minimal/absent bowel sounds.
82. What does an X-ray show in <b style="color:red">Ileus</b> compared to mechanical obstruction? | Air-fluid levels without distinct distended loops.
83. What are the management focuses for <b style="color:red">Ileus</b>? (3) | 1) Correct underlying abnormalities <br>2) Stop offending drugs <br>3) Nasogastric decompression.

### Mechanical Obstruction: Intussusception

84. Define <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b>. | Invagination (telescoping) of one bowel segment into another.
85. Define <b style="color:red">Intussusceptum</b> vs <b style="color:red">Intussuscipiens</b>. | Intussusceptum: Inner segment that telescopes. <br>Intussuscipiens: Outer segment receiving it.
86. What is the most common type of <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b>? | Ileocolic.
87. What is the peak age range for <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b>? | 5 months to 3 years.
88. What is the most common abdominal emergency in children under 2? | Intussusception.
89. What is the idiopathic cause of <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b> linked to? | Hypertrophied Peyer's patches.
90. Name 3 potential lead points for <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b> in children >2 years. | 1) Meckel diverticulum <br>2) Polyps <br>3) Lymphoma.
91. What is the classic clinical triad of <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b>? | Paroxysmal colicky pain, currant jelly stools, and a sausage-shaped mass.
92. Where is the <b style="color:red">sausage-shaped mass</b> typically palpated in intussusception? | Right Upper Quadrant (RUQ).
93. Describe <b style="color:red">currant jelly stools</b>. | Blood mixed with mucus.
94. What are the ultrasound signs of <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b>? (2) | 1) Target sign <br>2) Pseudo-kidney sign.
95. What barium enema sign is characteristic of <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b>? | Coiled spring sign.
96. What is the primary conservative treatment for <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b>? | Radiologic hydrostatic reduction (fluoroscopic or U/S guided).
97. List 4 indications for <b style="color:red">surgery</b> in intussusception. | 1) Shock <br>2) Peritonitis <br>3) Bowel necrosis <br>4) Multiple recurrences.

### Meckel Diverticulum

98. What is the most common congenital <b style="color:red">GI anomaly</b>? | Meckel Diverticulum.
99. What is the pathogenesis of <b style="color:red">Meckel Diverticulum</b>? | Incomplete obliteration of the omphalomesenteric duct.
100. Enumerate the <b style="color:red">Meckel Rule of 2s</b>. (5) | 1) 2% of population <br>2) Symptomatic by age 2 <br>3) 2 inches long <br>4) 2 feet from ileocecal valve <br>5) 2 ectopic tissues.
101. What are the 2 types of ectopic tissue found in <b style="color:red">Meckel Diverticulum</b>? | Gastric and pancreatic.
102. Describe the classic clinical presentation of <b style="color:red">Meckel Diverticulum</b>. | Painless rectal bleeding.
103. How is the stool described in <b style="color:red">Meckel Diverticulum</b>? | Brick-colored or maroon.
104. Which diagnostic test detects ectopic gastric mucosa in <b style="color:red">Meckel Diverticulum</b>? | Meckel radionuclide scan (Technetium-99m).
105. What is the standard surgical treatment for <b style="color:red">Meckel Diverticulum</b>? | Diverticulectomy.

### Inflammatory Bowel Disease (IBD)

106. Compare the distribution of <b style="color:red">Crohn's Disease vs. Ulcerative Colitis</b>. | Crohn's: Entire tract (mouth to anus) with skip lesions. <br>UC: Continuous and limited to colon/rectum.
107. Compare the depth of inflammation in <b style="color:red">Crohn's vs. UC</b>. | Crohn's: Transmural. <br>UC: Mucosa only.
108. What is the hallmark symptom of <b style="color:red">Ulcerative Colitis</b>? | Rectal bleeding.
109. List 3 classic symptoms of <b style="color:red">Crohn's Disease</b>. | 1) Abdominal pain <br>2) RLQ mass <br>3) Perianal lesions.
110. In which type of <b style="color:red">IBD</b> is growth failure more common? | Crohn's Disease.
111. What histological finding is pathognomonic for <b style="color:red">Crohn's Disease</b>? | Noncaseating granulomas.
112. What histological findings are seen in <b style="color:red">Ulcerative Colitis</b>? | Crypt abscesses and distortion.
113. List 2 extraintestinal manifestations specific to <b style="color:red">Ulcerative Colitis</b>. | 1) Pyoderma gangrenosum <br>2) Primary sclerosing cholangitis.
114. What stool marker is used for <b style="color:red">IBD</b> inflammation? | Fecal calprotectin.
115. What is required for definitive confirmation of <b style="color:red">IBD</b> diagnosis? | Colonoscopy with biopsy.
116. How is <b style="color:red">mild UC</b> treated? | Aminosalicylates.
117. What medication is used to induce <b style="color:red">IBD remission</b>? | Corticosteroids.
118. Which biologic is used for severe <b style="color:red">IBD</b> cases? | Infliximab.
119. What is <b style="color:red">Exclusive Enteral Nutrition (EEN)</b> used for? | Primary dietary therapy for pediatric Crohn's Disease.

### Celiac Disease and Malabsorption

120. Define <b style="color:red">Celiac Disease</b> pathogenesis. | Immune-mediated disorder triggered by gluten in genetically susceptible individuals (HLA-DQ2.5/DQ8).
121. Name 3 grains containing <b style="color:red">gluten</b>. | Wheat, rye, barley.
122. What is the most common extraintestinal manifestation of <b style="color:red">Celiac Disease</b>? | Iron deficiency anemia.
123. What is the first-line serological test for <b style="color:red">Celiac Disease</b>? | Anti-TG2 IgA.
124. Why must total IgA be checked during <b style="color:red">Celiac</b> screening? | Deficiency can mask a negative anti-TG2 result.
125. What does the histology of <b style="color:red">Celiac Disease</b> show? (3) | 1) Villous atrophy <br>2) Crypt hyperplasia <br>3) Increased lymphocytes.
126. What causes steatorrhea and B12 deficiency in <b style="color:red">SIBO</b>? | Bacterial colonization causing bile salt deconjugation.
127. How is <b style="color:red">Bacterial Overgrowth (SIBO)</b> treated? | Metronidazole.
128. Contrast <b style="color:red">Primary vs. Secondary Lactase Deficiency</b>. | Primary: Physiologic decline. <br>Secondary: Transient post-infection damage.
129. How is <b style="color:red">Lactase Deficiency</b> primarily diagnosed? | Dietary elimination trial or H2 breath test.
130. Define <b style="color:red">Short Bowel Syndrome</b>. | Loss of >50% of the small bowel.

### High-Yield Comparisons for Exams

131. Compare vomiting color: <b style="color:red">Pyloric Stenosis</b> vs. <b style="color:red">Duodenal Atresia</b>. | Pyloric Stenosis: Non-bilious. <br>Duodenal Atresia: Bilious.
132. Compare abdominal distention: <b style="color:red">Pyloric Stenosis</b> vs. <b style="color:red">Hirschsprung's</b>. | Pyloric Stenosis: No/minimal. <br>Hirschsprung's: Prominent.
133. Compare stool type: <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b> vs. <b style="color:red">Meckel Diverticulum</b>. | Intussusception: Currant jelly. <br>Meckel: Maroon/brick.
134. What stool type is seen in <b style="color:red">Ulcerative Colitis</b>? | Gross bloody mucus stools.
135. Compare X-ray signs: <b style="color:red">Duodenal Atresia</b> vs. <b style="color:red">Pyloric Stenosis</b>. | Duodenal Atresia: Double Bubble. <br>Pyloric Stenosis: String Sign.
136. What is the physical mass description for <b style="color:red">Pyloric Stenosis</b>? | Olive mass.
137. What is the physical mass description for <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b>? | Sausage mass.
138. Compare bowel sounds: <b style="color:red">Mechanical Obstruction</b> vs. <b style="color:red">Ileus</b>. | Mechanical: Hyperactive initially. <br>Ileus: Absent/minimal.
139. Compare <b style="color:red">Celiac vs. Lactose Intolerance</b> on biopsy. | Celiac: Villous atrophy. <br>Lactose Intolerance: No mucosal damage.
140. Compare <b style="color:red">Crohn's vs. UC</b> regarding complication risk. | Crohn's: Transmural (fistulas). <br>UC: Mucosal only.
141. What is the <b style="color:red">Meconium</b> alarm sign for <b style="color:red">Hirschsprung's Disease</b>? | Delayed passage in a full-term infant.
142. Compare growth failure in <b style="color:red">Crohn's vs. UC</b>. | Prominent in Crohn's; rare in UC.
143. Compare anemia type: <b style="color:red">Celiac</b> vs. <b style="color:red">SIBO</b>. | Celiac: Iron deficiency. <br>SIBO: B12 deficiency.
144. Compare EA/TEF abdomen: <b style="color:red">Pure Atresia</b> vs. <b style="color:red">Atresia + TEF</b>. | Pure atresia: Scaphoid (no air). <br>Atresia + TEF: Gas-distended.
145. Compare distribution: <b style="color:red">Crohn's</b> vs. <b style="color:red">Ulcerative Colitis</b>. | Crohn's: Skip lesions. <br>UC: Continuous from the rectum.
146. Compare age of diagnosis for <b style="color:red">HPS</b> vs. <b style="color:red">Intussusception</b>. | HPS: 3-6 weeks. <br>Intussusception: 5 months - 3 years.
147. When is <b style="color:red">IBD</b> commonly diagnosed? | Late adolescence.


# 9

## Summary
text
### Neonatal Cholestasis: General Principles

| Aspect | Neonatal Cholestasis Description |
| :--- | :--- |
| <b style="color:red">Definition</b> | Reduction in bile formation or excretion resulting in jaundice during the first 28 days of life. |
| <b style="color:red">Biochemical Criteria</b> | Direct Bilirubin <b style="color:red">>1.0 mg/dL</b> if Total Bilirubin (TB) is <b style="color:red">≤5 mg/dL</b>; OR Direct Bilirubin <b style="color:red">≥20%</b> of TB if TB is <b style="color:red">>5 mg/dL</b>. |
| <b style="color:red">Typical Features</b> | Icterus (scleral discoloration), highly staining urine (dark yellow), acholic stools (pale, white, or light yellow), and hepatomegaly. |
| <b style="color:red">Common Intrahepatic Causes</b> | Idiopathic neonatal hepatitis, TORCH infections, metabolic diseases (galactosemia, tyrosinemia), and prolonged TPN (>2 weeks). |
| <b style="color:red">Common Extrahepatic Causes</b> | Biliary Atresia and Choledochal cyst. |
| <b style="color:red">Malabsorption Complications</b> | Failure of bile delivery leads to malabsorption of fats and fat-soluble vitamins, causing malnutrition, growth retardation, and steatorrhea. |
| <b style="color:red">Portal Hypertension Features</b> | Manifests as hypersplenism, ascites (due to hypoalbuminemia), and varices which may cause hematemesis or hematochezia. |

* In <b style="color:red">Neonatal Cholestasis</b>, the first and most important laboratory test is bilirubin fractionation to determine if the elevation is direct (conjugated) or indirect.
* <b style="color:red">Icteropyloric Syndrome</b> in the source text context usually refers to unconjugated hyperbilirubinemia, but in <b style="color:red">Neonatal Cholestasis</b>, the focus is always on the conjugated fraction.
* <b style="color:red">Parenteral Nutrition-Associated Cholestasis (PNAC)</b> occurs in 1/5 of neonates receiving PN for <b style="color:red">>2 weeks</b>; risk factors include prematurity and sepsis.
* <b style="color:red">Ursodeoxycholic acid</b> is used in <b style="color:red">Neonatal Cholestasis</b> to increase bile secretion, but it is ineffective if there is a physical obstruction (e.g., Biliary Atresia).
* <b style="color:red">Galactosemia and Tyrosinemia</b>, which cause cholestasis, are detectable via the <b style="color:red">Philippine Expanded Newborn Screening</b> program.

### Major Extrahepatic Causes of Cholestasis

| Feature | Biliary Atresia | Choledochal Cyst |
| :--- | :--- | :--- |
| <b style="color:red">Pathogenesis</b> | Progressive fibroinflammatory obliteration of the extrahepatic biliary tree. | Congenital dilation of the biliary tree without initial obstruction. |
| <b style="color:red">Epidemiology</b> | <b style="color:red">Most common and severe</b> cause of NC; common in East Asia. | 2-3% of NC cases; significant female predominance. |
| <b style="color:red">Clinical Presentation</b> | Normal at birth, then jaundice persists <b style="color:red">>2 weeks</b>. | Classic Triad (Older children): <b style="color:red">Abdominal pain, jaundice, and palpable mass</b>. |
| <b style="color:red">Associated Anomalies</b> | Polysplenia, situs inversus, or cardiac anomalies. | Complicated by cholangitis, pancreatitis, and malignant transformation. |
| <b style="color:red">Diagnostic Sign</b> | Liver biopsy: <b style="color:red">Bile duct proliferation</b> and perilobular fibrosis with intact architecture. | Ultrasound or MRCP showing cyst; <b style="color:red">Type I</b> is the most common (70-90%). |
| <b style="color:red">Management</b> | <b style="color:red">Kasai hepatoportoenterostomy</b> (best before 8 weeks); eventually requires liver transplant. | <b style="color:red">Primary excision of the cyst</b> with Roux-en-Y choledochojejunostomy. |

* <b style="color:red">Inspissated Bile Syndrome</b> is a rare cause of cholestasis where bile plugs or sludge obstruct the ducts, often linked to hemolysis or drugs like <b style="color:red">Ceftriaxone</b>.
* In <b style="color:red">Biliary Atresia</b>, the <b style="color:red">Kasai Procedure</b> has a 90% success rate if performed before the 8th week of life.
* <b style="color:red">Choledochal Cyst</b> in infants presents with cholestatic jaundice and severe liver dysfunction (ascites, coagulopathy).

### Idiopathic Neonatal Hepatitis (INH)

* <b style="color:red">Idiopathic Neonatal Hepatitis (INH)</b>, also known as <b style="color:red">Giant Cell Hepatitis</b>, is a diagnosis of exclusion accounting for 13-30% of NC cases.
* The histologic hallmark of <b style="color:red">Idiopathic Neonatal Hepatitis</b> is <b style="color:red">Giant cell transformation</b> and lobular disarray.
* <b style="color:red">Idiopathic Neonatal Hepatitis</b> can be sporadic or familial; the familial form often suggests an underlying genetic or metabolic aberration.

### Viral Hepatitis Basics

| Virus | Type | Route | Incubation | Chronic Risk |
| :--- | :--- | :--- | :--- | :--- |
| <b style="color:red">Hepatitis A (HAV)</b> | RNA (Picornavirus) | Fecal-oral | <b style="color:red">15-49 days (Shortest)</b> | No |
| <b style="color:red">Hepatitis B (HBV)</b> | dsDNA (Hepadnaviridae) | Perinatal, Blood | Average 3 months | Yes (High in neonates) |
| <b style="color:red">Hepatitis C (HCV)</b> | RNA | Perinatal, Blood | Variable | Yes |

* <b style="color:red">Viral Hepatitis</b> pathogenesis is usually immune-mediated (especially HBV), with necrosis most marked in <b style="color:red">centrilobular</b> areas.
* <b style="color:red">Hepatitis C (HCV)</b> is unique among hepatotropic viruses because <b style="color:red">fatty exchange (steatosis)</b> is a common histologic finding.
* In <b style="color:red">Acute Viral Hepatitis</b>, the liver morphology typically returns to normal within <b style="color:red">3 months</b> of infection.
* <b style="color:red">Hepatitis A</b> period of communicability is <b style="color:red">2 weeks before to 7 days after</b> the onset of jaundice.
* <b style="color:red">Hepatitis A</b> diagnosis is confirmed by <b style="color:red">Anti-HAV IgM</b>, which is detectable when symptoms appear and remains for 4-6 months.
* <b style="color:red">Hepatitis A Vaccination</b> is given as a 2-dose series starting at <b style="color:red">12 months</b> of age, with at least a 6-month interval.

### Hepatitis B (HBV) Management

* The most important risk factor for acquiring <b style="color:red">Hepatitis B</b> in children is <b style="color:red">perinatal exposure</b>; infants of HBeAg-positive mothers have a 90% risk of chronic infection if untreated.
* The <b style="color:red">first biochemical evidence</b> of HBV infection is an elevation of <b style="color:red">ALT</b> levels, occurring 6-7 weeks after exposure.
* <b style="color:red">HBsAg</b> (Surface Antigen) presence indicates <b style="color:red">active infection</b> (acute or chronic).
* <b style="color:red">Anti-HBs</b> (Surface Antibody) indicate <b style="color:red">immunity</b> via either resolved infection or vaccination.
* <b style="color:red">IgM anti-HBc</b> (Core Antibody) is the hallmark marker for <b style="color:red">recent or acute HBV infection</b>.
* <b style="color:red">HBeAg</b> (e-Antigen) correlates with <b style="color:red">active viral replication and high infectivity</b>.
* For newborns weight <b style="color:red">≥2kg</b> born to HBsAg(+) mothers, administer both <b style="color:red">HBV vaccine and HBIG</b> within 12 hours of life.
* If a newborn is <b style="color:red">&lt2kg</b>, the birth dose of HBV vaccine does <b style="color:red">NOT</b> count toward the 3-dose series; 3 additional doses are required.

### Liver Abscess

* <b style="color:red">Staphylococcus aureus</b> is the leading single pathogenic agent for <b style="color:red">Liver Abscess</b> in children without underlying intestinal disease.
* The most common routes for <b style="color:red">Liver Abscess</b> include the <b style="color:red">portal vein</b> (from omphalitis or appendicitis) and the <b style="color:red">hepatic artery</b> (from sepsis).
* <b style="color:red">Liver Abscess</b> imaging: Ultrasound/CT is preferred; 75% are located in the <b style="color:red">right lobe</b> and 70% are solitary.
* <b style="color:red">Liver Abscess</b> treatment involves 2-3 weeks of <b style="color:red">IV antibiotics</b> followed by oral therapy to complete a 4-6 week course.
* <b style="color:red">Amoebic Liver Abscess</b> requires treatment with <b style="color:red">Metronidazole plus Paromomycin</b>.

### Cholelithiasis (Gallstones)

* <b style="color:red">Pigment stones</b> account for 70% of pediatric gallstones, while cholesterol stones comprise 15-20%.
* <b style="color:red">Biliary Pseudolithiasis</b> is a unique condition caused by <b style="color:red">high-dose Ceftriaxone</b> use (>10 days) resulting in calcium-ceftriaxone salt precipitates.
* <b style="color:red">Biliary Pseudolithiasis</b> typically <b style="color:red">resolves spontaneously</b> within weeks to months after discontinuing Ceftriaxone.
* The hallmark clinical feature of <b style="color:red">Cholelithiasis</b> is <b style="color:red">recurrent abdominal colicky pain</b> in the Right Upper Quadrant (RUQ).
* <b style="color:red">Laparoscopic Cholecystectomy</b> is the treatment of choice for symptomatic children with gallstones.

### High-Yield Comparisons for Differentiation

1. <b style="color:red">Biliary Atresia vs. Choledochal Cyst (Infant Presentation):</b> Both present with NC, but Biliary Atresia is characterized by a "disappearing" or obliterated biliary tree, while Choledochal Cyst shows a distinct dilation on ultrasound.
2. <b style="color:red">Biliary Atresia vs. Idiopathic Neonatal Hepatitis (INH) on Biopsy:</b> Biliary Atresia shows <b style="color:red">bile duct proliferation</b>; INH shows <b style="color:red">giant cell transformation</b> and lobular disarray.
3. <b style="color:red">Kasai Procedure vs. Liver Transplant:</b> Kasai is a <b style="color:red">temporizing measure</b> to slow progression to cirrhosis and sustain growth; Liver Transplant is the <b style="color:red">definitive</b> treatment for Biliary Atresia.
4. <b style="color:red">Hepatitis A vs. Hepatitis B transmission:</b> HAV is <b style="color:red">fecal-oral</b> (associated with hygiene and food); HBV is <b style="color:red">parenteral/perinatal</b> (associated with body fluids).
5. <b style="color:red">Gilbert Syndrome vs. Crigler-Najjar Type 1:</b> Both involve UDPGT, but Gilbert is <b style="color:red">impaired function</b> (mild, stress-induced), whereas Crigler-Najjar Type 1 is a <b style="color:red">complete absence</b> of the enzyme (severe).
6. <b style="color:red">HBeAg vs. Anti-HBe:</b> HBeAg indicates <b style="color:red">active replication</b> and high contagiousness; Anti-HBe (seroconversion) signals that active replication has <b style="color:red">ceased</b>.
7. <b style="color:red">HBsAg vs. Anti-HBs:</b> HBsAg means you <b style="color:red">HAVE</b> the virus (infection); Anti-HBs means you are <b style="color:red">PROTECTED</b> from the virus (immunity).
8. <b style="color:red">Biliary Atresia vs. Choledochal Cyst Mass:</b> A palpable RUQ mass is part of the classic triad for <b style="color:red">Choledochal Cyst</b> but is generally not a feature of Biliary Atresia.
9. <b style="color:red">Staphylococcus aureus vs. Entamoeba histolytica Abscess:</b> S. aureus is the most common <b style="color:red">pyogenic</b> cause; E. histolytica is the <b style="color:red">amoebic</b> cause requiring Metronidazole + Paromomycin.
10. <b style="color:red">Dubin-Johnson vs. Rotor Syndrome:</b> Both are causes of <b style="color:red">conjugated</b> hyperbilirubinemia in older children (along with Wilson's and Alpha-1 antitrypsin deficiency).
11. <b style="color:red">Pre-exposure vs. Post-exposure HAV prophylaxis:</b> HAV vaccine is preferred for <b style="color:red">both</b> in healthy persons if the exposure was within the last 2 weeks.
12. <b style="color:red">Pigment vs. Cholesterol Stones:</b> In children, <b style="color:red">pigment stones</b> are much more common (70%) compared to adults where cholesterol stones predominate.
13. <b style="color:red">Hepatitis C vs. others on histology:</b> Think <b style="color:red">Fat/Steatosis</b> for Hepatitis C; think <b style="color:red">Giant cells</b> for neonatal viral infections.
14. <b style="color:red">Ascites vs. Coagulopathy in NC:</b> In cholestasis, <b style="color:red">Ascites</b> is usually due to low albumin (synthetic failure), while <b style="color:red">Coagulopathy</b> is due to Vitamin K malabsorption (obstructive/cholestatic failure).

text
### Neonatal Cholestasis: General Principles

1. Define <b style="color:red">Neonatal Cholestasis</b> based on the period of occurrence. | First 28 days of life
2. What is the <b style="color:red">Definition</b> of Neonatal Cholestasis regarding bile? | Reduction in bile formation or excretion
3. Biochemical Criteria: <b style="color:red">Neonatal Cholestasis</b> when Total Bilirubin (TB) is ≤5 mg/dL. | Direct Bilirubin >1.0 mg/dL
4. Biochemical Criteria: <b style="color:red">Neonatal Cholestasis</b> when Total Bilirubin (TB) is >5 mg/dL. | Direct Bilirubin ≥20% of TB
5. Describe the urine in <b style="color:red">Neonatal Cholestasis</b>. | Highly staining (dark yellow)
6. Describe the stools in <b style="color:red">Neonatal Cholestasis</b>. | Acholic (pale, white, or light yellow)
7. What physical finding regarding the liver is a typical feature of <b style="color:red">Neonatal Cholestasis</b>? | Hepatomegaly
8. What is the medical term for scleral discoloration in <b style="color:red">Neonatal Cholestasis</b>? | Icterus
9. List the common intrahepatic causes (4) of <b style="color:red">Neonatal Cholestasis</b>. | 1) Idiopathic neonatal hepatitis <br>2) TORCH infections <br>3) Metabolic diseases <br>4) Prolonged TPN
10. Identify the metabolic diseases (2) causing intrahepatic <b style="color:red">Neonatal Cholestasis</b>. | Galactosemia and Tyrosinemia
11. What duration of Total Parenteral Nutrition (TPN) is a common cause of <b style="color:red">Neonatal Cholestasis</b>? | >2 weeks
12. List the common extrahepatic causes (2) of <b style="color:red">Neonatal Cholestasis</b>. | Biliary Atresia and Choledochal cyst
13. What complication results from the failure of bile delivery in <b style="color:red">Neonatal Cholestasis</b>? | Malabsorption
14. Malabsorption in <b style="color:red">Neonatal Cholestasis</b> specifically affects which substances (2)? | Fats and fat-soluble vitamins
15. What are the clinical consequences of fat malabsorption in <b style="color:red">Neonatal Cholestasis</b> (3)? | 1) Malnutrition <br>2) Growth retardation <br>3) Steatorrhea
16. How does <b style="color:red">Portal Hypertension</b> manifest in the context of Neonatal Cholestasis (3)? | 1) Hypersplenism <br>2) Ascites <br>3) Varices
17. What is the cause of ascites in <b style="color:red">Neonatal Cholestasis</b> with portal hypertension? | Hypoalbuminemia
18. What are the clinical signs of varices in <b style="color:red">Neonatal Cholestasis</b> (2)? | Hematemesis or hematochezia
19. What is the first and most important laboratory test for <b style="color:red">Neonatal Cholestasis</b>? | Bilirubin fractionation
20. Why is bilirubin fractionation performed in <b style="color:red">Neonatal Cholestasis</b>? | Determine if elevation is direct or indirect
21. What fraction of bilirubin is the focus in <b style="color:red">Neonatal Cholestasis</b>? | Conjugated fraction
22. <b style="color:red">Icteropyloric Syndrome</b> usually refers to which type of hyperbilirubinemia? | Unconjugated hyperbilirubinemia
23. What is the incidence of <b style="color:red">Parenteral Nutrition-Associated Cholestasis (PNAC)</b> in neonates on PN for >2 weeks? | 1/5 of neonates
24. List risk factors (2) for <b style="color:red">Parenteral Nutrition-Associated Cholestasis (PNAC)</b>. | Prematurity and sepsis
25. What is the function of <b style="color:red">Ursodeoxycholic acid</b> in Neonatal Cholestasis? | Increase bile secretion
26. When is <b style="color:red">Ursodeoxycholic acid</b> ineffective in treating cholestasis? | Physical obstruction (Biliary Atresia)
27. How are Galactosemia and Tyrosinemia detected in the <b style="color:red">Philippines</b>? | Philippine Expanded Newborn Screening

28. Define the pathogenesis of <b style="color:red">Biliary Atresia</b>. | Progressive fibroinflammatory obliteration

48. What causes duct obstruction in <b style="color:red">Inspissated Bile Syndrome</b>? | Bile plugs or sludge

51. What is the success rate of the <b style="color:red">Kasai Procedure</b> if performed before the 8th week? | 90% success rate
52. How does <b style="color:red">Choledochal Cyst</b> present specifically in infants? | Cholestatic jaundice and liver dysfunction
53. Name signs of severe liver dysfunction (2) in infants with <b style="color:red">Choledochal Cyst</b>. | Ascites and coagulopathy

### Idiopathic Neonatal Hepatitis (INH)

54. What is the alternative name for <b style="color:red">Idiopathic Neonatal Hepatitis</b>? | Giant Cell Hepatitis
55. What percentage of Neonatal Cholestasis cases are attributed to <b style="color:red">Idiopathic Neonatal Hepatitis</b>? | 13-30% of cases

### Viral Hepatitis Basics

63. Is there a chronic risk for <b style="color:red">Hepatitis A</b>? | No
64. Identify the virus type and family for <b style="color:red">Hepatitis B (HBV)</b>. | dsDNA (Hepadnaviridae)
65. What are the transmission routes (2) for <b style="color:red">Hepatitis B</b>? | Perinatal and Blood
66. What is the average incubation period for <b style="color:red">Hepatitis B</b>? | 3 months
67. Is there a chronic risk for <b style="color:red">Hepatitis B</b>? | Yes (High in neonates)
68. What is the virus type for <b style="color:red">Hepatitis C (HCV)</b>? | RNA
69. What are the transmission routes (2) for <b style="color:red">Hepatitis C</b>? | Perinatal and Blood
70. Is there a chronic risk for <b style="color:red">Hepatitis C</b>? | Yes
71. What is the usual pathogenesis of <b style="color:red">Viral Hepatitis</b> damage? | Immune-mediated

### Hepatitis B (HBV) Management

88. What does <b style="color:red">HBeAg</b> correlate with? | Active viral replication

### Cholelithiasis (Gallstones)

100. Percentage of <b style="color:red">Pigment stones</b> in pediatric gallstones? | 70% of cases
101. Percentage of <b style="color:red">Cholesterol stones</b> in pediatric gallstones? | 15-20% of cases
102. Cause of <b style="color:red">Biliary Pseudolithiasis</b>? | High-dose Ceftriaxone (>10 days)
103. Composition of <b style="color:red">Biliary Pseudolithiasis</b> precipitates? | Calcium-ceftriaxone salt

### High-Yield Comparisons

107. <b style="color:red">Biliary Atresia vs. Choledochal Cyst</b> ultrasound finding? | BA: Obliterated tree; CC: Distinct dilation
108. <b style="color:red">Biliary Atresia vs. INH</b> on biopsy? | BA: Duct proliferation; INH: Giant cells
109. <b style="color:red">Kasai vs. Liver Transplant</b> role? | Kasai: Temporizing; Transplant: Definitive
110. <b style="color:red">HAV vs. HBV</b> transmission? | HAV: Fecal-oral; HBV: Parenteral/Perinatal
111. <b style="color:red">Gilbert vs. Crigler-Najjar Type 1</b> UDPGT status? | Gilbert: Impaired; Crigler-Najjar 1: Absent
112. <b style="color:red">HBeAg vs. Anti-HBe</b> replication status? | HBeAg: Active; Anti-HBe: Ceased
113. <b style="color:red">HBsAg vs. Anti-HBs</b> status? | HBsAg: Infection; Anti-HBs: Protected
114. Presence of RUQ mass: <b style="color:red">Biliary Atresia vs. Choledochal Cyst</b>? | Choledochal Cyst (BA has none)
115. <b style="color:red">S. aureus vs. E. histolytica</b> abscess type? | S. aureus: Pyogenic; E. histolytica: Amoebic
116. <b style="color:red">Dubin-Johnson and Rotor</b> are causes of what hyperbilirubinemia? | Conjugated hyperbilirubinemia
117. <b style="color:red">HAV prophylaxis</b> (Pre and Post exposure) for healthy persons? | HAV vaccine (within 2 weeks)
118. <b style="color:red">Pediatric vs. Adult</b> gallstone predominance? | Pediatric: Pigment; Adult: Cholesterol
119. <b style="color:red">Hepatitis C vs. Neonatal viral</b> histology? | HCV: Steatosis; Neonatal: Giant cells
120. <b style="color:red">Ascites vs. Coagulopathy</b> etiology in NC? | Ascites: Synthetic failure; Coagulopathy: Malabsorption


# Cardio 10 - Heart Failure 

## Summary

### Definitions and Clinical Overview of Pediatric Heart Failure

* <b style="color:red">Pediatric Heart Failure</b> is a clinical and pathophysiologic syndrome resulting from ventricular dysfunction, volume overload, or pressure overload, occurring alone or in combination.
* A <b style="color:red">Structural or Functional Impairment</b> of ventricular filling or blood ejection characterizes the complex syndrome of heart failure.
* The <b style="color:red">Primary Work of an Infant</b> is feeding; therefore, heart failure in infants often manifests as interrupted feeding sessions or "suck-rest-suck" cycles.
* <b style="color:red">Poor Growth (Failure to Thrive)</b> is a key symptom of heart failure often discovered late in children aged 3 to 5 years.
* <b style="color:red">Exercise Intolerance and Fatigue</b> are the clinical benchmarks for heart failure in older children, whereas feeding tolerance is the benchmark for infants.
* <b style="color:red">Respiratory Distress</b> in pediatric heart failure manifests as tachypnea, subcostal retractions, or intercostal retractions, often leading to recurrent respiratory tract infections.
* <b style="color:red">Cardiac Output (CO)</b> is calculated by the formula: <i style="color:blue">Stroke Volume (SV) × Heart Rate (HR)</i>.
* <i style="color:blue">Systolic Dysfunction</i> refers specifically to disorders in ventricular <b style="color:red">ejection</b> (contraction).
* <i style="color:blue">Diastolic Dysfunction</i> refers specifically to disorders in ventricular <b style="color:red">filling</b> (relaxation).

### Classification Systems for Heart Failure

| Classification System | Categories | Clinical Features/Description |
| :--- | :--- | :--- |
| <b style="color:red">Ross Classification</b> (Pediatric) | Class I to IV | I: No symptoms; II: Symptoms with vigorous activity; III: Symptoms with routine activity; IV: Symptoms at rest. |
| <b style="color:red">ACCF/AHA Stage A</b> | High Risk | Patients at high risk for HF (e.g., asymptomatic Dengue with bradycardia) but without structural disease. |
| <b style="color:red">ACCF/AHA Stage B</b> | Pre-Heart Failure | Structural heart disease (e.g., stable VSD) present but no current or previous symptoms. |
| <b style="color:red">ACCF/AHA Stage C</b> | Symptomatic HF | Structural heart disease with current or previous symptoms (e.g., activity intolerance). |
| <b style="color:red">ACCF/AHA Stage D</b> | Advanced HF | Refractory symptoms requiring intensive interventions like continuous IV inotropes. |

### Hemodynamics and Systemic Transport

* <b style="color:red">Preload (Ventricular Filling Volume)</b> is directly proportional to stroke volume; as venous return increases, the heart fills more and produces a greater stroke volume (within physiological limits).
* The <b style="color:red">Frank-Starling Principle</b> states that as the Left Ventricular End Diastolic Volume (LVEDV) increases, the heart increases cardiac output until a maximum plateau is reached.
* <b style="color:red">Afterload (Peripheral Resistance)</b> is the resistance/tension against which the heart must pump; it is inversely proportional to stroke volume and cardiac output.
* <b style="color:red">Ventricular Compliance</b> refers to the elasticity of the heart; higher compliance allows the heart to accommodate more volume, thereby increasing potential stroke volume.
* <b style="color:red">Systemic Oxygen Transport</b> is determined by the product of cardiac output and oxygen content.
* In a <b style="color:red">Failing Heart</b>, increasing diastolic pressure or volume cannot produce a cardiac output similar to a normal heart, and excessive fluids may cause congestion.

### Adaptive Mechanisms in Heart Failure

* <b style="color:red">Myocardial Hypertrophy</b> is an adaptive response to maintain systolic emptying, but it can eventually lead to <i style="color:blue">diastolic dysfunction</i> due to the thickened wall.
* <b style="color:red">Adrenergic Stimulation (SNS)</b> increases heart rate and vascular tone via catecholamines (Epinephrine and Norepinephrine) as a compensatory mechanism.
* <b style="color:red">Renin-Angiotensin-Aldosterone System (RAAS)</b> activation aims to maintain blood pressure but contributes to long-term cardiac stress.
* <b style="color:red">Atrial Natriuretic Peptides (ANP) and BNP</b> are released to promote urinary loss of sodium/water and act as vasodilators to counteract heart failure.
* An <b style="color:red">Increase in 2,3-DPG</b> occurs in heart failure to shift the oxygen-hemoglobin dissociation curve to the right, facilitating oxygen delivery to tissues.

### Clinical Manifestations by Age and Side

* <b style="color:red">Infant-Specific Symptoms</b> of heart failure include "cold sweats" (due to SNS activation), poor feeding, ashen color, and poor weight gain.
* <b style="color:red">Left-Sided Failure (Pulmonary Congestion)</b> presents with tachypnea, orthopnea (difficulty breathing when lying flat), nocturnal dyspnea, and wheezing.
* <b style="color:red">Right-Sided Failure (Systemic Congestion)</b> presents with hepatomegaly (liver congestion), puffy eyelids, distended neck veins, and bipedal/ankle edema.
* <b style="color:red">Abdominal Symptoms</b> such as nausea, anorexia, and abdominal pain in heart failure children are typically due to liver congestion.

### Diagnostics for Pediatric Heart Failure

* <b style="color:red">Chest X-ray (CXR)</b> is used to detect cardiomegaly and assess pulmonary vascular patterns.
* <b style="color:red">Electrocardiogram (ECG)</b> is the best tool to detect rhythm disturbances that may be the primary cause of heart failure.
* <b style="color:red">Echocardiography (Echo)</b> is the standard clinical technique for assessing specific ventricular function and structure.
* <b style="color:red">B-type Natriuretic Peptide (BNP)</b> is a cardiac neurohormone released during increased ventricular wall tension; it is specific for indicating heart failure.
* <b style="color:red">Magnetic Resonance Angiography (MRA)</b> helps quantify RV/LV mass, volume, and coronary anatomy.

### General Management and Pharmacology

| Drug Class | Examples | Mechanism of Action / Clinical Note |
| :--- | :--- | :--- |
| <b style="color:red">Diuretics</b> | Furosemide, Spironolactone | Reduces circulating volume to treat pulmonary overload; requires electrolyte monitoring. |
| <b style="color:red">ACE Inhibitors</b> | Captopril, Enalapril | Reduces afterload by decreasing peripheral resistance; prevents <i style="color:blue">cardiac remodeling</i> (scarring). |
| <b style="color:red">Beta-Blockers</b> | Metoprolol, Carvedilol | Chronic treatment to improve exercise tolerance and reduce mortality; Carvedilol has free radical scavenging. |
| <b style="color:red">ARNI</b> | Sacubitril + Valsartan | Combined inhibition of neprilysin (increasing BNP) and blocking Angiotensin II; acts synergistically against cell death. |
| <b style="color:red">Inotropes</b> | Digoxin, Dopamine | Exert positive inotropic effects (increase contractility); Digoxin used less now due to toxicity risk. |
| <b style="color:red">PDE Inhibitors</b> | Milrinone | Prevents cAMP degradation; provides positive inotropic effects and peripheral/pulmonary vasodilation. |
| <b style="color:red">New Therapies</b> | Seralaxin, Ivabradine | Seralaxin (human relaxin-2) for acute HF; Ivabradine lowers HR without decreasing contractility. |

* <b style="color:red">Dietary Management</b> includes increasing daily calories (due to hypermetabolic state) and "no added salt."
* <b style="color:red">Fluid Management</b> depends on etiology: Volume <i style="color:blue">restriction</i> is used for ventricular dysfunction, while volume <i style="color:blue">replacement</i> is used for blood loss/hypovolemia.
* <b style="color:red">Dopamine</b> at higher doses has alpha-adrenergic effects, but it provides <i style="color:blue">selective renal vasodilation</i> at specific doses.
* <b style="color:red">Nitroprusside</b> is an IV afterload reducer used in critically ill patients for short durations; it also benefits cardiac remodeling.

### High-Yield Comparisons and Differentiators

* <b style="color:red">Infant vs. Older Child</b>: Infants show heart failure through feeding interruptions and "cold sweats," while older children show it through exercise intolerance and orthopnea.
* <b style="color:red">Preload vs. Afterload</b>: Preload is the "filling" volume (directly proportional to CO); afterload is the "resistance" against ejection (inversely proportional to CO).
* <b style="color:red">Systolic vs. Diastolic Dysfunction</b>: Systolic is a problem with "pumping/ejection" (contraction); diastolic is a problem with "relaxing/filling."
* <b style="color:red">Left-sided vs. Right-sided Failure</b>: Left-sided causes <i style="color:blue">pulmonary</i> symptoms (dyspnea, wheezing); Right-sided causes <i style="color:blue">systemic</i> symptoms (hepatomegaly, edema).
* <b style="color:red">Ross vs. ACCF/AHA</b>: Ross classifies based on <i style="color:blue">functional/symptomatic class</i> (I-IV), while ACCF/AHA classifies based on <i style="color:blue">disease progression/risk</i> (Stages A-D).
* <b style="color:red">Stage B vs. Stage C HF</b>: Stage B has structural heart disease <i style="color:blue">without</i> symptoms; Stage C has structural heart disease <i style="color:blue">with</i> symptoms.
* <b style="color:red">ACE Inhibitors vs. Beta-Blockers</b>: ACE inhibitors are primarily used to reduce <i style="color:blue">afterload</i> and remodeling; Beta-blockers are used in <i style="color:blue">chronic</i> treatment to improve long-term survival and mortality.
* <b style="color:red">Dopamine vs. Dobutamine</b>: Dopamine has dose-dependent renal vasodilation and alpha effects; Dobutamine has direct inotropic effects and moderate peripheral resistance reduction.
* <b style="color:red">BNP vs. End-Diastolic Volume</b>: BNP is a <i style="color:blue">neurohormone</i> biomarker of wall stress; LVEDV is a <i style="color:blue">hemodynamic volume</i> measurement of preload.
* <b style="color:red">Milrinone vs. Digoxin</b>: Milrinone is a phosphodiesterase inhibitor with vasodilatory properties; Digoxin is a cardiac glycoside with positive inotropic effects but higher toxicity risk.
* <b style="color:red">Hypovolemia vs. Ventricular Dysfunction</b>: In hypovolemic HF, the treatment is <i style="color:blue">fluid replacement</i>; in ventricular dysfunction HF, the treatment is <i style="color:blue">fluid restriction</i>.
* <b style="color:red">Sacubitril vs. Valsartan (in ARNI)</b>: Sacubitril blocks neprilysin to maintain high BNP; Valsartan blocks Angiotensin II to cause vasodilation.
* <b style="color:red">Inotropic vs. Lusitropic</b>: Inotropic relates to the force of <i style="color:blue">contraction</i>; lusitropic (implied by diastolic relaxation context) relates to the heart's ability to <i style="color:blue">relax</i>.

# Cardio 11 - Overview of Congenital Heart Disease
## Summary

### General Principles of Congenital Heart Disease (CHD)

* Congenital Heart Disease (CHD) occurs in approximately <b>0.8% of live births</b>.
* In terms of timing of diagnosis for CHD, <b>40-50%</b> are diagnosed at 1 week old and <b>50-60%</b> are diagnosed at 1 month old.
* Congenital Heart Disease is the <b>leading cause of death</b> in children with congenital malformations.
* The <b>most commonly occurring congenital heart lesion</b> is the <b>Ventricular Septal Defect (VSD)</b>, accounting for 30-35% of cases.
* <b>Acyanotic CHD</b> is divided into <b>Increased Volume Load</b> (ASD, VSD, AVSD, PDA) and <b>Increased Pressure Load</b> (Pulmonic stenosis, Aortic stenosis, Coarctation of the Aorta).
* <b>Cyanotic CHD</b> is divided into <b>Decreased Pulmonary Flow</b> (TOF, Pulmonary atresia, Tricuspid Atresia) and <b>Increased Pulmonary Flow</b> (TGA, TAPVR, Truncus arteriosus).
* To clinically check for cyanosis in a child, the <b>tongue or oral mucosa</b> should be inspected, as the lips can be affected by cold or anemia.
* <b>Clubbing</b> of fingernails in a pediatric patient indicates <b>chronic hypoxemia</b>.
* A <b>Left-to-Right (L → R) Shunt</b> occurs when pressure in the left side of the heart exceeds the right, leading to <b>volume overload</b> of the right atrium, right ventricle, and pulmonary circulation.
* Patients with <b>Acyanotic L → R shunts</b> are <b>initially not blue</b> but may present with frequent respiratory infections, tachypnea, and heart failure.
* A <b>Right-to-Left (R → L) Shunt</b> occurs when right-sided pressures exceed left-sided pressures (e.g., pulmonary hypertension), causing deoxygenated blood to enter systemic circulation and resulting in <b>cyanosis</b>.
* <b>Compliance</b> affects shunting; a more compliant pulmonary vasculature increases the likelihood of an L → R shunt.

### Acyanotic L-R Shunt Lesions (Increased Volume Load)

| Feature | Atrial Septal Defect (ASD) | Ventricular Septal Defect (VSD) | Atrioventricular Septal Defect (AVSD) | Patent Ductus Arteriosus (PDA) |
| :--- | :--- | :--- | :--- | :--- |
| **Pathogenesis** | Left-to-right shunt at the atrial level; <b>Qp:Qs</b> ratios of 2:1 to 4:1. | Shunt from LV to RV during <b>systole</b>; RV does not enlarge because blood goes directly to PA. | Failure of endocardial cushions to meet; involves both atrial and ventricular septal defects. | Persistent connection between the aorta and pulmonary artery. |
| **Most Common Type** | <b>Ostium Secundum</b> (most common type overall). | Perimembranous (most common section). | <b>Complete AVSD</b> (common in Down Syndrome). | Juxtaductal connection. |
| **Clinical Findings** | Often asymptomatic; <b>S2 widely split and fixed</b>; SEM at LUSB. | Dyspnea, growth failure, <b>holosystolic murmur at LLSB</b>. | Heart failure in infancy, recurrent RTI, <b>holosystolic murmur at apex (MR)</b>. | Bounding peripheral pulses; <b>Machinery-like murmur</b> at 2nd left interspace. |
| **ECG Findings** | RAD, RAE, RVH, <b>rsR pattern</b> in V1. | LVH or BVH; LAE. | BVH or RVH; tall P waves; <b>Left Axis Deviation (LAD)</b>. | LVH, BVH. |
| **CXR Findings** | RAE, RVE, prominent <b>Main Pulmonary Artery (MPA)</b>. | LAE, LVH (large VSD), pulmonary edema. | <b>Biatrial and Biventricular enlargement</b>; congested lungs. | LAE, LVE, <b>Prominent aortic knob</b>; "Picket fence" on Echo. |
| **Treatment** | Transcatheter closure (if Qp:Qs > 2:1) or Surgery. | Small: observe (30-50% close). Large: surgery for failure to thrive or PAH. | Surgery must be performed <b>early during infancy</b>. | 1st line: Transcatheter; Medical: Indomethacin/Ibuprofen (if premature). |

* <b>Ostium Secundum ASD</b> is more common in <b>females</b> (M:F 1:3) and occurs in the region of the fossa ovalis.
* <b>Holt-Oram Syndrome</b> is an autosomal dominant condition characterized by <b>hypoplasia of radii</b>, 1st degree AV block, and <b>ASD</b>.
* <b>Sinus Venosus ASD</b> is commonly associated with <b>Partial Anomalous Pulmonary Venous Return (PAPVR)</b>.
* <b>Ostium Primum ASD</b> (or Partial AVSD) presents with a <b>cleft in the anterior leaflet</b> of the mitral valve.
* <b>AVSD</b> is the <b>most common cardiac defect</b> in patients with <b>Down Syndrome</b>.
* In <b>AVSD</b>, the passage of blood from the LA through the mitral valve during systole produces a <b>murmur of Mitral Regurgitation</b> at the apex.
* <b>VSD</b> is the <b>most common cardiac malformation</b> overall, representing 25% of CHD.
* In <b>VSD</b>, the <b>Left Atrium (LA) and Left Ventricle (LV)</b> are the chambers that enlarge, rather than the RV.
* <b>Supracristal VSD</b> is a specific indication for surgery because it may lead to <b>Aortic Regurgitation (AR)</b>.
* <b>Eisenmenger physiology</b> is a complication of long-standing L-R shunts where pulmonary hypertension causes the shunt to reverse (Right-to-Left).
* <b>Patent Ductus Arteriosus (PDA)</b> is significantly associated with <b>maternal rubella infection</b> in the first trimester.
* <b>PDA</b> is more common in <b>premature infants</b> because the smooth muscle in the ductus is less responsive to high PO2.
* A <b>systolic thrill</b> maximal in the 2nd left interspace is classic for <b>PDA</b>.
* <b>Aortopulmonary Window</b> is a condition where the pulmonary artery and aorta are attached, mimicking PDA symptoms but requiring <b>bypass surgery</b>.

### Acyanotic Obstructive Lesions (Increased Pressure Load)

| Feature | Pulmonary Valve Stenosis (PS) | Aortic Stenosis (AS) | Coarctation of the Aorta (CoA) |
| :--- | :--- | :--- | :--- |
| **Associated Syndromes** | <b>Noonan Syndrome</b> (PV dysplasia); Alagille Syndrome. | <b>Turner Syndrome</b> (Bicuspid AV); Williams Syndrome (Supravalvular). | <b>Turner Syndrome</b>; Bicuspid Aortic Valve (70% of cases). |
| **Clinical Presentation** | SEM at LUSB radiating to back; RV lift if severe. | SEM at <b>Right 2nd ICS</b> radiating to neck; LV failure if critical. | <b>BP/Pulse disparity</b> (Upper > Lower); leg pain after exercise. |
| **Murmur** | Systolic Ejection Click; SEM at LUSB. | Systolic ejection click at apex; SEM at <b>right upper sternum</b>. | Short systolic murmur at <b>3rd-4th LSB</b>; systolic/continuous murals over back (collaterals). |
| **X-ray Findings** | Post-stenotic dilatation of PA; RVE. | Prominent ascending aorta; cardiomegaly in severe cases. | <b>3 sign</b> (aorta indentation); <b>E sign</b> (on barium swallow); <b>Rib notching</b>. |
| **Treatment** | <b>Balloon valvuloplasty</b> is first line for mod-severe. | Balloon valvuloplasty; <b>Ross procedure</b> (translocating PV to Aortic position). | Resection and end-to-end anastomosis; surgery at 2-3 years old if possible. |

* <b>Pulmonary Valve Stenosis</b> pathophysiology involves a pressure buildup in the RV leading to <b>RV hypertrophy</b> and potentially right-sided heart failure (hepatomegaly, edema).
* <b>Noonan Syndrome</b> is the most common association for <b>Pulmonary Valve Dysplasia</b>.
* <b>Mild Pulmonary Stenosis</b> is diagnosed when the PV gradient is <b>&lt;30 mmHg</b>.
* <b>Severe Pulmonary Stenosis</b> is diagnosed when the PV gradient is <b>>60 mmHg</b> and may present with <b>cyanosis</b> due to R-L shunting at the atrial level.
* <b>Bicuspid Aortic Valve</b> occurs in 1.5% of adults and carries a risk of <b>aortic dilatation</b>.
* <b>Supravalvular Aortic Stenosis</b> is classically associated with <b>Williams Syndrome</b>.
* <b>Shone Complex</b> consists of <b>Aortic Stenosis, Mitral Stenosis, and Coarctation</b>.
* The <b>Ross procedure</b> for Aortic Stenosis involves removing the patient's <b>pulmonary valve</b> to replace the aortic valve and using a homograft for the pulmonary position.
* <b>Coarctation of the Aorta</b> is most commonly <b>juxtaductal</b> (98%), occurring below the left subclavian artery.
* A blood pressure difference of <b>>20 mmHg</b> between the arms and legs is diagnostic for <b>Coarctation of the Aorta</b>.
* <b>Rib notching</b> in Coarctation is caused by the <b>dilation of intercostal arteries</b> serving as collateral circulation.
* <b>Postcoarctectomy syndrome</b> is a paradoxical hypertension occurring 3-5 days after repair due to rebound sympathetic and RAS activation.
* <b>Postoperative Mesenteric Arteritis</b> is a potential surgical complication of CoA repair presenting with abdominal pain and hypertension.
* In newborns with critical Coarctation, <b>Prostaglandin</b> is given to keep the ductus arteriosus open and maintain lower body perfusion.
* The <b>most common cause of death</b> in unrepaired Coarctation is <b>Congestive Heart Failure (26%)</b>, followed by aortic rupture and intracranial hemorrhage.

### Key Differentiations for Exams

* <b>ASD vs. VSD Heart Enlargement</b>: Atrial Septal Defect primarily causes <b>Right</b> heart enlargement (RAE, RVE), while Ventricular Septal Defect primarily causes <b>Left</b> heart enlargement (LAE, LVE).
* <b>ASD vs. PS Murmur</b>: Both have a Systolic Ejection Murmur at the LUSB, but <b>ASD</b> is characterized by a <b>fixed split S2</b>, whereas <b>Pulmonary Stenosis</b> typically has a <b>split S2 that varies</b> and may have an ejection click.
* <b>Murmur Location</b>: Aortic Stenosis is loudest at the <b>Right Upper Sternal Border</b>; Pulmonary Stenosis and ASD are loudest at the <b>Left Upper Sternal Border</b>; VSD is loudest at the <b>Left Lower Sternal Border</b>.
* <b>Murmur Quality</b>: PDA has a <b>continuous machinery-like</b> murmur; VSD has a <b>pansystolic/holosystolic</b> murmur; AS and PS have <b>crescendo-decrescendo ejection</b> murmurs.
* <b>Axis on ECG</b>: <b>AVSD</b> is unique among left-to-right shunts for having <b>Left Axis Deviation (LAD)</b> or Extreme Axis Deviation; Contrast this with <b>ASD</b>, which presents with <b>Right Axis Deviation (RAD)</b>.
* <b>Down Syndrome vs. Turner Syndrome</b>: Down Syndrome is linked to <b>AVSD</b>; Turner Syndrome is linked to <b>Coarctation of the Aorta</b> and <b>Bicuspid Aortic Valve</b>.
* <b>Cyanosis Check</b>: <b>Central cyanosis</b> (tongue/mucosa) suggests CHD; <b>Peripheral cyanosis</b> (lips/extremities) may just be cold or anemia.
* <b>Obstructive vs. Shunt X-ray</b>: Shunt lesions (VSD, ASD, PDA) show <b>increased pulmonary vascular markings</b> (congested lungs); obstructive lesions (PS, AS) often show <b>normal or decreased markings</b> unless heart failure occurs.
* <b>VSD Shunt Timing</b>: In VSD, the shunt occurs during <b>systole</b>; in PDA, the shunt occurs during <b>both systole and diastole</b>.
* <b>VSD Chamber Spare</b>: In VSD, the <b>Right Ventricle (RV) does not enlarge</b> because the shunted blood is ejected directly into the pulmonary artery during contraction.
* <b>CoA Blood Pressure</b>: If Coarctation is <b>between</b> the 2nd (LCC) and 3rd (LS) aortic branches, the <b>left arm</b> will be <b>hypotensive</b> while the <b>right arm</b> is <b>hypertensive</b>.
* <b>Supracristal VSD</b>: Unique among VSDs because it is highly associated with <b>Aortic Valve Prolapse and Regurgitation</b>.
* <b>PDA in Preterm vs. Term</b>: PDA in <b>preterm</b> infants is due to <b>physiological immaturity</b>; PDA in <b>term</b> infants is usually due to a <b>structural deficiency</b> in the ductal wall (lacks mucoid endothehal layer).
* <b>ASD Prevalence</b>: <b>Ostium secundum</b> is the most common ASD; <b>Ostium primum</b> is the one associated with AV valve defects.
* <b>Cyanotic Increased vs. Decreased Flow</b>: <b>TOF</b> has decreased pulmonary flow (clearer lungs on X-ray); <b>TGA and TAPVR</b> have increased pulmonary flow (congested "dirty" lungs on X-ray).
* <b>Medical vs. Surgical Timing</b>: <b>ASD</b> is often detected later (3rd decade); <b>AVSD</b> and <b>Large VSD</b> cause failure early in infancy and require <b>early surgical intervention</b>.

## QA
1. What is the approximate occurrence of <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Congenital Heart Disease (CHD)</span> in live births? | 0.8%
2. In <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Congenital Heart Disease</span>, what percentage of cases are diagnosed at 1 week old? | 40-50%
