3. In <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Congenital Heart Disease</span>, what percentage of cases are diagnosed at 1 month old? | 50-60%
4. What is the leading cause of death in children with <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">congenital malformations</span>? | Congenital Heart Disease
5. What is the most commonly occurring <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">congenital heart lesion</span>? | Ventricular Septal Defect (VSD)
6. What percentage of Congenital Heart Disease cases are accounted for by <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Ventricular Septal Defect (VSD)</span>? | 30-35%
7. Into what two categories is <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Acyanotic CHD</span> divided? | Increased Volume and Pressure Load
8. Enumerate the lesions associated with <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Increased Volume Load</span> in Acyanotic CHD (4). | ASD, VSD, AVSD, PDA
9. Enumerate the lesions associated with <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Increased Pressure Load</span> in Acyanotic CHD (3). | Pulmonic stenosis, Aortic stenosis, Coarctation
10. Into what two categories is <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Cyanotic CHD</span> divided? | Decreased and Increased Pulmonary Flow
11. Enumerate the lesions associated with <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Decreased Pulmonary Flow</span> (3). | TOF, Pulmonary atresia, Tricuspid Atresia
12. Enumerate the lesions associated with <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Increased Pulmonary Flow</span> (3). | TGA, TAPVR, Truncus arteriosus
13. Which areas should be inspected to clinically check for <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">cyanosis</span> in a child? (2) | Tongue or oral mucosa
14. Why are the <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">lips</span> unreliable for checking cyanosis in children? | Affected by cold or anemia
15. What does <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">clubbing</span> of fingernails indicate in a pediatric patient? | Chronic hypoxemia
16. In a <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Left-to-Right (L → R) Shunt</span>, which side of the heart has higher pressure? | Left side
17. What three areas experience <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">volume overload</span> in a Left-to-Right shunt? | RA, RV, and pulmonary circulation
18. How do patients with <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Acyanotic L → R shunts</span> initially present regarding color? | Initially not blue
19. Patients with <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Acyanotic L → R shunts</span> may present with what three symptoms? | Respiratory infections, tachypnea, heart failure
20. When does a <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Right-to-Left (R → L) Shunt</span> occur? | Right pressures exceed left
21. What is the result of deoxygenated blood entering systemic circulation in <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Right-to-Left shunts</span>? | Cyanosis
22. How does a more compliant <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">pulmonary vasculature</span> affect shunting? | Increases L → R shunt
23. What is the pathogenesis of <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Atrial Septal Defect (ASD)</span>? | Left-to-right atrial shunt
24. What is the typical <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Qp:Qs ratio</span> in Atrial Septal Defect? | 2:1 to 4:1
25. At what time during the cardiac cycle does the shunt occur in <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Ventricular Septal Defect (VSD)</span>? | Systole
26. Why does the <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Right Ventricle</span> not enlarge in Ventricular Septal Defect? | Blood goes directly to PA
27. What is the pathogenesis of <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Atrioventricular Septal Defect (AVSD)</span>? | Failure of endocardial cushions
28. Which two septal defects are involved in <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">AVSD</span>? | Atrial and ventricular
29. What is the pathogenesis of <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Patent Ductus Arteriosus (PDA)</span>? | Persistent aorta-PA connection
30. What is the most common type overall of <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Atrial Septal Defect</span>? | Ostium Secundum
31. What is the most common section for <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Ventricular Septal Defect</span>? | Perimembranous
32. Which type of AVSD is common in <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Down Syndrome</span>? | Complete AVSD
33. What is the anatomical connection type for <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Patent Ductus Arteriosus</span>? | Juxtaductal connection
34. What is the classic <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">S2 finding</span> in Atrial Septal Defect? | Widely split and fixed
35. What type of <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">murmur</span> is heard in ASD and where? | SEM at LUSB
36. What are the clinical findings of <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Ventricular Septal Defect</span>? (3) | Dyspnea, growth failure, holosystolic murmur
37. Where is the <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">holosystolic murmur</span> of VSD loudest? | Left Lower Sternal Border (LLSB)
38. What <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">murmur</span> is heard at the apex in Atrioventricular Septal Defect? | Holosystolic murmur (MR)
39. What type of peripheral pulses are found in <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Patent Ductus Arteriosus</span>? | Bounding peripheral pulses
40. Describe the <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">murmur</span> heard in Patent Ductus Arteriosus. | Machinery-like murmur
41. Where is the murmur of <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">PDA</span> located? | 2nd left interspace
42. What <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">pattern in V1</span> is seen on ECG for Atrial Septal Defect? | rsR pattern
43. What ECG findings are common in <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">ASD</span>? (3) | RAD, RAE, RVH
44. What <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">axis deviation</span> is unique to Atrioventricular Septal Defect? | Left Axis Deviation (LAD)
45. What are the <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">ECG findings</span> for Patent Ductus Arteriosus? | LVH, BVH
46. What <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Main Pulmonary Artery</span> finding is seen on CXR in ASD? | Prominent MPA
47. Which chambers enlarge on CXR in a large <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Ventricular Septal Defect</span>? | LAE and LVH
48. What are the <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">CXR findings</span> for AVSD? | Biatrial and Biventricular enlargement
49. What aortic finding is seen on CXR in <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Patent Ductus Arteriosus</span>? | Prominent aortic knob
50. What is the classic find on <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Echo</span> for Patent Ductus Arteriosus? | "Picket fence"
51. When is <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">transcatheter closure</span> indicated for Atrial Septal Defect? | Qp:Qs > 2:1
52. What is the observational management for a <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">small VSD</span>? | Observe (30-50% close)
53. When is <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">surgery</span> indicated for a large VSD? | Failure to thrive or PAH
54. When must <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">surgery</span> be performed for Atrioventricular Septal Defect? | Early during infancy
55. What is the <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">first-line treatment</span> for Patent Ductus Arteriosus? | Transcatheter closure
56. What <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">medical treatments</span> are used for PDA in premature infants? (2) | Indomethacin or Ibuprofen
57. In which <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">gender</span> is Ostium Secundum ASD more common? | Females (M:F 1:3)
58. Where does <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Ostium Secundum ASD</span> occur? | Region of fossa ovalis
59. Enumerate the characteristics of <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Holt-Oram Syndrome</span> (3). | Hypoplasia of radii, AV block, ASD
60. What condition is commonly associated with <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Sinus Venosus ASD</span>? | PAPVR
61. What <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">valvular defect</span> is present in Ostium Primum ASD? | Cleft in mitral valve
62. What is the most common cardiac defect in <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Down Syndrome</span>? | Atrioventricular Septal Defect (AVSD)
63. In AVSD, the systolic murmur at the apex mimics what <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">valvular condition</span>? | Mitral Regurgitation
64. What percentage of CHD does <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">VSD</span> represent? | 25%
65. Why is <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Supracristal VSD</span> a specific indication for surgery? | May lead to Aortic Regurgitation
66. Define <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Eisenmenger physiology</span>. | Shunt reversal due to PAH
67. What maternal infection is associated with <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Patent Ductus Arteriosus</span>? | Maternal rubella
68. Why is <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">PDA</span> more common in premature infants? | Ductus less responsive to PO2
69. What <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">palpatory finding</span> in the 2nd left interspace is classic for PDA? | Systolic thrill
70. What condition mimics PDA but requires <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">bypass surgery</span>? | Aortopulmonary Window
71. Which syndromes are associated with <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Pulmonary Valve Stenosis</span>? (2) | Noonan and Alagille Syndrome
72. Which syndrome is specifically associated with <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Bicuspid Aortic Valve</span>? | Turner Syndrome
73. Which syndrome is specifically associated with <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Supravalvular Aortic Stenosis</span>? | Williams Syndrome
74. What is the most common association for <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Coarctation of the Aorta</span>? | Turner Syndrome
75. What percentage of <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Coarctation</span> cases have a Bicuspid Aortic Valve? | 70%
76. What characterizes the clinical presentation of <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Coarctation of the Aorta</span>? | BP/Pulse disparity
77. Where does the SEM of <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Aortic Stenosis</span> radiate? | To the neck
78. What is the murmur location and type for <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Pulmonary Stenosis</span>? | SEM at LUSB (to back)
79. What 3 signs are seen on CXR for <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Coarctation of the Aorta</span>? | 3 sign, E sign, Rib notching
80. What is the first-line treatment for moderate-severe <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Pulmonary Stenosis</span>? | Balloon valvuloplasty
81. Describe the <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Ross procedure</span>. | Translocating PV to Aortic position
82. What is the preferred surgery for <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Coarctation of the Aorta</span>? | Resection and end-to-end anastomosis
83. At what age is <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Coarctation surgery</span> usually performed? | 2-3 years old
84. <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">RV hypertrophy</span> in Pulmonary Stenosis can lead to what two clinical signs? | Hepatomegaly and edema
85. What gradient defines <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Mild Pulmonary Stenosis</span>? | <30 mmHg
86. What gradient defines <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Severe Pulmonary Stenosis</span>? | >60 mmHg
87. What risk is associated with <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Bicuspid Aortic Valve</span> in adults? | Aortic dilatation
88. Enumerate the three components of <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Shone Complex</span>. | AS, MS, and Coarctation
89. In the <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Ross procedure</span>, what is used to replace the pulmonary position? | Homograft
90. Where does <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">juxtaductal Coarctation</span> occur relative to the left subclavian artery? | Below it
91. What <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">BP difference</span> between arms and legs is diagnostic for CoA? | >20 mmHg
92. What causes <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">rib notching</span> in Coarctation? | Dilation of intercostal arteries
93. When does <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">postcoarctectomy syndrome</span> occur? | 3-5 days after repair
94. What is the cause of <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">postcoarctectomy hypertension</span>? | Sympathetic and RAS activation
95. What complication of <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">CoA repair</span> presents with abdominal pain? | Postoperative Mesenteric Arteritis
96. Why is <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Prostaglandin</span> given in critical Coarctation? | Maintain lower body perfusion
97. What is the most common cause of death in <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">unrepaired Coarctation</span>? | Congestive Heart Failure (26%)
98. Contrast <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">ASD and VSD</span> heart enlargement. | ASD: Right; VSD: Left
99. Contrast the <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">S2</span> findings in ASD vs. Pulmonary Stenosis. | ASD Fixed; PS Varies
100. Where is the <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Aortic Stenosis murmur</span> loudest? | Right Upper Sternal Border
101. Where is the <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">VSD murmur</span> loudest? | Left Lower Sternal Border
102. Describe the murmur quality of <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">PDA</span>. | Continuous machinery-like
103. Describe the murmur quality of <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">VSD</span>. | Pansystolic/holosystolic
104. What is the ECG <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">axis deviation</span> for ASD? | Right Axis Deviation (RAD)
105. Compare the <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">syndromes</span>: Down vs. Turner. | Down: AVSD; Turner: CoA/BAV
106. Where is <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">central cyanosis</span> checked to suggest CHD? | Tongue/mucosa
107. What do <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">increased pulmonary markings</span> on X-ray suggest? | Shunt lesions (VSD, ASD, PDA)
108. Contrast the timing of the shunt in <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">VSD vs. PDA</span>. | VSD:-Systole; PDA: Systole/Diastole
109. If CoA is between LCC and LS branches, which <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">arm is hypotensive</span>? | Left arm
110. Which VSD is associated with <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Aortic Valve Prolapse</span>? | Supracristal VSD
111. Contrast PDA in <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">preterm vs. term</span> infants. | Preterm: physiological; Term: structural
112. Which <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">ASD</span> is the most common? | Ostium secundum
113. Contrast pulmonary flow in <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">TOF vs. TGA/TAPVR</span>. | TOF: Decreased; TGA/TAPVR: Increased
114. Contrast X-ray lungs in <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">TOF vs. TGA</span>. | TOF: Clear; TGA: Congested
115. Which acyanotic lesions require <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">early surgical intervention</span> in infancy? | AVSD and Large VSD
116. At what decade is <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">ASD</span> often detected? | 3rd decade
117. What is the <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">"3 sign"</span> on CXR representative of? | Coarctation of the Aorta
118. What is the gradient for <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Moderate Pulmonary Stenosis</span>? | 30-60 mmHg
119. What is required for <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">Aortopulmonary Window</span> treatment? | Bypass surgery
120. In <span style="color:red; font-weight:bold;">VSD</span>, which heart chamber is "spared" from enlargement? | Right Ventricle (RV)
# Cardio 12 - Acquired Heart Disease
## Summary
text
### I. RHEUMATIC FEVER (RF) AND RHEUMATIC HEART DISEASE (RHD)

| Category | Description |
| :--- | :--- |
| **Definition** | An inflammatory, delayed autoimmune response occurring in susceptible individuals following a Group A B-hemolytic Strep (GABHS) infection. |
| **Epidemiology** | **Rheumatic Heart Disease** is the most common acquired heart disease in children and adults worldwide and specifically in the Philippines. |
| **Pathogenesis** | Autoimmune/hypersensitivity reaction where autoantibodies against **M-protein** (strains M1, 3, 5, 6, 18) of GABHS cross-react with cardiac tissues. |
| **Target Organs** | Connective tissues of the heart (carditis), large joints (arthritis), skin (erythema marginatum), brain (chorea), and subcutaneous tissue. |
| **ARF vs. RHD** | **Acute Rheumatic Fever (ARF)** is the initial inflammatory attack. **Rheumatic Heart Disease (RHD)** is the chronic sequela characterized by permanent valvular damage (thickened valves). |

* **Strep vs. Viral Pharyngitis Differentiators:**
    * **Strep Pharyngitis:** Abrupt onset, age 5-15 years, poor appetite, <b>absent</b> cough/colds, beefy red granular pharynx with exudates, and tender submandibular adenopathy.
    * **Viral Pharyngitis:** Gradual onset, all ages, retained appetite, <b>present</b> cough/colds, boggy ulcers/vesicles, and non-tender lymph nodes.
* **Primary Prevention of RF:** Treats the initial GABHS infection to prevent RF. **Benzathine Penicillin G** (IM once) or **Penicillin V** (Oral for 10 days) are the Drugs of Choice (DOC).
* **Treatment for Penicillin-Allergic Patients (RF Primary Prevention):** Use Erythromycin, Clindamycin, or Azithromycin for a full course (10 days for most).
* **Jones Criteria (Establish initial ARF attack):** Diagnosis requires 2 Major criteria OR 1 Major + 2 Minor criteria, PLUS evidence of preceding Strep infection (except in Chorea or Indolent Carditis).
* **Jones Criteria - Major Manifestations (High-Risk/PH Population):**
    * **Polyarthritis:** The <b>most common</b> manifesting symptom (75%). Affects large joints, is asymmetric and polymigratory, and responds dramatically to Aspirin within 48-72 hours.
    * **Carditis:** The <b>most serious</b> manifestation causing permanent damage (40-50%). Can present as valvulitis (murmur), myocarditis (cardiomegaly), or pericarditis (friction rub).
    * **Subclinical Carditis:** Valvular dysfunction not heard on auscultation but detected via Echocardiography/Doppler.
    * **Sydenham’s Chorea:** Involuntarily movements ("dancing"), muscular weakness, and emotional disturbance. More common in prepubertal girls; has a long latent period (1-6 months).
    * **Erythema Marginatum:** Rare (<10%). Non-pruritic, pink, serpiginous/ring-like rashes on the trunk; evanescent (disappears in cold, reappears with heat).
    * **Subcutaneous Nodules:** Small, firm, painless nodules over bony prominences/extensor surfaces (knees, elbows, spine).
* **Jones Criteria - Minor Manifestations:** Includes Fever, Arthralgia (joint pain without inflammation), elevated acute phase reactants (ESR/CRP), and prolonged PR interval on ECG.
* **Evidence of Antecedent GABHS:** **ASO Titer** (Elevated ≥330 Todds in children; ≥250 in adults), Positive Throat Culture (found in 25%), or Anti-DNase B test.
* **Secondary Prophylaxis (Prevention of Recurrence):**
    * **Benzathine Penicillin G (IM):** Injected every 21 days (most effective method).
    * **Oral Penicillin V:** Taken twice daily every day.
    * **Erythromycin:** Reserved for those allergic to penicillin/sulfadiazine.
* **Duration of Secondary Prophylaxis:** 
    * **RF with Carditis AND Persistent Valvular Disease:** At least 10 years since last episode or until age 40 (sometimes lifelong).
    * **RF with Carditis but NO Valvular Disease:** 10 years or until adulthood (whichever is longer).
    * **RF WITHOUT Carditis:** 5 years or until age 21 (whichever is longer).
* **Anti-inflammatory Management:** **Aspirin** (high dose 90-100 mkd) is used for arthritis and mild/moderate carditis. **Prednisone** (2 mkd) is reserved for severe carditis or CHF.
* **Bed Rest Guidelines:** 1-2 weeks for arthritis; 3-4 weeks for mild carditis; 4-6 weeks for moderate carditis; and as long as CHF is present for severe carditis.

#### Valvular Lesions in RHD
* **Mitral Regurgitation (MR):** The <b>most common</b> RHD lesion. Presents with an apical holosystolic murmur radiating to the axilla; CXR shows LAE and LVH.
* **Mitral Stenosis (MS):** Becomes more common with increasing age. Presents with a loud S1 and an apical diastolic rumble; associated with high mortality.
* **Aortic Regurgitation (AR):** Presents with a basal diastolic blow, wide pulse pressure (Corrigan's pulse), and a <b>"swan-like"</b> heart appearance on CXR.

---

### II. KAWASAKI DISEASE (KD)

| Topic | Description |
| :--- | :--- |
| **Definition** | A self-limiting, febrile mucocutaneous vasculitis of childhood affecting small and medium-sized arteries, particularly the coronaries. |
| **Epidemiology** | 80% of cases occur in children <b><5 years old</b>. Male predominance (1.5:1). Rare in infants <4 months due to maternal antibodies. |
| **Etiology** | Unknown, but hypothesized to be infectious (seasonal/clusters) or a superantigen-driven immune response. |
| **Pathophysiology** | Characterized by vascular endothelial swelling, neutrophil/lymphocyte activation, and eventual fibrosis/stenosis of coronary arteries. |

* **5 Major Clinical Findings (Typical KD):**
    1. **Extremity changes:** Edema/erythema of palms and soles (acute); periungual desquamation (subacute).
    2. **Polymorphous exanthem:** Varied skin rash (not vesicular/bullous), often starting in the perineal/groin area.
    3. **Oropharyngeal changes:** Red/cracked/bleeding lips, strawberry tongue, and pharyngeal erythema.
    4. **Bulbar Conjunctivitis:** Non-exudative (dry), bilateral redness with limbic sparing.
    5. **Cervical Lymphadenopathy:** Usually unilateral, non-suppurative, and >1.5 cm.
* **Diagnostic Guideline:** Fever for ≥5 days <b>AND</b> at least 4 out of the 5 major clinical features.
* **Atypical/Incomplete Kawasaki Disease:** Diagnosed when a patient has fever >5 days but only 2-3 major criteria, supported by elevated CRP/ESR and positive supplemental labs or 2D Echo.
* **Clinical Phases of KD:**
    * **Acute (0-1.5 weeks):** High fever, conjunctivitis, rash, myocarditis.
    * **Subacute (2-6 weeks):** Resolution of fever, **desquamation** (glove-like), and highest risk of **coronary artery aneurysms**.
    * **Convalescent (>6 weeks):** Resolution of clinical signs until ESR normalizes.
* **Cardiac Imaging Schedule:** Echocardiogram should be performed: 1) At diagnosis, 2) At 2 weeks, and 3) At 6-8 weeks.
* **Coronary Artery Aneurysm Classification:** Small (<5 mm), Medium (5-8 mm), and Giant (>8 mm).
* **Risk Factors for Aneurysms (Harada Score):** Male sex, age <12 months, fever >10 days, WBC >12,000, Platelets <350,000, Hematocrit <35%, Albumin <3.5 g/dL, and CRP >3+.
* **Management Goals:** Reduce coronary artery inflammation and prevent thrombosis.
* **Acute Phase Treatment:** **IVIG (2 g/kg)** single infusion PLUS **High-dose Aspirin (80-100 mkd)**. This must be initiated within 10 days of fever onset to prevent aneurysms.
* **Post-Acute Phase Treatment:** Switch to **Low-dose Aspirin (3-5 mkd)** for anti-thrombotic effect once fever is controlled (usually until day 14 or 6-8 weeks).
* **Vaccination Precautions:** Defer Measles and Varicella (live) vaccines for <b>11 months</b> after IVIG administration.
* **Influenza Vaccine:** Mandatory for children on long-term Aspirin therapy to prevent Reye’s Syndrome.

---

### III. INFECTIVE ENDOCARDITIS (IE)

| Component | Description |
| :--- | :--- |
| **Definition** | Microbial infection of the endocardial surface, valves, or foreign cardiac devices. |
| **Pathogenesis** | Endothelial damage → Fibrin-platelet deposition (NBTE) → Bacterial adherence/proliferation → Protected vegetation. |

* **High-Risk Conditions for IE:** Prosthetic valves, previous IE, complex cyanotic CHD (TOF, TGA), surgically constructed shunts, and IV drug use.
* **Moderate-Risk Conditions for IE:** PDA, VSD, Bicuspid aortic valve, Mitral valve prolapse with regurgitation, and RHD.
* **Acyanotic CHD Note:** Simple acyanotic lesions (like Secundum ASD) are generally <b>not</b> at risk for IE unless repaired with prosthetic material or if residual defects exist.
* **Vascular/Immunologic Phenomena (Classic IE Signs):**
    * **Osler Nodes:** Small, <b>painful</b> nodules on finger/toe pads.
    * **Janeway Lesions:** Hemorrhagic, <b>painless</b> macules on palms/soles.
    * **Roth Spots:** Retinal hemorrhages with pale centers (funduscopy).
    * **Splinter Hemorrhages:** Linear red streaks under the nails.
* **Duke Criteria for Diagnosis:**
    * **Major Criteria:** 
        1. Positive blood cultures for typical organisms (e.g., Viridans Strep) from two separate cultures.
        2. Evidence of endocardial involvement on Echogram (vegetation, abscess, or new valvular regurgitation).
    * **Minor Criteria:** Predisposition (heart condition/IV drug use), Fever ≥38°C, Vascular phenomena, Immunologic phenomena, and Microbiological evidence (atypical cultures).
* **Diagnosis Interpretation:** Definitive IE requires 2 Major criteria OR 1 Major + 3 Minor OR 5 Minor.
* **IE Prophylaxis for Dental Procedures:** Only recommended for high-risk patients (Prosthetic valves, unrepaired cyanotic CHD, repaired CHD with prosthetic material for 6 months).
* **Antibiotic Prophylaxis DOC:** **Amoxicillin** (50 mg/kg) orally 30-60 minutes before the procedure; use Clindamycin or Cephalexin if allergic to Penicillin.

---

### COMPARATIVE ANALYSIS FOR EXAMS

| Feature | Rheumatic Fever (RF) | Kawasaki Disease (KD) | Infective Endocarditis (IE) |
| :--- | :--- | :--- | :--- |
| **Primary Trigger** | Post-GABHS (Strep Throat) | Unknown (likely Infectious) | Direct Microbial infection |
| **Fever Pattern** | Usually present (Minor criteria) | High, Spiking, >5 days (Required) | Persistent/Prolonged |
| **Skin Signs** | Erythema Marginatum (Serpiginous) | Polymorphous rash / Desquamation | Janeway lesions / Osler nodes |
| **Pathognomonic Site**| Heart valves (Endocardium) | Coronary Arteries (Vasculitis) | Vegetations on Valves/Devices |
| **Diagnostic Tool** | Jones Criteria + ASO Titer | Fever + 4/5 clinical criteria | Duke Criteria + Blood Culture |
| **Aspirin Use** | High dose for Anti-inflammatory | High (Acute) then Low (Subacute) | Not indicated (use Antibiotics) |
| **Most Common Valve**| Mitral Valve (MR) | N/A (Mainly Coronary Arteries) | Varies (Native or Prosthetic) |
| **Age Group** | 5 - 14 years old | < 5 years (80% of cases) | Any age (with risk factors) |
| **Joint Involvement** | Polymigratory Arthritis (Large joints) | Arthritis can occur (not a major criteria) | Rare/Non-specific |
| **Treatment DOC**| Penicillin (Secondary Prophylaxis) | IVIG + Aspirin | Long-term IV Antibiotics |

* **Joint Pain Distinction:** In **RF**, Arthritis is **Major** (swelling/redness) and Polymigratory, while Arthralgia is **Minor** (pain only).
* **Skin Lesion Distinction:** **Janeway lesions** (IE) are painless and flat; **Osler nodes** (IE) are painful and raised; **Erythema Marginatum** (RF) is ring-shaped and non-pruritic.
* **Strep Infection Timeline:** RF occurs **10-14 days** after strep throat; IE is an active infection.
* **CHD Risk Distinction:** **Cyanotic CHD** are high risk for IE; **Acyanotic CHD** (like Secundum ASD) are considered low/no risk for IE by the lecturer.
* **Desquamation Distinction:** KD desquamation is <b>glove-like</b> and occurs in the <b>subacute phase</b> (2-6 weeks).
* **ASO Titer Importance:** High ASO titer establishes preceding Strep infection for RF but is NOT used for KD or IE diagnosis.
* **Vaccination Delay:** Specifically associated with **Kawasaki Disease** (defer live vaccines for 11 months due to IVIG).
* **Aspirin Response:** RF arthritis responds dramatically within 2-3 days; KD fever responds/resolves within 1-2 days of IVIG.

## QA
1. What is the definition of <b style="color:red;">Rheumatic Fever (RF)</b>? | Delayed autoimmune response. <br>Inflammatory response occurring in susceptible individuals following a Group A B-hemolytic Strep infection.
2. In epidemiology, what is the most common acquired heart disease in children and adults in the Philippines? | <b style="color:red;">Rheumatic Heart Disease (RHD)</b>.
3. What is the pathogenesis of <b style="color:red;">Rheumatic Fever</b>? | Autoimmune/hypersensitivity reaction. <br>Autoantibodies cross-react with cardiac tissues.
4. Which specific protein of Group A B-hemolytic Strep do autoantibodies target in <b style="color:red;">Rheumatic Fever</b>? | <b>M-protein</b>. <br>Specifically strains M1, 3, 5, 6, and 18.
5. What are the target organs (5) of <b style="color:red;">Rheumatic Fever</b>? | Heart, joints, skin, brain, and subcutaneous tissue.
6. What is the clinical difference between <b style="color:red;">Acute Rheumatic Fever (ARF)</b> and <b style="color:red;">Rheumatic Heart Disease (RHD)</b>? | ARF is the initial inflammatory attack; RHD is the chronic sequela with permanent valvular damage.
7. How does the onset differ between <b style="color:red;">Strep Pharyngitis</b> and Viral Pharyngitis? | Strep is abrupt; Viral is gradual.
8. What is the typical age range for <b style="color:red;">Strep Pharyngitis</b>? | 5 to 15 years old.
9. Describe the appetite and presence of cough in <b style="color:red;">Strep Pharyngitis</b>. | <b>Poor</b> appetite; <b>absent</b> cough/colds.
10. Describe the pharyngeal appearance in <b style="color:red;">Strep Pharyngitis</b>. | Beefy red granular pharynx. <br>Includes exudates.
11. What type of lymphadenopathy is found in <b style="color:red;">Strep Pharyngitis</b>? | Tender submandibular adenopathy.
12. Contrast the cough and appetite in <b style="color:red;">Viral Pharyngitis</b> vs. Strep. | Retained appetite and <b>present</b> cough/colds.
13. What pharyngeal findings are characteristic of <b style="color:red;">Viral Pharyngitis</b>? | Boggy ulcers/vesicles.
14. What is the primary prevention of <b style="color:red;">Rheumatic Fever</b>? | Treats initial GABHS infection. <br>Prevents the occurrence of Rheumatic Fever.
15. What are the Drugs of Choice (2) for primary prevention of <b style="color:red;">Rheumatic Fever</b>? | <b>Benzathine Penicillin G</b> (IM) or <b>Penicillin V</b> (Oral).
16. What is the duration of oral Penicillin V for <b style="color:red;">Rheumatic Fever</b> primary prevention? | 10 days.
17. What are the treatments for <b style="color:red;">Penicillin-Allergic patients</b> in RF primary prevention? (3) | Erythromycin, Clindamycin, or Azithromycin.
18. What are the requirements for the <b style="color:red;">Jones Criteria</b> to diagnose an initial Rheumatic Fever attack? | 2 Major OR 1 Major + 2 Minor criteria plus evidence of Strep.
19. When is evidence of preceding Strep infection NOT required for <b style="color:red;">Jones Criteria</b>? (2) | Chorea or Indolent Carditis.
20. What is the <b>most common</b> manifesting symptom (75%) of <b style="color:red;">Acute Rheumatic Fever</b>? | <b>Polyarthritis</b>.
21. Describe the joint involvement in <b style="color:red;">Rheumatic Polyarthritis</b>. | Large joints; asymmetric and polymigratory.
22. How does <b style="color:red;">Rheumatic Polyarthritis</b> respond to Aspirin? | Dramatically within 48-72 hours.
23. What is the <b>most serious</b> manifestation of <b style="color:red;">Acute Rheumatic Fever</b>? | <b>Carditis</b>. <br>Affects 40-50% and causes permanent damage.
24. In what three ways can <b style="color:red;">Rheumatic Carditis</b> present? | Valvulitis (murmur), myocarditis (cardiomegaly), or pericarditis (friction rub).
25. Define <b style="color:red;">Subclinical Carditis</b> in Rheumatic Fever. | Valvular dysfunction found via Echo. <br>Not heard on auscultation.
26. What three features define <b style="color:red;">Sydenham’s Chorea</b>? | Involuntary movements, weakness, and emotional disturbance.
27. What is the epidemiology and latent period of <b style="color:red;">Sydenham’s Chorea</b>? | Prepubertal girls; long latent period (1-6 months).
28. Describe the skin appearance of <b style="color:red;">Erythema Marginatum</b>. | Pink, serpiginous/ring-like rashes. <br>Non-pruritic, located on the trunk.
29. What makes <b style="color:red;">Erythema Marginatum</b> evanescent? | Disappears in cold; reappears with heat.
30. What are <b style="color:red;">Subcutaneous Nodules</b> in Rheumatic Fever? | Small, firm, painless nodules. <br>Over bony prominences or extensor surfaces.
31. List the Minor manifestations of the <b style="color:red;">Jones Criteria</b>. (4) | Fever, Arthralgia, elevated ESR/CRP, and prolonged PR interval.
32. Define <b style="color:red;">Arthralgia</b> as a Minor criterion. | Joint pain without inflammation.
33. What are the specific <b style="color:red;">ASO Titer</b> levels that evidence antecedent GABHS? | ≥330 Todds (children); ≥250 (adults).
34. Besides ASO Titer, what other tests evidence <b style="color:red;">GABHS infection</b>? (2) | Positive Throat Culture or Anti-DNase B test.
35. What is the <b>most effective method</b> for secondary prophylaxis of <b style="color:red;">Rheumatic Fever</b>? | <b>Benzathine Penicillin G</b> injected every 21 days.
36. What is the oral drug for <b style="color:red;">Rheumatic Fever</b> secondary prophylaxis? | Penicillin V. <br>Taken twice daily every day.
37. Which drug is reserved for secondary prophylaxis in <b style="color:red;">Penicillin-Allergic patients</b>? | Erythromycin.
38. What is the duration of secondary prophylaxis for <b style="color:red;">RF with Carditis and Persistent valvular disease</b>? | At least 10 years or until age 40. <br>Whichever is longer; sometimes lifelong.
39. What is the duration of secondary prophylaxis for <b style="color:red;">RF with Carditis but NO valvular disease</b>? | 10 years or until adulthood.
40. What is the duration of secondary prophylaxis for <b style="color:red;">RF WITHOUT Carditis</b>? | 5 years or until age 21.
41. What is the treatment for <b style="color:red;">Rheumatic Arthritis</b> and mild/moderate carditis? | <b>High-dose Aspirin</b> (90-100 mg/kg/day).
42. When is <b>Prednisone</b> (2 mg/kg/day) indicated in <b style="color:red;">Rheumatic Fever</b>? | Severe carditis or Congestive Heart Failure (CHF).
43. What is the bed rest guideline for <b style="color:red;">Rheumatic Arthritis</b>? | 1 to 2 weeks.
44. What is the bed rest guideline for <b>Severe</b> <b style="color:red;">Rheumatic Carditis</b>? | As long as CHF is present.
45. What is the <b>most common</b> valvular lesion in <b style="color:red;">Rheumatic Heart Disease</b>? | <b>Mitral Regurgitation (MR)</b>.
46. What type of murmur characterizes <b style="color:red;">Mitral Regurgitation</b>? | Apical holosystolic murmur radiating to axilla.
47. What are the Chest X-ray findings for <b style="color:red;">Mitral Regurgitation</b>? | LAE and LVH. <br>Left Atrial Enlargement / Left Ventricular Hypertrophy.
48. Describe the murmur and S1 sound in <b style="color:red;">Mitral Stenosis (MS)</b>. | Loud S1 and apical diastolic rumble.
49. What physical findings are associated with <b style="color:red;">Aortic Regurgitation (AR)</b>? | Basal diastolic blow and wide pulse pressure.
50. What is the classic name for wide pulse pressure in <b style="color:red;">Aortic Regurgitation</b>? | Corrigan's pulse.
51. What is the characteristic Chest X-ray heart appearance in <b style="color:red;">Aortic Regurgitation</b>? | <b>"Swan-like"</b> heart.
52. What is the definition of <b style="color:red;">Kawasaki Disease (KD)</b>? | Febrile mucocutaneous vasculitis. <br>Affects small and medium-sized arteries (coronaries).
53. What percentage of <b style="color:red;">Kawasaki Disease</b> cases occur in children under 5 years old? | 80% of cases.
54. Why is <b style="color:red;">Kawasaki Disease</b> rare in infants under 4 months? | Maternal antibodies.
55. What is the hypothesized etiology of <b style="color:red;">Kawasaki Disease</b>? | Unknown. <br>Possibly infectious or superantigen-driven.
56. What characterizes the pathophysiology of <b style="color:red;">Kawasaki Disease</b>? | Vascular endothelial swelling and coronary artery fibrosis.
57. In <b style="color:red;">Kawasaki Disease</b>, what are the extremity changes in the acute vs. subacute phase? | Acute: Edema/erythema (palms/soles); Subacute: Periungual desquamation.
58. Describe the <b>Polymorphous exanthem</b> in <b style="color:red;">Kawasaki Disease</b>. | Varied skin rash (non-vesicular); starts in perineal/groin area.
59. What are the typical <b>Oropharyngeal changes</b> in <b style="color:red;">Kawasaki Disease</b>? (3) | Red/cracked lips, strawberry tongue, and pharyngeal erythema.
60. Describe <b style="color:red;">Bulbar Conjunctivitis</b> in Kawasaki Disease. | Non-exudative (dry) and bilateral with limbic sparing.
61. What are the characteristics of <b>Cervical Lymphadenopathy</b> in <b style="color:red;">Kawasaki Disease</b>? | Usually unilateral, non-suppurative, and >1.5 cm.
62. What is the diagnostic guideline for <b style="color:red;">Typical Kawasaki Disease</b>? | Fever ≥5 days and at least 4 out of 5 major clinical features.
63. How is <b style="color:red;">Atypical/Incomplete Kawasaki Disease</b> diagnosed? | Fever >5 days, only 2-3 major criteria, plus elevated inflammatory markers or positive 2D Echo.
64. What occurs during the <b>Acute Phase</b> (0-1.5 weeks) of <b style="color:red;">Kawasaki Disease</b>? | High fever, conjunctivitis, rash, and myocarditis.
65. What are the hallmarks (2) of the <b>Subacute Phase</b> (2-6 weeks) of <b style="color:red;">Kawasaki Disease</b>? | <b>Desquamation</b> and highest risk of <b>coronary artery aneurysms</b>.
66. When does the <b>Convalescent Phase</b> of <b style="color:red;">Kawasaki Disease</b> end? | When the ESR normalizes.
67. What is the <b style="color:red;">Cardiac Imaging Schedule</b> for Kawasaki Disease? | At diagnosis, 2 weeks, and 6 to 8 weeks.
68. Contrast the sizes of Small, Medium, and Giant <b style="color:red;">Coronary Artery Aneurysms</b>. | Small (<5 mm), Medium (5-8 mm), Giant (>8 mm).
69. List 5 risk factors in the <b>Harada Score</b> for coronary aneurysms. | Male sex, age <12 months, fever >10 days, WBC >12,000, and Platelets <350,000.
70. What are the <b>management goals</b> for <b style="color:red;">Kawasaki Disease</b>? | Reduce coronary inflammation and prevent thrombosis.
71. What is the <b>Acute Phase treatment</b> for <b style="color:red;">Kawasaki Disease</b>? | <b>IVIG</b> (2 g/kg) plus <b>High-dose Aspirin</b> (80-100 mg/kg/day).
72. When must treatment be initiated to prevent aneurysms in <b style="color:red;">Kawasaki Disease</b>? | Within 10 days of fever onset.
73. What is the <b>Post-Acute Phase treatment</b> for <b style="color:red;">Kawasaki Disease</b>? | <b>Low-dose Aspirin</b> (3-5 mg/kg/day).
74. How long should live vaccines (Measles/Varicella) be deferred after <b style="color:red;">IVIG</b>? | 11 months.
75. Why is the <b>Influenza vaccine</b> mandatory for children with <b style="color:red;">Kawasaki Disease</b> on Aspirin? | To prevent <b>Reye’s Syndrome</b>.
76. What is the definition of <b style="color:red;">Infective Endocarditis (IE)</b>? | Microbial infection of endocardial surface, valves, or foreign cardiac devices.
77. What is the pathogenesis sequence of <b style="color:red;">Infective Endocarditis</b>? | Endothelial damage → Fibrin-platelet deposition → Bacterial adherence → Vegetation.
78. List 4 high-risk conditions for <b style="color:red;">Infective Endocarditis</b>. | Prosthetic valves, previous IE, complex cyanotic CHD, and IV drug use.
79. What are 3 moderate-risk conditions for <b style="color:red;">Infective Endocarditis</b>? | PDA, VSD, and Bicuspid aortic valve.
80. Which acyanotic heart lesion is generally <b>not</b> at risk for <b style="color:red;">Infective Endocarditis</b>? | Secundum ASD.
81. Describe <b style="color:red;">Osler Nodes</b> in Infective Endocarditis. | Small, <b>painful</b> nodules on finger/toe pads.
82. Describe <b style="color:red;">Janeway Lesions</b> in Infective Endocarditis. | Hemorrhagic, <b>painless</b> macules on palms/soles.
83. What are <b style="color:red;">Roth Spots</b>? | Retinal hemorrhages with pale centers.
84. Describe <b style="color:red;">Splinter Hemorrhages</b>. | Linear red streaks under the nails.
85. List the two Major <b style="color:red;">Duke Criteria</b> for Infective Endocarditis. | 1) Positive blood cultures (typical organisms) 2) Echo evidence of endocardial involvement.
86. List the five Minor <b style="color:red;">Duke Criteria</b> for Infective Endocarditis. | Predisposition, Fever ≥38°C, Vascular phenomena, Immunologic phenomena, and Microbiological evidence.
87. What combinations (3) constitute a definitive diagnosis of <b style="color:red;">Infective Endocarditis</b>? | 2 Major OR 1 Major + 3 Minor OR 5 Minor.
88. Who is recommended for <b style="color:red;">IE Prophylaxis</b> during dental procedures? | High-risk patients only.
89. What is the Drug of Choice and dose for <b style="color:red;">IE Prophylaxis</b>? | <b>Amoxicillin</b> (50 mg/kg) orally 30-60 minutes before the procedure.
90. What is used for <b style="color:red;">IE Prophylaxis</b> if the patient is allergic to Penicillin? | Clindamycin or Cephalexin.
91. Compare the primary trigger of <b style="color:red;">Rheumatic Fever</b> vs. Kawasaki Disease. | RF is Post-GABHS; KD is Unknown (likely infectious).
92. Compare the <b>Fever Pattern</b> of Kawasaki Disease vs. Infective Endocarditis. | KD: High, Spiking, >5 days; IE: Persistent/Prolonged.
93. Contrast the pathognomonic sites of <b style="color:red;">Rheumatic Fever</b> and Kawasaki Disease. | RF: Heart valves; KD: Coronary Arteries.
94. Which disease uses the <b style="color:red;">ASO Titer</b> as a diagnostic tool? | Rheumatic Fever.
95. In which condition is <b style="color:red;">Aspirin</b> <b>not indicated</b>? | Infective Endocarditis (Use antibiotics).
96. Compare the age group of <b style="color:red;">Rheumatic Fever</b> vs. Kawasaki Disease. | RF: 5-14 years; KD: <5 years.
97. How does joint involvement differ between <b style="color:red;">Arthritis</b> and Arthralgia in RF? | Arthritis is Major (inflammation); Arthralgia is Minor (pain only).
98. What is the timeline of <b style="color:red;">Rheumatic Fever</b> following a strep throat infection? | 10 to 14 days later.
99. Describe the <b style="color:red;">Desquamation</b> in Kawasaki Disease. | Glove-like. <br>Occurs in the subacute phase (2-6 weeks).
100. How quickly does <b style="color:red;">Rheumatic Arthritis</b> respond to Aspirin? | Dramatically within 2 to 3 days.
# Cardio 13 - Cyanotic Heart Disease
## Summary
text
## PEDIATRIC CARDIOLOGY SUMMARY

<h2 style="color:red;">I. CYANOTIC HEART DISEASES WITH DECREASED PULMONARY BLOOD FLOW</h2>

<table>
  <thead>
    <tr style="background-color:#f2f2f2;">
      <th>Feature</th>
      <th>Tetralogy of Fallot (TOF)</th>
      <th>Tricuspid Valve Atresia (TVA)</th>
      <th>Ebstein Anomaly</th>
    </tr>
  </thead>
  <tbody>
    <tr>
      <td><b>Anatomical Hallmark</b></td>
      <td>RVOT obstruction, VSD, Overriding Aorta, RVH.</td>
      <td>Agenesis of Tricuspid valve; no RA to RV connection.</td>
      <td>Downward displacement of TV; "Atrialized" RV.</td>
    </tr>
    <tr>
      <td><b>Classic CXR Finding</b></td>
      <td><b style="color:red;">Coeur de sabot</b> (Boot-shaped heart).</td>
      <td>Variable; may show left-sided enlargement.</td>
      <td><b style="color:red;">Box-shaped</b> massive cardiomegaly.</td>
    </tr>
    <tr>
      <td><b>ECG Findings</b></td>
      <td>RVH and Right Atrial Enlargement (RAE).</td>
      <td><b style="color:red;">Left Axis Deviation (LAD)</b> and LVH.</td>
      <td>RBBB and <b style="color:red;">Wolff-Parkinson-White (WPW)</b>.</td>
    </tr>
    <tr>
      <td><b>Cardiac Murmur</b></td>
      <td>Systolic ejection murmur (LSB) radiating to back.</td>
      <td>Holosystolic murmur at LSB (from VSD).</td>
      <td>Holosystolic murmur (TR) and <b style="color:red;">Gallop rhythm</b>.</td>
    </tr>
    <tr>
      <td><b>Key Survival Feature</b></td>
      <td>Degree of RVOT stenosis determines severity.</td>
      <td>Requires <b style="color:red;">ASD</b> and <b style="color:red;">VSD/PDA</b> for blood flow.</td>
      <td>Requires <b style="color:red;">ASD</b> for survival.</td>
    </tr>
  </tbody>
</table>

### Tetraology of Fallot (TOF)
- <b style="color:red;">Tetralogy of Fallot (TOF)</b> consists of four components: Pulmonary/RVOT stenosis, malaligned VSD, Overriding Aorta, and Right Ventricular Hypertrophy (RVH).
- The severity of <b style="color:red;">Tetralogy of Fallot (TOF)</b> depends on the degree of RVOT stenosis, not the VSD size.
- A <b style="color:red;">"Pink TOF"</b> occurs when RVOT stenosis is mild, causing a Left-to-Right shunt and heart failure symptoms.
- Patients with <b style="color:red;">Tetralogy of Fallot (TOF)</b> assume a <b style="color:red;">squatting position</b> to increase Systemic Vascular Resistance (SVR), which reduces right-to-left shunting and improves oxygenation.
- A <b style="color:red;">Paroxysmal Hypercyanotic attack (Tet spell)</b> is characterized by hyperpnea, restlessness, worsening cyanosis, and the temporary disappearance of the murmur.
- Chronic hypoxemia in <b style="color:red;">Tetralogy of Fallot (TOF)</b> leads to <b style="color:red;">Polycythemia</b> and clubbing of the fingers.
- The CXR of <b style="color:red;">Tetralogy of Fallot (TOF)</b> shows a concave Main Pulmonary Artery and an uplifted apex.
- <b style="color:red;">Di George Syndrome (CATCH 22)</b> is an associated anomaly of TOF involving chromosome 22q11 deletion.
- Acute management of a <b style="color:red;">Tet spell</b> includes knee-chest position, oxygen, morphine, sodium bicarbonate, and <b style="color:red;">Propranolol</b>.
- The <b style="color:red;">Blalock-Taussig Shunt (BTS)</b> is a temporary surgical procedure creating a connection like an artificial PDA to stimulate pulmonary artery growth.

### Tricuspid Valve Atresia (TVA)
- <b style="color:red;">Tricuspid Valve Atresia (TVA)</b> is the only cyanotic CHD that typically presents with Left Axis Deviation (LAD) and Left Ventricular Hypertrophy (LVH).
- Survival in <b style="color:red;">Tricuspid Valve Atresia (TVA)</b> is dependent on an ASD to allow blood to flow from the RA to the LA.
- <b style="color:red;">TVA Type I</b> involves normally related great arteries, while <b style="color:red;">TVA Type II</b> involves Transposition of the Great Arteries (TGA).
- The <b style="color:red;">Bidirectional Glenn Shunt</b> involves anastomosing the Superior Vena Cava (SVC) to the pulmonary arteries at 3-6 months of age.
- The <b style="color:red;">Modified Fontan Procedure</b> involves anastomosing the Inferior Vena Cava (IVC) to the pulmonary arteries at 3 years of age.
- <b style="color:red;">Rashkind atrial balloon septostomy (BAS)</b> is used to enlarge a small ASD to improve mixing in TVA.

### Ebstein Anomaly
- <b style="color:red;">Ebstein Anomaly</b> involves the downward displacement of the tricuspid valve, leading to a small, functional right ventricle and a massive right atrium.
- <b style="color:red;">Functional Pulmonary Atresia</b> occurs in Ebstein Anomaly because the RV is too small to pump blood effectively to the lungs.
- Cardiac screening is essential for <b style="color:red;">Ebstein Anomaly</b> because RA enlargement can stretch the SA node, causing life-threatening arrhythmias like <b style="color:red;">SVT</b>.

<h2 style="color:red;">II. CYANOTIC HEART DISEASES WITH INCREASED PULMONARY BLOOD FLOW</h2>

<table>
  <thead>
    <tr style="background-color:#f2f2f2;">
      <th>Disease</th>
      <th>Key Pathophysiology</th>
      <th>Characteristic CXR Sign</th>
    </tr>
  </thead>
  <tbody>
    <tr>
      <td><b>D-TGA</b></td>
      <td>Aorta from RV; Purmonay Artery from LV (Parallel circuits).</td>
      <td><b style="color:red;">Egg-on-a-string</b> (Narrow mediastinum).</td>
    </tr>
    <tr>
      <td><b>TAPVR</b></td>
      <td>No direct PV connection to LA; all blood returns to right heart.</td>
      <td><b style="color:red;">Snowman sign</b> (Figure-of-8).</td>
    </tr>
    <tr>
      <td><b>Truncus Arteriosus</b></td>
      <td>Single arterial trunk supplying systemic, pulmonary, and coronary circuits.</td>
      <td>Increased PBF; <b style="color:red;">Right-sided aorta</b> in 50%.</td>
    </tr>
  </tbody>
</table>

### D-Transposition of the Great Arteries (D-TGA)
- <b style="color:red;">D-TGA</b> is common in infants of mothers with <b style="color:red;">Insulin-Dependent Diabetes Mellitus (IDDM)</b>.
- In <b style="color:red;">D-TGA</b>, the aorta arises from the RV and the pulmonary artery from the LV, creating independent parallel circulations. 
- Clinical signs of <b style="color:red;">D-TGA</b> include severe cyanosis in the first hours of life, tachypnea, and a single, loud S2; murmurs are often absent.
- The <b style="color:red;">Arterial Switch Operation (Jatene)</b> is the definitive surgical treatment, typically performed within the first 2 weeks of life.

### Total Anomalous Pulmonary Venous Return (TAPVR)
- <b style="color:red;">TAPVR</b> is characterized by pulmonary veins draining into systemic venous channels instead of the Left Atrium.
- The <b style="color:red;">Supracardiac type</b> is the most common form (50%) of TAPVR, where pulmonary veins drain into the SVC.
- <b style="color:red;">Snowman sign</b> (or Figure-of-8) on CXR is associated with supracardiac TAPVR due to an enlarged SVC and persistent left vertical vein.

### Truncus Arteriosus
- <b style="color:red;">Truncus Arteriosus</b> features a single arterial trunk arising from the heart, always associated with a VSD.
- Infants with <b style="color:red;">Truncus Arteriosus</b> develop heart failure by 1-2 months of age, presenting with bounding pulses and wide pulse pressure.

<h2 style="color:red;">III. DISEASES OF THE MYOCARDIUM (CARDIOMYOPATHIES)</h2>

<table>
  <thead>
    <tr style="background-color:#f2f2f2;">
      <th>Type</th>
      <th>Mechanism</th>
      <th>Key Clinical/Differentiating Fact</th>
    </tr>
  </thead>
  <tbody>
    <tr>
      <td><b>Dilated (DCM)</b></td>
      <td>Ventricular dilatation and <b style="color:red;">systolic</b> dysfunction.</td>
      <td><b style="color:red;">Most common</b> form in children.</td>
    </tr>
    <tr>
      <td><b>Hypertrophic (HCM)</b></td>
      <td>Thickened LV wall; <b style="color:red;">obstructive</b> physiology.</td>
      <td><b style="color:red;">Most common cause of sudden death</b> in young athletes.</td>
    </tr>
    <tr>
      <td><b>Restrictive (RCM)</b></td>
      <td>Impaired <b style="color:red;">diastolic</b> filling; rigid walls.</td>
      <td>Least common; shows <b style="color:red;">Square root sign</b> on cardiac cath.</td>
    </tr>
  </tbody>
</table>

### Dilated Cardiomyopathy (DCM)
- <b style="color:red;">Dilated Cardiomyopathy (DCM)</b> is the most common pediatric cardiomyopathy, often idiopathic or following viral myocarditis. 
- Pathogenesis of <b style="color:red;">Dilated Cardiomyopathy (DCM)</b> involve myocyte loss (apoptosis) and extracellular matrix remodeling.
- <b style="color:red;">ACE inhibitors and Beta-blockers</b> are used in DCM to retard or reverse maladaptive ventricular remodeling.
- Physical findings in <b style="color:red;">Dilated Cardiomyopathy (DCM)</b> include a displaced apical impulse, S3/S4 gallop, and a murmur of mitral regurgitation at the apex.

### Hypertrophic Cardiomyopathy (HCM)
- <b style="color:red;">Hypertrophic Cardiomyopathy (HCM)</b> is often caused by mutations in <b style="color:red;">cardiac β-myosin heavy-chain (MYH7)</b> or myosin-binding protein C.
- Histopathology of <b style="color:red;">Hypertrophic Cardiomyopathy (HCM)</b> shows myocytes arranged in a <b style="color:red;">chaotic, disorganized fashion</b>.
- Patients with <b style="color:red;">Hypertrophic Cardiomyopathy (HCM)</b> may have a double/triple apical impulse and a systolic ejection murmur that increases with LV outflow obstruction.
- Competitive sports are <b style="color:red;">prohibited</b> for patients diagnosed with HCM due to the risk of ventricular fibrillation and sudden death.

### Restrictive Cardiomyopathy (RCM)
- <b style="color:red;">Restrictive Cardiomyopathy (RCM)</b> is characterized by normal ventricular size but <b style="color:red;">massive biatrial enlargement</b>.
- Cardiac catheterization in <b style="color:red;">Restrictive Cardiomyopathy (RCM)</b> reveals an early diastolic dip and plateau pattern known as the <b style="color:red;">Square root sign</b>.

### Myocarditis
- <b style="color:red;">Viral infection</b> is the most common cause of myocarditis (e.g., COVID-19, Dengue).
- <b style="color:red;">Milrinone</b> is an inotrope used in myocarditis that also helps decrease pulmonary artery pressure, but it should not be given if the patient is hypotensive.
- <b style="color:red;">Endomyocardial biopsy</b> is the diagnostic gold standard for myocarditis, showing inflammatory infiltrates and myocyte necrosis.

<h2 style="color:red;">IV. DISEASES OF THE PERICARDIUM</h2>

### Acute Pericarditis and Tamponade
- <b style="color:red;">Acute Pericarditis</b> presents with sharp chest pain that is relieved by sitting upright or leaning forward.
- <b style="color:red;">Pulsus paradoxus</b> is an excessive fall of systolic blood pressure (&gt;10 mm Hg) during inspiration, indicating cardiac tamponade.
- <b style="color:red;">Beck’s Triad</b> for cardiac tamponade consists of: 1) Hypotension, 2) Jugular Venous Distension (Kussmaul's sign), and 3) Distant/Muffled heart sounds.
- CXR findings for massive pericardial effusion include the <b style="color:red;">Water bottle sign</b> or Erlenmeyer flask appearance.

### Constrictive Pericarditis
- <b style="color:red;">Constrictive Pericarditis</b> involves fibrosis and calcification of the pericardium, leading to impaired diastolic filling.
- A <b style="color:red;">Pericardial knock</b> is a characteristic clinical finding in constrictive pericarditis.
- <b style="color:red;">Pericardiectomy</b> (surgical removal of the pericardium) is the definitive treatment for constrictive pericarditis.

<h2 style="color:red;">V. KEY DIFFERENTIATORS AND COMPARISONS</h2>

- <b style="color:red;">Boot-shaped heart</b> (TOF) vs. <b style="color:red;">Egg-on-a-string</b> (TGA) vs. <b style="color:red;">Snowman sign</b> (TAPVR) vs. <b style="color:red;">Box-shaped heart</b> (Ebstein).
- <b style="color:red;">Tetralogy of Fallot</b> vs. <b style="color:red;">Tricuspid Atresia</b> ECG: TOF has Right Axis Deviation/RVH; TVA has <b style="color:red;">Left Axis Deviation/LVH</b>.
- <b style="color:red;">Tet spell</b> disappearance of murmur: Due to a sudden increase in RVOT obstruction, reducing the flow across the pulmonary valve.
- <b style="color:red;">Squatting in TOF</b> vs. <b style="color:red;">Knee-chest position</b>: Both aim to increase <b style="color:red;">Systemic Vascular Resistance (SVR)</b> to force blood through the lungs.
- <b style="color:red;">Dilated Cardiomyopathy</b> vs. <b style="color:red;">Hypertrophic Cardiomyopathy</b> morphology: DCM has thin, weak walls with chamber dilation; HCM has thick, nondilated walls with narrow outflow tracts.
- <b style="color:red;">Restrictive Cardiomyopathy</b> vs. <b style="color:red;">Constrictive Pericarditis</b>: Both present with diastolic failure and similar symptoms; RCM is a muscle problem, whereas CP is a pericardial scarring problem treatable by <b style="color:red;">pericardiectomy</b>.
- <b style="color:red;">PDA-dependent Pulmonary Flow</b> (e.g., TOF with Atresia, TVA Type Ia) requires <b style="color:red;">Prostaglandin E1</b> to maintain patency.
- <b style="color:red;">Blalock-Taussig Shunt (BTS)</b> vs. <b style="color:red;">Glenn Shunt</b>: BTS connects the subclavian artery to the PA; Glenn connects the <b style="color:red;">Superior Vena Cava</b> to the PA.
- <b style="color:red;">S3 gallop</b> is common in <b style="color:red;">Dilated Cardiomyopathy</b> (systolic failure), whereas a <b style="color:red;">Pericardial knock</b> is unique to <b style="color:red;">Constrictive Pericarditis</b>.
- <b style="color:red;">IDDM association</b>: TGA is the classic heart defect associated with infants of diabetic mothers.
- <b style="color:red;">DiGeorge Syndrome association</b>: Frequently associated with <b style="color:red;">TOF</b>, <b style="color:red;">TGA</b>, and <b style="color:red;">Truncus Arteriosus</b>.
- <b style="color:red;">Kussmaul’s sign</b> (JVD on inspiration) is a hallmark of <b style="color:red;">Cardiac Tamponade</b> and <b style="color:red;">Constrictive Pericarditis</b>.
- <b style="color:red;">Square root sign</b> (diastolic dip and plateau) on pressure tracings is pathognomonic for <b style="color:red;">Restrictive Cardiomyopathy</b>.
- <b style="color:red;">Rashkind BAS</b> is a catheter-based palliative procedure, while <b style="color:red;">Jatene</b> is a definitive open-heart surgery.

## QA

text
## CYANOTIC HEART DISEASES WITH DECREASED PULMONARY BLOOD FLOW

1. What is the Anatomical Hallmark: <font color="red">Tetralogy of Fallot (TOF)</font>? | RVOT obstruction, VSD, Overriding Aorta, RVH.
2. What is the Anatomical Hallmark: <font color="red">Tricuspid Valve Atresia (TVA)</font>? | Agenesis of Tricuspid valve.
3. What is the connection status in <font color="red">Tricuspid Valve Atresia (TVA)</font>? | No RA to RV connection.
4. What is the Anatomical Hallmark: <font color="red">Ebstein Anomaly</font>? | Downward displacement of TV.
5. What is the status of the RV in <font color="red">Ebstein Anomaly</font>? | "Atrialized" RV.
6. What is the Classic CXR finding: <font color="red">Tetralogy of Fallot (TOF)</font>? | <font color="red">Coeur de sabot</font> (Boot-shaped heart).
7. What is the Classic CXR finding: <font color="red">Tricuspid Valve Atresia (TVA)</font>? | Variable; left-sided enlargement.
8. What is the Classic CXR finding: <font color="red">Ebstein Anomaly</font>? | <font color="red">Box-shaped</font> massive cardiomegaly.
9. What are the ECG findings: <font color="red">Tetralogy of Fallot (TOF)</font>? | RVH and Right Atrial Enlargement.
10. What are the ECG findings: <font color="red">Tricuspid Valve Atresia (TVA)</font>? | <font color="red">Left Axis Deviation (LAD)</font> and LVH.
11. What are the ECG findings: <font color="red">Ebstein Anomaly</font>? | RBBB and <font color="red">Wolff-Parkinson-White (WPW)</font>.
12. What is the Cardiac Murmur: <font color="red">Tetralogy of Fallot (TOF)</font>? | Systolic ejection murmur (LSB).
13. Where does the <font color="red">Tetralogy of Fallot</font> murmur radiate? | To the back.
14. What is the Cardiac Murmur: <font color="red">Tricuspid Valve Atresia (TVA)</font>? | Holosystolic murmur at LSB.
15. What is the Cardiac Murmur: <font color="red">Ebstein Anomaly</font>? | Holosystolic murmur (TR) and <font color="red">Gallop rhythm</font>.
16. What determines severity in <font color="red">Tetralogy of Fallot (TOF)</font>? | Degree of RVOT stenosis.
17. What structures are required for survival in <font color="red">Tricuspid Valve Atresia (TVA)</font>? (3) | <font color="red">ASD</font>, <font color="red">VSD</font>, and <font color="red">PDA</font>.
18. What structure is required for survival in <font color="red">Ebstein Anomaly</font>? | <font color="red">ASD</font>.

## TETRALOGY OF FALLOT (TOF)

19. Components (4): <font color="red">Tetralogy of Fallot (TOF)</font>? | RVOT stenosis, VSD, Overriding Aorta, RVH.
20. What does the severity of <font color="red">TOF</font> NOT depend on? | VSD size.
21. What defines a <font color="red">"Pink TOF"</font>? | Mild RVOT stenosis.
22. Shunt direction: <font color="red">"Pink TOF"</font>? | Left-to-Right shunt.
23. Why do <font color="red">Tetralogy of Fallot</font> patients assume a <font color="red">squatting position</font>? | Increase Systemic Vascular Resistance (SVR).
24. Benefit of squatting in <font color="red">TOF</font>? | Reduces right-to-left shunting.
25. Characteristics (4): <font color="red">Paroxysmal Hypercyanotic attack (Tet spell)</font>? | Hyperpnea, restlessness, cyanosis, murmur disappearance.
26. What happens to the murmur during a <font color="red">Tet spell</font>? | Temporary disappearance.
27. Result of chronic hypoxemia: <font color="red">Tetralogy of Fallot</font>? (2) | <font color="red">Polycythemia</font> and finger clubbing.
28. CXR features (2): <font color="red">Tetralogy of Fallot</font>? | Concave Main Pulmonary Artery, uplifted apex.
29. Associated anomaly: <font color="red">Di George Syndrome (CATCH 22)</font>? | <font color="red">Tetralogy of Fallot (TOF)</font>.
30. Genetic defect: <font color="red">Di George Syndrome</font>? | Chromosome 22q11 deletion.
31. Management (5): Acute <font color="red">Tet spell</font>? | Knee-chest, oxygen, morphine, bicarbonate, Propranolol.
32. Drug used for acute <font color="red">Tet spell</font> management? | <font color="red">Propranolol</font>.
33. Purpose: <font color="red">Blalock-Taussig Shunt (BTS)</font>? | Stimulate pulmonary artery growth.
34. Type of procedure: <font color="red">Blalock-Taussig Shunt (BTS)</font>? | Temporary surgical procedure (artificial PDA).

## TRICUSPID VALVE ATRESIA (TVA)

35. Identifying ECG: <font color="red">Tricuspid Valve Atresia (TVA)</font>? | <font color="red">LAD</font> and LVH.
36. Required flow for <font color="red">TVA</font> survival? | RA to LA (via ASD).
37. Anatomical state: <font color="red">TVA Type I</font>? | Normally related great arteries.
38. Anatomical state: <font color="red">TVA Type II</font>? | Transposition of Great Arteries (TGA).
39. Procedure: <font color="red">Bidirectional Glenn Shunt</font>? | SVC to pulmonary arteries.
40. Age for <font color="red">Bidirectional Glenn Shunt</font>? | 3-6 months.
41. Procedure: <font color="red">Modified Fontan Procedure</font>? | IVC to pulmonary arteries.
42. Age for <font color="red">Modified Fontan Procedure</font>? | 3 years.
43. Procedure to enlarge small ASD in <font color="red">TVA</font>? | <font color="red">Rashkind atrial balloon septostomy (BAS)</font>.

## EBSTEIN ANOMALY

44. Pathogenesis: <font color="red">Ebstein Anomaly</font>? | Downward displacement of tricuspid valve.
45. Heart chamber sizes: <font color="red">Ebstein Anomaly</font>? | Small RV; massive RA.
46. Why does <font color="red">Functional Pulmonary Atresia</font> occur in <font color="red">Ebstein</font>? | RV too small to pump.
47. Risk of stretched SA node in <font color="red">Ebstein Anomaly</font>? | Life-threatening arrhythmias (<font color="red">SVT</font>).

## CYANOTIC HEART DISEASES WITH INCREASED PULMONARY BLOOD FLOW

48. Pathophysiology: <font color="red">D-TGA</font>? | Aorta from RV; artery from LV.
49. Circulation type: <font color="red">D-TGA</font>? | Parallel circuits.
50. Characteristic CXR: <font color="red">D-TGA</font>? | <font color="red">Egg-on-a-string</font>.
51. Characteristic CXR: <font color="red">TAPVR</font>? | <font color="red">Snowman sign</font> (Figure-of-8).
52. Pathophysiology: <font color="red">TAPVR</font>? | Pulmonary veins drain to right heart.
53. Characteristic CXR: <font color="red">Truncus Arteriosus</font>? | Right-sided aorta (50%).
54. Pathophysiology: <font color="red">Truncus Arteriosus</font>? | Single arterial trunk.

## D-TRANSPOSITION OF THE GREAT ARTERIES (D-TGA)

55. Maternal association: <font color="red">D-TGA</font>? | <font color="red">Insulin-Dependent Diabetes Mellitus (IDDM)</font>.
56. Source of Aorta in <font color="red">D-TGA</font>? | Right Ventricle (RV).
57. Source of Pulmonary Artery in <font color="red">D-TGA</font>? | Left Ventricle (LV).
58. Clinical signs: <font color="red">D-TGA</font>? (3) | Severe cyanosis, tachypnea, single S2.
59. Definitive treatment: <font color="red">D-TGA</font>? | <font color="red">Arterial Switch Operation (Jatene)</font>.
60. Timing for <font color="red">Jatene</font> operation? | First 2 weeks of life.

## TOTAL ANOMALOUS PULMONARY VENOUS RETURN (TAPVR)

61. Where do veins drain in <font color="red">TAPVR</font>? | Systemic venous channels.
62. Most common form: <font color="red">TAPVR</font>? | <font color="red">Supracardiac type</font> (50%).
63. Where do veins drain: <font color="red">Supracardiac TAPVR</font>? | Superior Vena Cava (SVC).
64. Cause of <font color="red">Snowman sign</font> on CXR? | Enlarged SVC; left vertical vein.

## TRUNCUS ARTERIOSUS

65. Essential cardiac defect: <font color="red">Truncus Arteriosus</font>? | VSD.
66. Age of heart failure: <font color="red">Truncus Arteriosus</font>? | 1-2 months.
67. Pulse characteristic: <font color="red">Truncus Arteriosus</font>? | Bounding pulses; wide pulse pressure.

## DISEASES OF THE MYOCARDIUM (CARDIOMYOPATHIES)

68. Mechanism: <font color="red">Dilated (DCM)</font>? | Ventricular dilatation; <font color="red">systolic</font> dysfunction.
69. Most common form of cardiomyopathy in children? | <font color="red">Dilated (DCM)</font>.
70. Mechanism: <font color="red">Hypertrophic (HCM)</font>? | Thickened LV wall; <font color="red">obstructive</font> physiology.
71. Leading cause of sudden death in young athletes? | <font color="red">Hypertrophic (HCM)</font>.
72. Mechanism: <font color="red">Restrictive (RCM)</font>? | Impaired <font color="red">diastolic</font> filling; rigid walls.
73. Cardiac cath sign: <font color="red">Restrictive (RCM)</font>? | <font color="red">Square root sign</font>.

## DILATED CARDIOMYOPATHY (DCM)

74. Common triggers: <font color="red">Dilated Cardiomyopathy (DCM)</font>? | Idiopathic or viral myocarditis.
75. Pathogenesis: <font color="red">DCM</font>? (2) | Myocyte loss (apoptosis); matrix remodeling.
76. Drugs used to retard remodeling in <font color="red">DCM</font>? (2) | <font color="red">ACE inhibitors</font> and <font color="red">Beta-blockers</font>.
77. S3/S4 gallop is characteristic of? | <font color="red">Dilated Cardiomyopathy (DCM)</font>.
78. Murmur found at the apex in <font color="red">DCM</font>? | Mitral regurgitation.

## HYPERTROPHIC CARDIOMYOPATHY (HCM)

79. Gene mutation: <font color="red">Hypertrophic Cardiomyopathy (HCM)</font>? | <font color="red">Cardiac β-myosin heavy-chain (MYH7)</font>.
80. Histopathology: <font color="red">Hypertrophic Cardiomyopathy (HCM)</font>? | Myocytes in <font color="red">chaotic, disorganized fashion</font>.
81. Apical finding: <font color="red">Hypertrophic Cardiomyopathy (HCM)</font>? | Double/triple apical impulse.
82. What maneuver increases <font color="red">HCM</font> murmur? | LV outflow obstruction.
83. Activity status: Patients diagnosed with <font color="red">HCM</font>? | Competitive sports <font color="red">prohibited</font>.
84. Risk in <font color="red">HCM</font> during sports? | Ventricular fibrillation; sudden death.

## RESTRICTIVE CARDIOMYOPATHY (RCM)

85. Morphological features: <font color="red">Restrictive Cardiomyopathy (RCM)</font>? | Normal ventricular size; <font color="red">massive biatrial enlargement</font>.
86. Hemodynamic pattern: <font color="red">Restrictive Cardiomyopathy (RCM)</font>? | Early diastolic dip and plateau.

## MYOCARDITIS

87. Most common cause of <font color="red">Myocarditis</font>? | <font color="red">Viral infection</font> (COVID-19, Dengue).
88. Inotrope used in <font color="red">myocarditis</font>? | <font color="red">Milrinone</font>.
89. Contraindication for <font color="red">Milrinone</font>? | Hypotension.
90. Diagnostic Gold Standard: <font color="red">Myocarditis</font>? | <font color="red">Endomyocardial biopsy</font>.
91. Biopsy findings: <font color="red">Myocarditis</font>? | Inflammatory infiltrates; myocyte necrosis.

## DISEASES OF THE PERICARDIUM

92. Chest pain relief: <font color="red">Acute Pericarditis</font>? | Sitting upright/leaning forward.
93. Define <font color="red">Pulsus paradoxus</font>? | Fall of SBP >10 mm Hg during inspiration.
94. Condition associated with <font color="red">Pulsus paradoxus</font>? | Cardiac tamponade.
95. Components: <font color="red">Beck’s Triad</font>? (3) | Hypotension, JVD, distant heart sounds.
96. Definition of <font color="red">Kussmaul's sign</font>? | Jugular Venous Distension (JVD) on inspiration.
97. CXR signs: massive pericardial effusion? (2) | <font color="red">Water bottle sign</font>; Erlenmeyer flask.
98. Pathophysiology: <font color="red">Constrictive Pericarditis</font>? | Fibrosis and calcification of pericardium.
99. Clinical hallmark: <font color="red">Constrictive Pericarditis</font>? | <font color="red">Pericardial knock</font>.
100. Definitive treatment: <font color="red">Constrictive Pericarditis</font>? | <font color="red">Pericardiectomy</font>.

## KEY DIFFERENTIATORS AND COMPARISONS

101. CXR sign: <font color="red">TOF</font>? | <font color="red">Boot-shaped heart</font>.
102. CXR sign: <font color="red">TGA</font>? | <font color="red">Egg-on-a-string</font>.
103. CXR sign: <font color="red">TAPVR</font>? | <font color="red">Snowman sign</font>.
104. CXR sign: <font color="red">Ebstein</font>? | <font color="red">Box-shaped heart</font>.
105. Compare ECG: <font color="red">TOF</font> vs <font color="red">TVA</font>? | TOF: RVH/RAD; <br>TVA: LVH/LAD.
106. Why does the murmur disappear in a <font color="red">Tet spell</font>? | Sudden increase in RVOT obstruction.
107. Hemodynamic goal of <font color="red">Squatting</font> and <font color="red">Knee-chest</font>? | Increase <font color="red">SVR</font>.
108. Wall morphology: <font color="red">DCM</font> vs <font color="red">HCM</font>? | DCM: thin, weak walls. <br>HCM: thick, nondilated walls.
109. Anatomical problem: <font color="red">RCM</font> vs <font color="red">Constrictive Pericarditis</font>? | RCM: muscle problem. <br>CP: pericardial scarring.
110. Drug used for <font color="red">PDA-dependent</font> Pulmonary Flow? | <font color="red">Prostaglandin E1</font>.
111. Connection: <font color="red">Blalock-Taussig Shunt (BTS)</font>? | Subclavian artery to pulmonary artery (PA).
112. Connection: <font color="red">Glenn Shunt</font>? | <font color="red">SVC</font> to pulmonary artery (PA).
113. Identifying heart sound: <font color="red">Dilated Cardiomyopathy</font>? | <font color="red">S3 gallop</font> (systolic failure).
114. Heart defect triad: <font color="red">DiGeorge Syndrome</font>? | <font color="red">TOF</font>, <font color="red">TGA</font>, and <font color="red">Truncus Arteriosus</font>.
115. Hallmark: <font color="red">Cardiac Tamponade</font> and <font color="red">Constrictive Pericarditis</font>? | <font color="red">Kussmaul’s sign</font>.
116. Pathognomonic pressure tracing sign: <font color="red">Restrictive Cardiomyopathy</font>? | <font color="red">Square root sign</font>.
117. Palliative catheter procedure for mixing? | <font color="red">Rashkind BAS</font>.
118. Definitive open-heart surgery for TGA? | <font color="red">Jatene</font> (Arterial switch).
119. Effect of squatting on right-to-left shunting? | Reduces it.
120. Most common pediatric cardiomyopathy? | <font color="red">Dilated Cardiomyopathy</font>.
